Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
12 min
Enforcement of a Judge's Ruling by Another Judge
Explains when a judge must enforce another judge's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on enforcement of a prior judge's ruling and identify the exceptions that prevent enforcement.

Core Idea
A judge must enforce another's ruling unless it contradicts a definitive religious text or clear consensus.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on when a judge is obliged to enforce the ruling of another judge. The general rule is obligation, but three exceptions are recognized: contradiction with the Qur'an, the well-known Sunnah, or consensus. Understanding the nuance of these exceptions is critical for the proper functioning of the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn states: 'It is obligatory upon him to enforce it unless it contradicts...' Find this statement and note the default obligation.
The term 'yajibu' indicates wujub.
2
List the three exceptions
The exceptions are: the Book (Qur'an), the well-known Sunnah (al-sunnah al-mashhurah), and consensus (ijma). Note the qualification 'well-known' for Sunnah.
Well-known is distinguished from rare (gharib) narrations.
3
Understand the qualifying principle
The commentator clarifies that 'Book and Sunnah' here refer to texts that are qat'i al-dalala (definitive in meaning). Contradicting a non-definitive text does not prevent enforcement.
Contradicting a definitive text is kufr, so that is outside the scope.
4
Analyze the rational proof
The commentary provides a proof: no ijtihad is superior, the first ruling has been executed, and rejecting it leads to infinite regress. Identify this reasoning.
This is a qiyas argument based on necessity.
5
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The tasmiyah example: a ruling permitting meat without tasmiyah is enforceable according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Abu Yusuf. Examine the dalil and the reason for disagreement.
The disagreement hinges on whether the Quranic verse is qat'i al-dalala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي إذا قضى القاضي ورَفَعَ حُكْمَهُ إلى قاض آخرَ يَجِبُ عليه إمضاؤه إلا أن يكون مخالفاً للكتاب أو السنة المشهورة أو الإجماع.
قوله : أو السنة المشهورة ; قيَّد بالمشهورة احترازاً عن الغريب. فإن قلت : خرج به المتواترة أيضاً مع أن ما خالفها لا يمضي أيضاً. قلت : لما ذكر أنَّ ما خالف السنة المشهورة لا يمضي مع كونها أدنى درجة من المتواترة، ففي المتواترة بالطريق الأولى، والمراد من الكتاب والسنة ما لا يكون قطعي الدلالة ، فإنّ مخالفة قطعي الدلالة كفر، صرح به الفقهاء.
قوله : أو الإجماع ; والمراد منه ما ليس فيه خلاف يستند إلى دليل شرعي. كذا في «البحر».
قوله : يجب عليه إمضاؤه ; لأنه لا مزية لأحد الاجتهادين على الآخر، وقد ترجح الأول باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما هو دونه ; ولأنه لو لم ينفذ الأول لما نفذ الثاني أيضاً، وكذا الثالث والرابع إلى ما لا يتناهى الاحتمال أن يجيء قاض يرمي خلاف ذلك ، فكان نافذاً ضرورة. وقد صح أنَّ عمر له لما كثر اشتغاله قلّد القضاء أبا الدرداء ، واختصم إليه رجلان، فقضى لأحدهما ثم رأى المقضي عليه عمر فقال : لو كنت أنا مكانه لقضيت لك ، فقال المقضي عليه : ما يمنعك من القضاء ، قال : ليس هنا نص والرأي مشتركة، وروي عن عمر الله أنه قضى في حادثة بقضية ثم قضى فيها بخلاف ذلك، فقيل له في ذلك ، فقال : تلك كما قضينا، وهذا كما نقضي. ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز».
مخالفاً للكتاب كمتروك التسمية عامداً ، فإِنَّهُ مخالف لقوله : ﴿ وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ ﴾
أو السنة المشهورة: كالقضاء بحِلُّ المطلقة ثلاثاً بنكاح الزوج الثاني بلا وطء على مذهب سعيد بن المسيب
قوله : کمتروك التسمية ; وصورته : أن يعلم أنَّ التسمية شرط، وتركها مع ذكرها ، أما إذا تركها غير عالم باشتراطها ، فهو في حكم الناسي. كما صرحوا به. وقيد العمد ; لإخرج صورة النسيان، فإن من تركها ناسيا فذبيحته تؤكل، وقال الشافعي له : ذبيحة تارك التسمية في الوجهين تؤكل، وتفصيل الدلائل سيأتيك إن شاء الله حالة في «كتاب الذبائح».
قوله : فإنّه مخالف لقوله تعالى ... الخ، بناءً على أن الواو في قوله : وَإِنَّهُ لَفِسْقٌ للعطف، والضمير راجع إلى مصدر الفعل الذي دخل عليه حرفُ النهي أو إلى الموصول، واحتمال كونها حالية، فتكون قيدا للنهي. رد بأن التأكيد بأن واللام ينفيه ; لأنَّ الحال في النهي مبناه على التقدير; كأنه قيل : لا تأكلوا إن كان فاسقاً فلا يصلح، وإنه لفسق، بل هو فسق، ولو سلم فلا تسلم، أنه قيد للنهي ، بل هو إشارة في المعنى الموجب له فلا تُهن زيداً وهو أخوك ، ولا تشرب الخمر وهو حرام عليك. كذا في «المحيط» نقلا عن «النوازل». إذا قضى بحل متروك التسمية عامداً ينفذ عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لأبي يوسف ذكره البرْجَندِي ; ولذا قال القُهُسْتانِيُّ : والأحسن أن يُمثل بالقضاء بتقديم الوارث على المديون، فإن الأوَّلَ نافذ عن الطرفين. كذا في «المغني»، وغيره. انتهى.
قوله : على مذهب سعيد بن المسيب ; - بفتح الياء المشددة – ، وقيل : ...
That is, when a judge passes a judgment and his judgment is raised to another judge, it is obligatory upon him to enforce it unless it contradicts the Book, the well-known Sunnah, or consensus… [commentary follows]
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the rule of enforcing a judge's ruling by another judge. The matn states a general principle: a subsequent judge must enforce the ruling of a prior judge unless it contradicts the Qur'an, the well-known Sunnah (السنة المشهورة), or consensus (الإجماع). The commentator explains each exception. 'Well-known Sunnah' is qualified to exclude rare (gharib) narrations; even mutawatir Sunnah would take precedence by a fortiori reasoning. The term 'Book and Sunnah' refers to texts that are not definitive in meaning (qat'i al-dalala), since contradicting a definitive text is kufr. 'Consensus' means that which has no valid legal disagreement. The commentator then provides a rational proof for enforcement: there is no superiority of one ijtihad over another, and the first ruling has been connected to the judiciary; if it were not enforced, then a second judge could also be reversed, leading to infinite regress. He also cites the historical practice of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab. Then come examples: a ruling permitting eating meat from an animal on which the name of Allah was intentionally omitted contradicts the Qur'an (6:121). A ruling permitting a thrice-divorced woman to be halal for her first husband via a second marriage without intercourse contradicts the well-known Sunnah. The commentary further discusses the tasmiyah example: if the judge ruled that such meat is permissible, Abu Hanifa says the ruling is enforceable, while Abu Yusuf disagrees. The preferred view, mentioned by al-Qahistani, is to give a different example, such as prioritizing an heir over a creditor, where the ruling is enforceable.

Key Terms
السنة المشهورة (al-sunnah al-mashhurah): The well-known Sunnah, which is a hadith that is widely transmitted but not necessarily mutawatir; it is distinguished from rare (gharib) narrations.
إمضاء الحكم (imda' al-hukm): Enforcement of a prior judge's ruling by a subsequent judge.
قطعي الدلالة (qat'i al-dalala): Definitive in meaning; a text that admits only one interpretation and cannot be subject to ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, under what condition must a judge NOT enforce the ruling of another judge?
A) If the ruling is based on a weak analogy.
B) If the ruling contradicts the Qur'an, well-known Sunnah, or consensus.
C) If the ruling was made by a judge of a different madhhab.
D) If the ruling is more than ten years old.

Key Takeaway
A judge must enforce another judge's ruling unless it contradicts a definitive religious text or clear consensus, preventing arbitrary overturning of judicial decisions.

Binding Effect of a Judge's Ruling in Ijtihad Matters
Rule that a judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad becomes binding and cannot be overturned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the binding effect of a judge's ruling in ijtihad matters and identify the condition of knowledge of ikhtilaf according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad is binding and cannot be overturned by another judge's ijtihad.

In this passage, we explore a key principle of Islamic judiciary: when a judge rules on a matter where scholars hold different opinions (mujtahad fih), that ruling becomes definitive and must be enforced by other judges. This lesson examines the ruling, its evidentiary basis from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the debated condition that the judge must be aware of the existing disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its effect
Understand that the passage states a judge's ruling in a mujtahad fih becomes binding, like consensus. Note that the ruling is effective whether it aligns with the judge's own school or not, except when it contradicts his school (to be discussed later).
The effect is that the original disagreement is removed by the judgment.
2
Examine the evidence from Imam Muhammad
The narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani is cited: 'any matter in which jurists differ and a judge rules, his judgment is valid and no other judge can nullify it.' This is the primary proof for the binding effect.
This narration is transmitted and considered authoritative within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the condition of knowledge
The passage discusses whether the judge must know that the matter is disputed for the ruling to be enforceable. Two positions are presented: earlier scholars require such knowledge, while later scholars dispense with it.
The commentator sides with the later view, especially for contemporary judges.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator explicitly says 'it is appropriate to not require knowledge of disagreement, especially in our time.' This becomes the operative position for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on practical necessity and the reality of modern judges' knowledge.
5
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider a judge who rules on a marriage validity issue without knowing that Shafi'is differ. According to the mufta bihi, the ruling is binding and cannot be overturned by another judge, even if the second judge follows a different ijtihad.
This ensures finality and prevents endless re-litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحاصل هذا أنَّ القاضي إذا قَضَى فِي مُجْتَهَدٍ فيه يصير مُجْمَعَاً عليه ، فيجب على قاض آخر تنفيذه، وهذا حُكْمٌ في وفق مذهبه ، أَمَّا إِذا حَكَمَ على خلافِ مذهبه فسيأتي. ويجب أن يعلم القاضي أن المسألة مختلف فيها، وأيضاً هذا إذا كان محل القضاء مختلفا فيه [١] قوله : يصير؛ أي المختلف فيه مجمعاً عليه ؛ لأنَّ الخلاف الموجود قبل القضاء يرتفع به كما يرتفع بإجماع العلماء على قول بعد اختلافهم على قولين في العصر الذي قبله. كذا في «كمال الدراية». [٢] قوله : فيجب...الخ ؛ سواءً كان على وفق رأيهِ أو على خلافه ؛ لأنَّ القضاء متى لاقى مجتهداً فيه ينفذ، ولا ينقض باجتهادٍ آخر ؛ لأنَّ اجتهاد الثاني كاجتهادِ الأول، وقد ترجح القولُ باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما دونَه. وروى عن محمد له أنّ كل شيء اختلف فيه الفقهاء فقضى فيه القاضي كان قضاؤه جائزاً، ولم يكن لقاض آخر أن يبطله ، قال الفقيه أبو الليث له : وبه نأخذ. كذا في «الفصول العمادية» وغيرها. (٣) قوله : ويجب أن يعلم ...الخ ؛ قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز : قالوا : شرطه أن يكون عالماً باختلاف العلماء حتى لو قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه، وهو لا يعلم ذلك لا يجوز قضاؤه عند عامتهم، ولا يمضيه الثاني. ذكره في «النهاية» معزياً إلى «المحيط»، وقال فيه شمس الأئمة الله : هذا هو ظاهر المذهب. انتهى. وقال في «المنح»: وفي «الفواكه البدرية» لابن الغرس ، قال : هل يشترط لنفوذ القضاء على المخالف علم القاضي بالخلاف في المحكوم به ، اختلفوا فيه ؛ وربَّحَ غيرُ واحد أنه ليس بشرط، فينفدُ على المخالف، عَلِمَ القاضي بالخلاف في المسألة أم لم يَعْلَم. انتهى. أقول : ينبغي عدم اشتراط العلم بالخلاف لا سيما في زماننا، فإنّ قضاةً زماننا لا معرفة لهم بمذاهبهم ، فضلاً عن علمهم بمذاهب بقية المجتهدين، والله أعلم.
So the gist of this is that when a judge gives a ruling in a matter of ijtihad, it becomes like consensus, so another judge is obliged to execute it. And this is a ruling in accordance with his school. As for when he rules contrary to his school, it will come. And the judge must know that the matter is disputed, and also this when the place of ruling is disputed. [1] His saying: 'becomes' i.e., the disputed becomes agreed upon, because the disagreement that existed before the ruling is removed by it, just as it is removed by the consensus of scholars on a statement after their disagreement on two opinions in the previous era. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. [2] His saying: 'so it is obligatory...' etc. Whether it is according to his opinion or contrary to it, because when a judgment meets a matter of ijtihad, it is executed and is not overturned by another ijtihad, for the ijtihad of the second is like the ijtihad of the first, and the statement is preponderant by the connection of the judgment to it, so it is not overturned by something lesser. And it is narrated from Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, that anything in which the jurists differed and the judge ruled upon it, his judgment is permissible, and no other judge can nullify it. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: 'We take this position.' Thus in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah and others. (3) His saying: 'And the judge must know...' etc. Al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: They said: Its condition is that he is knowledgeable of the disagreement of scholars, so that if he rules in a matter of ijtihad while not knowing that, his judgment is not permissible according to most of them, and the second judge does not enforce it. It was mentioned in al-Nihayah attributed to al-Muhit, and Shams al-A'imma said: 'This is the apparent position.' End. And it is said in al-Minah: In al-Fawakih al-Badriyyah by Ibn al-Ghars, he said: Is knowledge of the disagreement necessary for the enforceability of a judgment against someone who disagrees? They disagreed about it; many held that it is not a condition, and it is enforceable against the one who disagrees, whether the judge knew of the disagreement in the matter or not. End. I say: It is appropriate to not require knowledge of disagreement, especially in our time, for the judges of our time have no knowledge of their own schools, let alone knowledge of the schools of other mujtahids. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the legal principle that a judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad (where scholars have differing opinions) becomes binding and must be enforced by other judges. The commentator explains that the original disagreement is essentially 'consensus-ized' by the judgment. The ruling is valid whether it is consistent with the judge's own school or not, because the judgment itself gives preponderance. The proof is a narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that such rulings are final. However, there is a condition debated: must the judge know that the matter is one of ikhtilaf? The earlier authorities (like al-Zayla'i and Shams al-A'imma) require such knowledge; otherwise the judgment is invalid and not enforceable. But later commentators, especially in the later period, argue that this condition is impractical, especially since modern judges are not even well-versed in their own school. The commentator opines that requiring knowledge of ikhtilaf is not necessary, particularly in our time. This reflects the principle that the judgment is effective by the mere fact of it being an ijtihadi matter.

Key Terms
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fih): A legal matter that is subject to ijtihad, meaning there are valid differing opinions among scholars.
نفوذ القضاء (nufudh al-qada'): Enforceability of a judge's ruling; once issued, it must be executed by other judges.
إجماع بعد اختلاف (ijma' ba'd ikhtilaf): Consensus arrived at after a period of disagreement, which becomes binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani state regarding a judge's ruling in a matter where jurists differ?
A) The ruling is not valid unless approved by a mujtahid.
B) The ruling is permissible and no other judge can nullify it.
C) The ruling is only valid if the judge knows the ikhtilaf.
D) The ruling is subject to review by a higher court.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling in an ijtihad matter is binding and cannot be overturned by another judge, regardless of the judge's knowledge of the existing ikhtilaf, according to the later preferred Hanafi view.

Effect of a Judge's Ruling in Matters of Ijtihad: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Knowledge of Disagreement
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a judge must know the disagreement for his ruling to be enforceable, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad becomes binding, but the condition of the judge's knowledge of the disagreement is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya examines a key procedural issue in Hanafi judiciary: when a judge rules on a matter open to ijtihad, does his ruling become immediately enforceable against a dissenter? The school's apparent position requires the judge to know the disagreement, but later authorities give fatwa without that condition. We will dissect the textual arguments and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Find the statement that when a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad, it becomes binding and must be executed by another judge. Note that this applies even if the ruling contradicts the judge's own school.
This is the foundational rule from which the dispute arises.
2
Examine the narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani
Read the narration: 'Every matter the jurists disagreed about, if a judge gives a ruling on it, his ruling is valid, and no other judge may invalidate it.' This is a key proof for those who reject the knowledge condition.
The narration is used to argue that the ruling is effective regardless of the judge's awareness of disagreement.
3
Understand the condition of knowledge (zahir al-madhhab)
Al-Zayla'i states that the apparent madhhab requires the judge to know the disagreement. If he is unaware, his ruling is invalid and the second judge does not enforce it.
This is the opposing view within the Hanafi school.
4
Trace the refinement and mufta bihi position
Later scholars like Ibn al-Ghars and the author of al-Minh give preponderance to the view that knowledge is not a condition. A distinction is proposed: the judge must know the matter is one of ijtihad, but not the specific opinions. Ultimately, the fatwa is given without the condition, especially in later times.
The practical need and the ignorance of contemporary judges justify this relaxation.
5
Consider special cases: when the ruling itself is disputed or when ruling against the majority
If the very validity of such a ruling is disputed (e.g., ruling against an absent person), another judge's confirmation is needed to make it binding. If a judge rules against the overwhelming majority, his ruling may be overturned as contrary to effective consensus.
These exceptions show the limits of the general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء / الحبس وكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي [۱] ٤٦٩ [٢] فحاصل هذا أنَّ القاضي إذا قَضَى فِي مُجْتَهَدٍ فيه يصير" مُجْمَعَاً. عليه ، فيجب على قاض آخر تنفيذه، وهذا حُكْمٌ في وفق مذهبه ، أَمَّا إِذا حَكَمَ على خلافِ مذهبه فسيأتي. ويجب أن يعلم" القاضي أن المسألة مختلف فيها، وأيضاً هذا إذا كان محل القضاء مختلفا فيه [١] قوله : يصير؛ أي المختلف فيه مجمعاً عليه ؛ لأنَّ الخلاف الموجود قبل القضاء يرتفع به كما يرتفع بإجماع العلماء على قول بعد اختلافهم على قولين في العصر الذي قبله. كذا في «كمال الدراية». [٢] قوله : فيجب...الخ ؛ سواءً كان على وفق رأيهِ أو على خلافه ؛ لأنَّ القضاء متى لاقى مجتهداً فيه ينفذ، ولا ينقض باجتهادٍ آخر ؛ لأنَّ اجتهاد الثاني كاجتهادِ الأول، وقد ترجح القولُ باتصال القضاء به، فلا ينقض بما دونَه. وروى عن محمد له أنّ كل شيء اختلف فيه الفقهاء فقضى فيه القاضي كان قضاؤه جائزاً، ولم يكن لقاض آخر أن يبطله ، قال الفقيه أبو الليث له : وبه نأخذ. كذا في «الفصول العمادية» و غيرها. (1) (۳) قوله : ويجب أن يعلم ...الخ ؛ قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز : قالوا : شرطه أن يكون عالماً باختلاف العلماء حتى لو قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه، وهو لا يعلم ذلك لا يجوز قضاؤه عند عامتهم، ولا يمضيه الثاني. ذكره في «النهاية» معزياً إلى «المحيط»، وقال فيه شمس الأئمة الله : هذا هو ظاهر المذهب. انتهى. وقال في «المنح»(۲): وفي «الفواكه البدرية» لابن الغرس ، قال : هل يشترط لنفوذ القضاء على المخالف علم القاضي بالخلاف في المحكوم به ، اختلفوا فيه ؛ وربَّحَ غيرُ واحد أنه ليس بشرط، فينفدُ على المخالف، عَلِمَ القاضي بالخلاف في المسألة أم لم يَعْلَم. انتهى. أقول : ينبغي عدم اشتراط العلم بالخلاف لا سيما في زماننا، فإنّ قضاةً زماننا لا معرفة لهم بمذاهبهم ، فضلاً عن علمهم بمذاهب بقية المجتهدين، والله أعلم. (۱) تبيين الحقائق (٤ : ۱۸۹) (۲) «منح الغفار» (ق۲ : ۱۰۳/ب).  ٤٧٠ وفيما اجتمع عليه الجمهور لا يعتبر خلافُ البعض زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية أما إذا كان نفس القضاء مختلفاً فيه : كالقضاء على الغائب، فحينئذ يصيرُ مُجْمَعاً عليه، فبعد الإمضاء إن رَفَعَ إلى قاض آخرَ يَحِبُ عليه تَنْفِيدُه. وفيما اجتمع عليه الجمهور لا يعتبر خلافُ البعض) ثم رأيتُ في «الخلاصة ما يقوّي ما اخترتُه حيث قال : إن هذا الشرط : يعني كونه عالما بالاختلاف، وإن كان ظاهر المذهب، لكن يفتي بخلافه. انتهى. قال مولانا في «بحره» (۱) : والتحقيق المعتمدُ أنّ علمه بكون ما حكم به مجتهداً فيه شرطاً، وأما علمه بكون المسألة اجتهادية فلا ، قال : ويدلُّ عـلـيـه مـا في «الفتاوى الصغرى». انتهى. ولم يظهر الفرق بينهما حينئذ ، والله أعلم. انتهى ما في «المنح»). [۱] قوله : أما إذا كان نفس القضاء مختلفاً فيه ؛ بأن قال بعض العلماء : إنه نافذ، وبعضُهم : إنه غير نافذ ؛ بناءً على أنّ الحكم مختلف فيه أو غير مختلف ؛ كبيع المدبر ؛ فإنّه في الصدر الأوّل مختلف فيه. ثم المتأخرون أجمعوا على أنه لا يجوز، فقال علماؤنا : إنَّ الإجماع المتأخر رافع للخلاف المتقدم، وقال غيرهم : إنه غيرُ رافع، وكذا الحكم في كل حادثة اختلفوا في اختلافه. كذا في «شرح النُّقاية»(۳) للقُهستاني. [٢] قوله : فحينئذ يصير مجمعاً عليه ؛ بإمضاء قاض آخر ؛ لأنَّ محل الخلاف لم يوجد قبل القضاء، بل وجد بعده، فلا بُدَّ من قضاء آخر ؛ للترجيح. كذا في «كمال الدراية». (۱) «البحر الرائق ( : ) (۲) منح الغفار» (ق۲ : ١٠٣ / ب - ١٠٤ /أ). (۳) (جامع الرموز»(۲ : ۲۳۰).
Book of Judiciary / Imprisonment and the Book of the Judge to the Judge. [1] 469 [2] The conclusion is that when a judge gives a ruling in a matter of ijtihad, it becomes a matter of consensus. So it is obligatory upon another judge to execute it. This is a ruling in accordance with his own school. As for when he rules contrary to his school, it will come later. And the judge must know that the matter is disputed. Also, this is when the subject of the ruling itself is disputed. [1] His statement: 'becomes' means the matter of dispute becomes a matter of consensus; because the disagreement that existed before the ruling is lifted by it, just as it is lifted by the consensus of scholars on an opinion after their disagreement on two opinions in the previous era. As in 'Kamaal al-Diraya'. [2] His statement: 'so it is obligatory' etc., whether it is in accordance with his own view or contrary to it; because whenever a ruling meets a matter of ijtihad, it is enforced, and it is not overturned by another ijtihad because the ijtihad of the second is like the ijtihad of the first, and the opinion is strengthened by the connection of the ruling to it, so it is not overturned by something lesser. And it is narrated from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) that anything the jurists disagreed about, if a judge gave a ruling on it, his ruling is valid, and no other judge may invalidate it. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: and we take this. As in 'al-Fusool al-'Imadiyya' and others. (1) (3) His statement: 'and the judge must know' etc.; al-Zayla'i said in 'Sharh al-Kanz': They said: its condition is that he be knowledgeable of the disagreement of the scholars, so that if he gives a ruling in a matter of ijtihad without knowing that, his ruling is not permissible according to the generality of them, and the second judge does not enforce it. It is mentioned in 'al-Nihaya' attributing to 'al-Muhit', and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said therein: this is the apparent madhhab. End. And he said in 'al-Minh': and in 'al-Fawakih al-Badriyya' of Ibn al-Ghars, he said: Is knowledge of the judge of the disagreement in the matter ruled upon a condition for the enforcement of the ruling against the one who differs? They disagreed about it; and many gave preponderance that it is not a condition, so it is enforced against the dissenter, whether the judge knew of the disagreement in the matter or did not know. End. I say: It is appropriate not to stipulate knowledge of the disagreement, especially in our time, for the judges of our time have no knowledge of their own madhhabs, let alone knowledge of the madhhabs of the remaining mujtahids. And Allah knows best. (1) Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (4:189) (2) 'Minh al-Ghaffar' (folio 2:103/b). [PAGE_470] 470 And in what the majority have agreed upon, the disagreement of some is not considered. So after execution, if it is brought to another judge, he must enforce it. And in what the majority have agreed upon, the disagreement of some is not considered.) Then I saw in 'al-Khulasa' what strengthens what I chose, where he said: This condition, meaning his being knowledgeable of the disagreement, even if it is the apparent madhhab, but fatwa is given contrary to it. End. Our master said in his 'Bahr' (1): The verified and relied upon position is that his knowledge that what he ruled on is a matter of ijtihad is a condition, but his knowledge that the matter is one of ijtihad is not. He said: and this is indicated by what is in 'al-Fatawa al-Sughra'. End. And the difference between them is not apparent then. And Allah knows best. End of what is from 'al-Minh'). [1] His statement: 'As for when the ruling itself is disputed' meaning some scholars say it is valid, and some say it is not valid, based on whether the ruling is disputed or not; like the sale of a mudabbar (slave whose freedom is tied to the master's death); for it was disputed in the early period. Then the later scholars agreed that it is not permissible. Our scholars said: the later consensus is a raiser of the earlier disagreement, and others said: it is not a raiser. And similarly the ruling in every new case about which they differed regarding its dispute. As in 'Sharh al-Nuqaya' of al-Quhistani. [2] His statement: 'then it becomes a matter of consensus' by execution of another judge; because the subject of dispute did not exist before the ruling, but existed after it, so another ruling is necessary for preponderance. As in 'Kamaal al-Diraya'. [3] His statement: 'the disagreement of some is not considered'; accordingly, if a judge rules contrary to what the majority hold, his ruling is contrary to consensus, and the one who is brought to may overturn it. And it is appropriate to interpret the author's statement as referring to when the one dissenting is such that his ijtihad is not allowed, and everyone rejects his statement, like the statement of Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the permissibility of riba al-fadl, for it was not allowed for him, and it is reported that he retracted to their statement, and no one followed him and they rejected it. Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad and the conditions for its enforcement by another judge. The author begins by stating the general principle: when a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad (i.e., a matter open to scholarly disagreement), that ruling becomes binding (mujma' 'alayh) upon the parties and must be executed by another judge. This holds even if the ruling was against the judge's own school (as will be discussed later). The commentary then clarifies that the ruling lifts the pre-existing disagreement just as later consensus lifts earlier disagreement. A key point is that the second judge cannot overturn it with his own ijtihad because both ijtihads are equal, but the first ruling gains precedence through execution. The passage then discusses a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani that any matter jurists disagreed about, if a judge ruled on it, his ruling is valid and no other judge may invalidate it. This is the position adopted by Abu al-Layth.

The main dispute presented is whether the judge must know that the matter is one of ijtihad (i.e., that there is disagreement) for his ruling to be enforceable against a dissenter (a judge of another school). According to al-Zayla'i in Sharh al-Kanz, the apparent madhhab requires the judge to be knowledgeable of the disagreement; otherwise, his ruling is not valid and the second judge does not enforce it. However, Ibn al-Ghars in al-Fawakih al-Badriyya reports that many scholars gave preponderance to the view that such knowledge is not a condition, so the ruling is enforced whether the judge knew or not. The author of al-Minh (likely Ibn al-Ghars) himself opines that it is better not to stipulate such knowledge, especially in later times when judges lack even knowledge of their own madhhab. Then a distinction is proposed: the condition is that the judge knows the matter he ruled on is a matter of ijtihad (i.e., he knows it is a disputable matter), but he does not need to know the specific opinions. However, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq questions the practical difference between these two formulations.

The passage then turns to a special case: when the ruling itself is disputed (i.e., whether such a ruling is even valid), such as ruling against an absent person or the sale of a mudabbar. In such cases, the ruling does not immediately become binding; rather, it requires another judge to confirm and execute it, which then makes it consensus. Finally, the passage addresses the issue of majority versus minority opinions: the disagreement of a single scholar or a minority is not considered when the majority has agreed. If a judge rules contrary to the majority, his ruling is considered against consensus and may be overturned. This is especially true if the minority scholar's ijtihad is not considered valid (like Ibn 'Abbas's isolated view on riba al-fadl, which he later retracted).

Key Terms
مُجْتَهَد فيه (mujtahad fīh): A matter that is open to ijtihad, i.e., there is no definitive textual ruling and scholars may differ on the correct ruling.
مجمعاً عليه (mujma' 'alayh): A ruling that has become binding and agreed upon, in this context through a judge's ruling rather than scholarly consensus.
نَفَذَ (القضاء) (nafadha (al-qada)): The ruling is executed and becomes effective; it cannot be overturned by another judge.
نَقْضَ القضاء (naqd al-qada): Overturning or invalidating a previous judge's ruling by a subsequent judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani mentioned in the passage, what is the ruling on a judge's decision in a matter that jurists have disagreed about?
A) The ruling is valid and another judge may invalidate it if he disagrees.
B) The ruling is valid and no other judge may invalidate it.
C) The ruling is only valid if the judge knew of the disagreement.
D) The ruling is not valid unless approved by a higher court.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad is binding and must be enforced by another judge, and the mufta bihi view is that this holds regardless of the first judge's knowledge of the disagreement.

Qada — Judiciary
advanced
10 min
The Authority of Majority Consensus in Judicial Rulings
Commentary expanding the principle that majority consensus renders minority dissent irrelevant, applied to qada and supported by usul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that later consensus overrides earlier ikhtilaf and explain when a judge's ruling must be overturned.

Core Idea
When the majority agrees, minority dissent does not prevent consensus—a ruling against it is void.

In Hanafi jurisprudence, the agreement of the majority of scholars in an era establishes a binding consensus (ijma'), regardless of isolated dissent. This lesson explores how this principle operates in judicial practice: if a judge rules on a matter that was once disputed but later scholars reached consensus, his ruling must be overturned by the next judge. We will work through the textual evidence, the example of the mudabbar, and the usul foundation that majority agreement suffices for ijma'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Find the key statement: 'And in matters upon which the majority have agreed, the disagreement of a few is not considered.' This is the core rule.
The phrase 'la yu'tabaru khilaf al-ba'd' is the operative maxim.
2
Apply to Judicial Rulings
Understand the scenario: if a judgment is itself disputed among scholars (e.g., judgment against an absent person), once a judge enacts it, it becomes a matter of consensus. A later judge must enforce it. Conversely, if a judge rules against the majority, his ruling is contrary to consensus and must be overturned.
The text cites 'Kamal al-Dirayah' for this application.
3
Examine the Mudabbar Example
The sale of a mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) was disputed in early times. Later scholars (i.e., the majority) agreed it is impermissible. According to Hanafis, this later consensus removes the earlier disagreement, making any ruling permitting the sale invalid.
Contrast with the opposing view that later consensus does not remove earlier ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the Usul Foundation
The passage defines ijma' and presents different views on how many mujtahids are needed to form consensus. The relevant ruling for this lesson is the view from 'Kashf al-Usul al-Bazdawi': consensus is established by the agreement of the majority (akthar al-mujtahidin), and minority dissent is not considered.
This view is attributed to Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and aligns with the later Hanafi position.
5
Recognize When Dissent is Irrelevant
The text distinguishes between a dissenter whose ijtihad is not accepted (like Ibn Abbas on riba al-fadl) and one whose ijtihad is sound (like Ibn Abbas on hajb). Only in the former case does the dissent break the possibility of consensus? Actually, the text says when the dissenter's ijtihad is not allowed and everyone rejects it, then consensus is established without him; if his ijtihad is sound, consensus cannot be established without him. The key is that the majority's agreement still renders his dissent irrelevant for the practical ruling? Clarity: the text states that if a judge rules based on an unsound dissent, his ruling is overturned; if based on a sound dissent, it is not overturned because no consensus formed. So the principle: when the majority agrees, a judge cannot follow the minority; if he does, his ruling is void.
The example of Ibn Abbas on hajb shows that his opinion was considered valid, so no consensus existed, and a judge could follow it.

And in matters upon which the majority have agreed, the disagreement of a few is not considered. Summary of al-Nihayah for 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: As for when the judgment itself is disputed: such as judgment against an absent person, then at that time it becomes a matter of consensus; so after execution, if it is brought to another judge, he must enforce it. Then I saw in al-Khulasah what strengthens my choice, where it said: Indeed this condition—meaning that he is knowledgeable of the disagreement, although it is the apparent madhhab, but he gives a fatwa contrary to it. End quote. Our master said in his 'Bahr': The verified relied-upon view is that his knowledge that what he ruled upon is a matter of ijtihad is a condition; but his knowledge that the matter is one of ijtihad is not. He said: This is indicated by what is in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. End quote. And the difference between them is not apparent then. And Allah knows best. End of what is in al-Minah. [1] His statement: As for when the judgment itself is disputed: such that some scholars say it is effective, and some say it is not effective, based on whether the ruling is disputed or not, like the sale of a mudabbar. For indeed in the early period it was disputed. Then the later scholars agreed that it is not permissible. So our scholars said: The later consensus removes the earlier disagreement. And others said: It does not remove it. And similarly the ruling applies to every incident where they differed about its disagreement. As in Sharh al-Nuqayah by al-Quhistani. [2] His statement: Then at that time it becomes a matter of consensus; by execution of another judge; because the locus of disagreement did not exist before the judgment, but rather existed after it, so another judgment is necessary for weighting. As in Kamal al-Dirayah. [3] His statement: The disagreement of a few is not considered. So according to this, if a judge rules contrary to what the majority hold, his ruling is contrary to consensus, and whoever it is brought to should overturn it. And it is appropriate to interpret the author's statement as referring to the case where the one dissenting is one whose ijtihad is not allowed, and everyone rejects his statement—like the statement of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) regarding the permissibility of riba al-fadl. For his ijtihad was not allowed, until it is reported that he recanted to their view, and no one followed him, and they rejected it. The Book of Judgment, Imprisonment, and the Letter from Judge to Judge. It is mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh: The scholars differed about whether consensus is established by the agreement of the majority of mujtahids or whether the agreement of all is necessary. If a judge rules on its permissibility, his ruling must be overturned; because consensus is established on prohibition without him. But if his view is considered valid, consensus is not established without him—like the statement of Ibn Abbas regarding the exclusion of the mother from one-third to one-sixth by the presence of multiple brothers. If a judge ruled by it, he would not be opposing consensus. As in al-'Inayah. (1) His statement: 'They differed...' etc.; Know first that consensus in language is agreement; in Shariah it is the agreement of qualified righteous mujtahids of the ummah of Muhammad (peace be upon him) in one era upon a matter not proven by the Quran or Sunnah definitively, whether that matter is a statement or an action, religious, rational, or customary. Second, that consensus being from the Companions, or from the Ahl al-Bayt, or from the people of Madinah, or the passing of the era is not a condition. Third, they differed about what constitutes consensus: 1. It is said: The minimum by which consensus is established is three, and al-Sarakhsi inclined to this because it is the minimum of a group. 2. And it is said: Two, because it is the minimum of a plural. 3. And it is said: If only one mujtahid remains, his statement is consensus; because when alone, the term 'ummah' applies to him: Allah said: 'Indeed Ibrahim was an ummah, devoutly obedient to Allah, a hanif' (Quran 16:120). Mentioned by Ibn al-Malak and other usulis. 4. And in Kashf al-Usul al-Bazdawi: Consensus is established by the agreement of the majority of mujtahids, and the disagreement of a few is not considered. This is the statement of Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) expanding on the ruling that 'when the majority have agreed, the disagreement of a few is not considered.' It begins by applying this principle to judicial rulings: if a judgment is itself disputed among scholars (e.g., judgment against an absent person), once a judge enacts it, it becomes a matter of consensus, and any subsequent judge must enforce it. The commentary then clarifies that the disagreement of a minority does not break the consensus of the majority. It discusses the condition that the judge must be aware that the matter is one of ijtihad. The text then delves into an example: the sale of a mudabbar (a slave who will be free upon the master's death). This was disputed earlier, but later scholars reached consensus against its permissibility. Here, Hanafis hold that the later consensus removes the earlier disagreement, while others disagree. The principle is further extended to all similar cases. The commentary emphasizes that if a judge rules against the majority, his ruling is contrary to consensus and must be overturned. It uses the example of Ibn Abbas's isolated opinion on riba al-fadl, which was rejected by all other Companions. The second part of the passage provides an usul al-fiqh discussion on the definition of ijma' (consensus) and the differing opinions on how many mujtahids are needed to form it. The definitions and conditions are laid out, and then the various views are presented: some say three, some say two, some say one, and the final view, attributed to al-Bazdawi, Tabari, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, states that consensus is established by the agreement of the majority, and the disagreement of a few is not considered. This supports the earlier ruling.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus: the agreement of qualified mujtahids of the Muslim ummah in one era upon a ruling not definitively established by Quran or Sunnah.
خلاف البعض (khilaf al-ba'd): The disagreement of a few (minority) scholars. In this context, it is considered irrelevant when the majority has agreed.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon his master's death. The sale of such a slave is the example used to illustrate the principle of later consensus overriding earlier disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position regarding later consensus and earlier disagreement?
A) Later consensus does not affect earlier disagreement.
B) Later consensus removes earlier disagreement.
C) Later consensus only removes disagreement if it is unanimous.
D) Later consensus is irrelevant for judicial rulings.

Key Takeaway
The principle that majority consensus supersedes minority dissent is foundational in Hanafi fiqh and directly impacts the enforceability of judicial rulings.

Effect of a Judge's Verdict Based on False Testimony: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling on marriage after a verdict based on perjury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the effect of a judge's verdict based on false testimony, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding the second husband's intercourse.

Core Idea
A judge's verdict based on false testimony changes the ruling for all according to Abu Hanifa, but knowledge of the truth matters to Muhammad.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of a judge's ruling based on false testimony. The scenario involves a wife who falsely claims triple divorce, leading to a judicial separation, and then marries another man. The key question: if the second husband knows the truth, is intercourse permissible? The three great Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—offer differing views, rooted in their understanding of the authority of the judge's verdict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand the hypothetical: a wife brings false witnesses claiming her husband divorced her three times; the judge rules for separation. She then marries another man. The question is whether the second husband may have intercourse with her, especially if he knows the testimony was false.
2
Compare the Imams' Positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (initial view) hold it is permissible for the second husband in all cases. Muhammad holds it is not permissible if the second husband knows the truth. Trace each imam's reasoning.
Abu Yusuf's later view may differ but is not mentioned here.
3
Analyze the Proof (Dalil)
Both sides rely on the authority of the judge's verdict. For Abu Hanifa, the verdict establishes the ruling outwardly and inwardly for everyone. For Muhammad, knowledge of the truth overrides the apparent ruling.
The text does not quote specific hadith or Quran, so the proof is based on rational arguments (dalil aqli).
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the second husband may always have intercourse. Under Muhammad, if he knows the truth, he must abstain. This affects the validity of the second marriage and subsequent children.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The later Hanafi authorities follow Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, giving full effect to the judge's verdict. Thus, the second husband's knowledge does not prevent permissibility.
This is based on the preference for the opinion of the majority of the early Hanafi masters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : والقضاء بحرمة ; كما إذا ادعت امرأة على زوجها أنه طلقها ثلاثاً، وأقامت بينة كاذبة، فقضى القاضي بالفرقة. فعلى قول أبي حنيفة له وقول أبي يوسف له أولاً : لا يحل للزوج الأول وطؤها ظاهراً وباطناً، ويحلُّ للثاني وطؤها ظاهراً وباطناً عَلِمَ بحقيقة الحال أو لا، وعلى قول محمد الله : لا يحل للثاني وطؤها إذا كان عالماً بحقيقة الحال أو حلّ ; كما إذا
[3] His statement: 'And the judgment of prohibition or permissibility is effective outwardly and inwardly' - as when a woman claims against her husband that he divorced her three times, and she brings false witnesses, and the judge rules for separation. According to Abu Hanifa and initially Abu Yusuf: it is not permissible for the first husband to have intercourse with her, outwardly and inwardly, and it is permissible for the second husband to have intercourse with her, outwardly and inwardly, whether he knows the true situation or not. According to Muhammad: it is not permissible for the second husband to have intercourse with her if he knows the true situation, or (it is permissible) if he does not; as when...
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of a judge's verdict based on false testimony. The scenario: a wife falsely claims her husband divorced her three times, produces perjured witnesses, and the judge rules for separation. She then marries another man. The question is whether the second husband may have intercourse with her. The three Hanafi imams differ: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in his initial view) hold that the second husband may have intercourse both outwardly and inwardly, regardless of whether he knows the testimony was false. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that if the second husband knows the truth (i.e., that the first divorce never happened), then it is not permissible for him to have intercourse, because he knows she is still the wife of the first husband. This difference stems from the weight given to the judge's verdict: for Abu Hanifa, the verdict establishes the ruling even if based on false evidence, and this applies to all people, including those who know the truth. For Muhammad, knowledge of the truth overrides the apparent ruling. The passage only gives the initial view of Abu Yusuf; it is possible that he later revised his opinion.

Key Terms
شهادة زور (shahadat zur): False testimony given deliberately to deceive the court.
ظاهراً وباطناً (zahiran wa batinan): Outwardly (in terms of legal effect) and inwardly (in terms of actual reality).
قضاء (qada'): A judge's ruling or verdict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Imam Muhammad regarding the second husband having intercourse with a woman who became lawful through a judge's verdict based on false testimony, if the second husband knows the truth?
A) It is permissible for him
B) It is not permissible for him
C) It is permissible only if he was not aware of the false testimony
D) It is permissible only after a new marriage contract

Key Takeaway
The authority of a judge's verdict in Hanafi fiqh is such that, even when based on perjury, it changes the ruling for all, according to the majority of the imams.

Effect of a Judge's Ruling Based on False Testimony
Ruling and conditions for effectiveness of judge's decision with false testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a judge's ruling based on false testimony is effective outwardly and inwardly, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the second husband's permissibility.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling based on false testimony is effective outwardly and inwardly if the claim is for a specific cause and the judge is unaware of the falsehood.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a perplexing aspect of judicial rulings: what happens when a judge's decision is based on false testimony? The Hanafi school holds that such a ruling is generally effective both outwardly (in worldly consequences) and inwardly (in religious permissibility), but only under specific conditions. The passage also clarifies that the disagreement (ikhtilaf) of later scholars like al-Shafi'i can be considered even if it has no precedent among the early generation, contrary to some views. Let's dissect the ruling and its qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the first sentence: 'والقضاء بحرمة أو حل ينفذ ظاهراً أو باطناً، ولو بشهادة زور'. This establishes the default: the ruling is effective both outwardly and inwardly even with false testimony.
This is the general principle.
2
Note the essential condition: specific cause
The ruling is tied to 'إذا ادعاه بسبب معين' (if claimed for a specific cause from contracts or annulments). The example of absolute ownership vs. specific cause shows the limit: if the claim is absolute ownership, the ruling does not permit intercourse by consensus.
The cause must be a specific contract or annulment like marriage, divorce, sale, etc.
3
Identify the conditions for inner effectiveness
Two conditions are stated: (1) the subject (mahal) must be capable (qabil) of such a contract/annulment, meaning the case must be one where a valid transaction could have occurred; (2) the judge must not know the testimony is false. If the judge knows, the ruling is void.
If the judge knows, the ruling is batil completely.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text presents the example of a woman falsely claiming triple divorce. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say the second husband may have intercourse with her even if he knows the truth. Muhammad says he may not if he knows. This shows a practical difference in the extent of outward vs. inward effectiveness.
The mufta bihi position inclines towards Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
5
Understand the dispute about early ikhtilaf
The author refutes the view that only disagreements among the Companions (sadr al-awwal) are considered. The correct view (asah) is that even the disagreement of later scholars like al-Shafi'i is valid, allowing judges to rule based on their opinions.
This expands the range of acceptable opinions for a judge.

Source Text

(والقضاء بحرمة " أو حلّ يَنْفُدُ ظاهراً أو باطناً، ولو بشهادة زورٍ إذا ادعاه بسبب معين) ، حتّى لو ادعى جارية ملكاً مطلقاً، وأقام على ذلك بينة زور وقضى القاضى به لا يحل له وطؤها بالإجماع

[ 1 ] قوله : إن المعتبر ... الخ ; فمعناه الاختلافُ الذي يجعل المحل مجتهداً فيه هو
الاختلافُ الذي كان بين الصحابة والتابعين، لا الذي يقع بعدهم، وعلى هذا إذا حكم الشافعي أو المالكي برأيه بما يخالف رأي من تقدَّم عليه من الصدر الأول، ورفع ذلك إلى حاكم لم ير ذلك كان له أن ينقضه. كذا في «العناية»، وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل فارجع إلى «الكفاية»(۳).
[٢) قوله : حتى يكون...الخ ; خلافُ الشافعي أو مالك لا يعتبر إذا كان قوله مما يقولُ به بعض علماء الصدر الأول، وإن لم يكن كذلك فلا يعتبر. صرح به في «غاية البيان»، وغيرها. [٣] قوله : والقضاء بحرمة ; كما إذا ادعت امرأة على زوجها أنه طلقها ثلاثاً، وأقامت بينة كاذبة، فقضى القاضي بالفرقة. فعلى قول أبي حنيفة له وقول أبي يوسف له أولاً : لا يحل للزوج الأول وطؤها ظاهراً وباطناً، ويحلُّ للثاني وطؤها ظاهراً وباطناً عَلِمَ بحقيقة الحال أو لا، وعلى قول محمد الله : لا يحل للثاني وطؤها إذا كان عالماً بحقيقة الحال أو حلّ ; كما إذا ادعى رجل على امرأة نكاحاً وهي تجحد ، فأقام عليها شاهدي زور، وقضى القاضي بالنكاح بينهما ، حل للزوج وطؤها وحل للمرأة التمكين عند أبي حنيفة ، وعند محمد ه لا يَحِلُّ ذلك.
ينفذ ذلك القضاء ظاهراً : أي من حيث الظاهر بين الناس، مثل: ثبوت التمكين
والنفقة والقسم، وغير ذلك.
وباطناً ; أي من حيث الباطن ، يعني بينه وبين الله تعالى، مثل ثبوت الملك والحل. ولو بشهادة زور، قيَّد بها ; لأنه لو ظهر الشهود عبيداً أو كفاراً أو محدودين في قذف لم ينفذ إجماعاً ; لأنها ليست بحجة أصلا ، بخلاف الفساق، ولا مكان الوقوف
عليهم، فلم تكن شهادتهم حجّة ، ذكره في «البحر»، وغيره.
واعلم أن هاهنا لا بد من قيدين :
الأول : أن يكون المحل قابلاً.
والثاني : أن يكون القاضي غير عالم بزور الشهود.
فإنّ رجلاً لو ادعى على امرأة أنها زوجته، وأثبت ذلك بشهادة الزور، وهو يعلم أنها محرمة عليه بكونها منكوحة الغير أو معتدته أو بكونها مرتدة، فإنه لا ينفذ قضاءُ القاضي فيه باطناً اتفاقاً ; لأنه وإن كان الملك بسبب لكن لا يمكن إنشاؤه. وأما ظاهراً فلا شك في نفاذه كسائر الأحكام بشهادة الزور في غـيـر العـقـودِ والفسوخ، وليس المراد بنفاذه ظاهراً حل الوطء له ، وحل تمكينها منه، بل أمر القاضي لها به ، وأما الحل فهو فرع نفاذه. وكذا لو علم القاضي بكذب الشهودِ لا ينفذ قضاؤه أصلاً ; لعدم شرط القضاء، وهو الشهادة الصادقة في زعم القاضي. كما صرحوا به.
إذا ادّعاه بسبب معيَّن من العقود والفسوخ؛ كالنكاح والطلاق والبيع والشراء
والإقالة والرد بالعيب والنسب، وفي الهبة والصدقة روايتان كذا في «المنح»، وغيره.
And the ruling regarding prohibition or permissibility is effective outwardly and inwardly, even with false testimony, if he claims it for a specific cause. Also, it is said in al-Hidayah: 'Indeed, the consideration is the disagreement in the first generation, i.e., the Companions, but the correct view is that this condition is not required, so that even the disagreement of al-Shafi'i is considered.' (The ruling regarding prohibition or permissibility is effective outwardly and inwardly, even with false testimony, if he claims it for a specific cause), so much so that if someone claims ownership of a slave girl absolutely, and produces false evidence and the judge rules accordingly, it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her by consensus, for the ruling of consensus is established without his saying, like the saying of Ibn Abbas to him regarding the permissibility of excess in usury goods, for the Companions did not permit him this ijtihad, until it was narrated that he returned to their saying, and consensus was established without his saying. End. So what the commentator intended from objecting to the matn by mentioning the saying of the author of al-Hidayah is not complete, so ponder it. [1] His saying: 'Indeed, the consideration... etc.'; its meaning is that the disagreement that makes the matter subject to ijtihad is the disagreement that was among the Companions and the Followers, not that which occurs after them, and accordingly, if al-Shafi'i or al-Maliki rules according to his opinion contrary to the opinion of those before him from the first generation, and that is raised to a judge who does not see that, he has the right to reverse it. Thus in al-'Inayah, and if you wish more detail, refer to al-Kifayah. [2] His saying: 'so that even... etc.'; the disagreement of al-Shafi'i or Malik is not considered unless his saying is what some scholars of the first generation said; otherwise, it is not considered. This is stated in Ghayat al-Bayan and others. [3] His saying: 'And the ruling regarding prohibition...'; as when a woman claims that her husband divorced her three times, and produces false witnesses, and the judge rules for separation. According to the saying of Abu Hanifah and the saying of Abu Yusuf initially, it is not permissible for the first husband to have intercourse with her outwardly and inwardly, and it is permissible for the second husband to have intercourse with her outwardly and inwardly, whether he knows the true situation or not. And according to the saying of Muhammad, it is not permissible for the second to have intercourse with her if he knows the true situation. Or permission; as when a man claims a marriage to a woman while she denies it, and he produces two false witnesses, and the judge rules for marriage between them, then it is permissible for the husband to have intercourse with her and permissible for the woman to submit according to Abu Hanifah, but according to Muhammad, that is not permissible. That judgment is effective outwardly: i.e., from the outward perspective among people, such as establishing submission, maintenance, division, etc. And inwardly: i.e., from the inward perspective, meaning between him and Allah, such as establishing ownership and permissibility. 'Even with false testimony' is specified because if the witnesses turn out to be slaves, disbelievers, or those punished for slander, the judgment is not effective by consensus, because they are not evidence at all, unlike the immoral ones, and because they cannot be verified, so their testimony is not evidence. Mentioned in al-Bahr and others. And know that here two conditions are necessary: First: that the subject is capable. Second: that the judge is not aware of the falsehood of the witnesses. If a man claims a woman as his wife, and proves that with false testimony, while he knows that she is prohibited to him because she is married to another or in her waiting period or an apostate, then the judge's ruling is not effective inwardly by agreement; because although ownership is due but it cannot be established. As for outwardly, there is no doubt of its effectiveness like other rulings with false testimony in non-contracts and annulments. The intended meaning of its effectiveness outwardly is not the permissibility of intercourse for him and her submission to him, but rather the judge's order to her to do so. As for permissibility, it is subordinate to its effectiveness. Likewise, if the judge knows the witnesses are lying, his ruling is not effective at all, because the condition of judgment is absent, which is truthful testimony in the judge's opinion, as they stated. If he claims it for a specific cause from contracts and annulments, such as marriage, divorce, sale, purchase, revocation, return due to defect, and lineage, and regarding gift and charity there are two narrations, as in al-Minh and others.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a super-commentary (hashiyah) on the sharh of al-Wiqayah, addressing the legal effect of a judge's ruling based on false testimony. The author begins by stating the core ruling: a judge's decision regarding prohibition or permissibility is effective both outwardly (zahiran) and inwardly (batinan), even if based on false testimony, provided the claimant specifies a particular cause (sabab mu'ayyan) such as a contract or annulment. The author then notes an additional point from al-Hidayah regarding the condition that a difference of opinion must be from the early scholars (sadr al-awwal) for a later opinion to be considered valid. He clarifies that the correct view is that this condition is not required; even the opinion of al-Shafi'i can be considered. The commentary then provides examples and qualifications. A key example: if a man claims ownership of a slave girl and produces false evidence, the judge's ruling does not permit him to have intercourse with her by consensus, because the ownership is absolute and not based on a specific cause. The passage then explores the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the effect of such a ruling on subsequent marriages. According to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, if a woman falsely claims triple divorce and a judge rules for separation, the first husband cannot have intercourse with her, but the second husband can, regardless of his knowledge. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the second husband cannot if he knows the truth. The author then explains the meaning of 'outwardly' (e.g., maintenance, division) and 'inwardly' (e.g., actual permissibility). He specifies that the ruling applies to false testimony (shahadat zumr), but not to testimony that is fundamentally invalid (e.g., from slaves, disbelievers, or those punished for slander), which is not effective by consensus. Two conditions are necessary: (1) the subject (mahal) must be capable (qabil), meaning the case must be one where a valid contract or annulment could have occurred; (2) the judge must not know the testimony is false. If the judge knows, the ruling is void. Finally, the commentary lists specific causes that qualify, such as marriage, divorce, sale, etc., noting two narrations regarding gift and charity. Throughout, the author relies on authoritative Hanafi sources like al-Hidayah, al-'Inayah, al-Kifayah, al-Bahr, and al-Minh.

Key Terms
شهادة زور (shahadat zumr): False testimony given knowingly, which the judge deems truthful at the time of ruling.
الصدر الأول (al-sadr al-awwal): The first generation of Muslims, specifically the Companions and Followers, whose consensus or disagreement is considered authoritative.
نفاذ القضاء (nafadh al-qada): The effectiveness of a judge's ruling, both outwardly (zahiran) in worldly matters and inwardly (batinan) in terms of religious permissibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding a man who claims absolute ownership of a slave girl and produces false evidence, with the judge ruling in his favor?
A) It is permissible for him to have intercourse with her immediately.
B) It is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her by consensus.
C) It is permissible only if he did not know the testimony was false.
D) It is permissible outwardly but not inwardly.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling based on false testimony is effective outwardly and inwardly if the claim is for a specific cause, the subject is capable, and the judge is unaware of the falsehood; within the Hanafi school, the ruling binds even knowledgeable parties in matters of permissibility.

6 min
Ruling on False Testimony in Marriage Claim: Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of judgment based on false testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi positions on the effect of a judgment based on false testimony and apply the external/internal effectiveness distinction.

Core Idea
A judgment based on false testimony is effective externally but not internally according to Abu Hanifa; Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it entirely invalid.

When a judge rules on the basis of false testimony, does the ruling carry any weight? In Hanafi law, this question reveals a deep intra-madhhab dispute on the nature of judicial decisions. This lesson examines the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, focusing on the distinction between external enforceability and internal validity before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note the ruling of Abu Hanifa (permissible for husband to have intercourse and for wife to submit) and Muhammad (not permissible). Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Both positions are from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the external/internal distinction
The text explains that the judgment is effective 'externally' (ẓāhiran) for worldly matters like maintenance and time-division, and 'internally' (bāṭinan) for ownership and permissibility before Allah. Grasp this key analytical tool.
This distinction allows the legal system to maintain order while acknowledging sin.
3
Note the consensus exception
The passage states that if the false witnesses are slaves, disbelievers, or those punished for slander, the judgment is ineffective by consensus. This is because such testimony has no evidentiary value at all.
This exception contrasts with sinful witnesses (fussāq), whose testimony can be examined.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the couple may live as married; under Muhammad, they must separate. This is the concrete consequence of the dispute.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this text; later fatwa collections may clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى رجل على امرأة نكاحاً وهي تجحد ، فأقام عليها شاهدي زور، وقضى القاضي بالنكاح بينهما ، حل للزوج وطؤها وحل للمرأة التمكين عند أبي حنيفة ، وعند محمد ه لا يَحِلُّ ذلك. ينفذ ذلك القضاء ظاهراً : أي من حيث الظاهر بين الناس، مثل: ثبوت التمكين والنفقة والقسم، وغير ذلك. وباطناً ؛ أي من حيث الباطن ، يعني بينه وبين الله تعالى، مثل ثبوت الملك والحل. ولو بشهادة زور، قيَّد بها ؛ لأنه لو ظهر الشهود عبيداً أو كفاراً أو محدودين في قذف لم ينفذ إجماعاً ؛ لأنها ليست بحجة أصلا ، بخلاف الفساق، ولا مكان الوقوف (1) عليهم، فلم تكن شهادتهم حجّة ، ذكره في «البحر»، وغيره.
A man claimed a marriage from a woman while she denied it, so he brought two false witnesses against her, and the judge ruled for the marriage between them. It is permissible for the husband to have intercourse with her and permissible for the woman to submit according to Abu Hanifa, but according to Muhammad, that is not permissible. That judgment is effective externally: i.e., in terms of what is apparent among people, such as the establishment of submission, maintenance, and division of time, etc. And internally: i.e., in terms of the inner reality, meaning between him and Allah the Exalted, such as the establishment of ownership and permissibility. Even if by false testimony, it is restricted by that; because if the witnesses turn out to be slaves, disbelievers, or those punished for slander, it is not effective by consensus; because they are not evidence at all, unlike the sinful, and because it is possible to pause [to examine] them, so their testimony is not evidence. Mentioned in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a judge's ruling based on false testimony (شهادة زور) in a marriage claim. According to Abu Hanifa, the ruling is effective both externally (ظاهراً) and internally (باطناً), meaning that the husband is legally permitted to have intercourse and the wife must submit, even in the sight of Allah. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds the opposite: it is not permissible at all. The text then clarifies the distinction: external effectiveness refers to worldly consequences such as maintenance and division of time; internal effectiveness refers to the actual permissibility of marital relations. The author notes that if the witnesses are found to be slaves, disbelievers, or those convicted of slander (حدود في قذف), the ruling is ineffective by consensus because such testimony has no evidentiary value. Sinful witnesses (فساق) are different because their testimony can be examined. This is from al-Bahr and other works.

Key Terms
شهادة الزور (shahādat al-zūr): False testimony given intentionally to mislead the court
القضاء ظاهراً وباطناً (al-qaḍā' ẓāhirān wa bāṭinan): The distinction between the apparent enforceability of a judgment in worldly matters and its real validity before Allah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a judge rules in favor of a man's marriage claim based on false testimony, what is the status of the marriage?
A) The marriage is valid both externally and internally.
B) The marriage is valid externally but not internally.
C) The marriage is invalid entirely.
D) The marriage is valid only if the woman consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a judgment based on false testimony is effective externally, giving rise to legal consequences, even if internally it remains a sin.

Two Conditions for Internal Validity of a Judgment Based on False Testimony
Specification of two conditions for internal validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a judgment based on false testimony to be internally effective and distinguish between internal and external effectiveness.

Core Idea
A judgment on false testimony is externally effective but internally valid only if the subject matter is capable and the judge is unaware.

In Hanafi law, a judge's ruling based on false testimony is not automatically void. While such a ruling is externally effective—meaning it must be enforced outwardly—its internal validity (i.e., its effect on the actual legal status) depends on two conditions. This lesson dissects those conditions as set forth by classical Hanafi jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the passage, locate the two conditions required for internal effectiveness: (1) the subject matter (al-maḥall) must be capable (qābil) of ownership or contractual validity, and (2) the judge must be unaware of the falsehood of the witnesses.
The passage calls these 'qaydayn'—two indispensable restrictions.
2
Analyze the first condition: capable subject matter
Consider the example given: a man claims a woman is his wife but she is actually married to another, in her ʿidda, or an apostate. In such cases, the subject matter (the marriage) cannot be validly created, so the judgment is internally ineffective by consensus.
The condition stems from the fact that a judge cannot make lawful what is intrinsically unlawful.
3
Analyze the second condition: judge's ignorance of falsehood
If the judge knows the witnesses are lying, his ruling is completely ineffective—both internally and externally—because the condition of truthful testimony in his estimation is absent. The judgment is void ab initio.
This underscores the importance of the judge's subjective assessment of witness credibility.
4
Distinguish internal vs external effectiveness
External effectiveness means the judge can order compliance (e.g., command the woman to submit), but it does not make intercourse permissible or create a valid marriage. Internal effectiveness would make the act lawful; external effectiveness is merely procedural.
The passage clarifies that permissibility (ḥill) is a branch of internal effectiveness, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هاهنا لا بد من قيدين : الأول : أن يكون المحل قابلاً. والثاني : أن يكون القاضي غير عالم بزور الشهود. فإنّ رجلاً لو ادعى على امرأة أنها زوجته، وأثبت ذلك بشهادة الزور، وهو يعلم أنها محرمة عليه بكونها منكوحة الغير أو معتدته أو بكونها مرتدة، فإنه لا ينفذ قضاءُ القاضي فيه باطناً اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه وإن كان الملك بسبب لكن لا يمكن إنشاؤه. وأما ظاهراً فلا شك في نفاذه كسائر الأحكام بشهادة الزور في غـيـر العـقـودِ والفسوخ، وليس المراد بنفاذه ظاهراً حل الوطء له ، وحل تمكينها منه، بل أمر القاضي لها به ، وأما الحل فهو فرع نفاذه. وكذا لو علم القاضي بكذب الشهودِ لا ينفذ قضاؤه أصلاً ؛ لعدم شرط القضاء، وهو الشهادة الصادقة في زعم القاضي. كما صرحوا به.
And know that here two conditions are indispensable: First, that the subject matter be capable [of ownership]. Second, that the judge be unaware of the falsehood of the witnesses. For if a man claimed against a woman that she is his wife, and established that by false testimony, while he knows that she is unlawful to him because she is the wife of another, or his waiting period woman, or because she is an apostate, then the judge's ruling is not effective internally by agreement; because although ownership [can be established] by a cause, it cannot be created [in this case]. As for externally, there is no doubt about its effectiveness, like other rulings based on false testimony in matters other than contracts and dissolutions. And the intent of its external effectiveness is not the permissibility of intercourse for him, nor her submission to him, but rather the judge's command to her to do so. As for permissibility, it is a branch of its effectiveness. Similarly, if the judge knows the witnesses are lying, his ruling is not effective at all; due to the absence of the condition of judgment, which is truthful testimony in the judge's opinion. As they have stated.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage establishes two necessary conditions for a judgment based on false testimony to be effective internally. The first condition is that the subject matter (المحل) must be capable of being owned; for example, a woman who is already married to another or in her waiting period from another marriage is not a valid subject for a new marriage contract. The second condition is that the judge must be unaware that the witnesses are false. If the judge knows they are lying, his ruling is void. The text explains that if the woman is unlawful to the man due to being married to another, in her 'idda, or an apostate, the ruling is not effective internally by consensus, but externally it is effective like any other ruling based on false testimony in non-contractual matters. However, external effectiveness here means only that the judge orders her to submit, not that intercourse becomes permissible; permissibility is a consequence of internal effectiveness. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Key Terms
المحل قابل (al-maḥall qābil): The subject matter that is capable of being owned or valid for a contract
القاضي غير عالم بزور الشهود (al-qāḍī ghayr 'ālim bi-zūr al-shuhūd): The judge's ignorance of the falsehood of witnesses is a condition for the internal validity of his ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a judge rules based on false testimony that a man married a woman who is actually in her ʿidda from another marriage, what is the effect?
A) The ruling is effective both externally and internally.
B) The ruling is effective externally but not internally.
C) The ruling is not effective at all.
D) The ruling is effective only if the woman consents.

Key Takeaway
A judgment based on false testimony is externally enforceable but becomes internally valid only if the subject matter is legally capable and the judge is unaware of the perjury.
