Enforceability of a Judge's Ruling Based on False Testimony
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of false testimony on a judge's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the enforceability of a judge's ruling based on false testimony, and explain the relied-upon view and its practical consequences.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling based on false testimony is enforceable outwardly but not inwardly; the witnesses sin, but the ruling stands.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: if a judge issues a ruling (e.g., declaring a marriage valid) based on false testimony, does that ruling create real legal consequences in the sight of Allah? Abu Hanifa argued that a haram cause cannot produce a halal effect. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagreed, and their view became the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the two companions regarding the inward effect (batinan) of a judge's decision based on false testimony.
Abu Hanifa says the ruling should not take effect inwardly; the companions say it does not take effect inwardly but does outwardly.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Grasp why Abu Hanifa finds it problematic: absolute haram (false testimony) cannot be a cause for halal. The judge's ruling cannot make something lawful that is actually unlawful.
3
Analyze the companions' arguments
Review the evidence and analogies presented by the companions: the precedent of 'Ali (ra), the li'an analogy, the buyer-seller dispute, and the overall purpose of the judiciary to end disputes.
The judge's ruling acts like a new contract; false testimony is only the external trigger.
4
Distinguish between outward and inward enforceability
Understand that 'zahiran' refers to worldly enforcement (the judge hands over the wife, orders her to enable the husband), while 'batinan' refers to the actual legal reality before Allah.
This distinction is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh.
5
Recognize the practical consequence and the mufta bihi
Note that the fatwa is according to the two companions' view. The wife is lawfully married in this world, but she is not lawful for him in the sight of Allah; the witnesses commit a grave sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندهما يَنْفُدُ ظاهراً : أي يُسَلِّمُ القاضي الزَّوجة إلى الزوج، ويأمرها بالتمكين لا باطناً : أي لا يثبت فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى، ومذهبهما ظاهر، وأما مذهب أبي حنيفة فمشكل جداً، فإنَّ الحرامَ المحض كيف يكون سبباً للحل فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. وجوابه: إنا لم نجعل الحرام المحض: وهو الشَّهادة الكاذبة من حيث إنَّه إخبار كاذب سبباً للحلّ، بل حُكم القاضى صار كإنشاء عقد جديد والشافعي وأحمد له ينفذ ظاهراً ؛ لأنَّ شهادة الزور حجَّةٌ ظاهراً لا باطناً. فصار كما لو كان الشهود عبيداً أو كفاراً أو محدودين في قذف، وكما لو قضى القاضي بنكاح رجل على امرأة وهي منكوحة أو معتدة لغيره، وكما في الأملاك المرسلة. ولهما : ما روي أنّ عليَّا له قضى بالنكاح بين رجل وامرأة بشهادة الشاهدين ، فقالت : يا أمير المؤمنين إن لم يكن به فزوجني ، فقال عليه : شاهداك زوجاك، ولم يلتفت إلى قولها من تجديد النكاح مع كون الشهود زوراً، بدلالة القصَّةِ بناءً على أنَّ حكم القاضي بمنزلة إنشاء عقد صحيح ؛ ولأنَّ القضاء موضوع لقطع المنازعة بينهما من كل وجه ، فلو لم ينفذ باطناً كان تمهيداً لها ، وقد عُهِدَ نفود القضاء بمثل ذلك في الشرع. ألا ترى أن التفريق في اللعان ينفذ باطناً، وأحدهما كاذب بيقين، وكذا إذا اختلف المتبايعان وتحالفا يفسخ القاضي بينهما البيع، فينفذ الفسخ باطناً، حتى يحلُّ للبائع وطء الجارية المبيعة، فكذا في باقي الفسوخ، والقاضي مكلف بحسب الوسع ، فيجب التعديل عليه، إذ الوقوف على حقيقة الصدق متعدّر، والعبيد والكفار والمحدودون في القذف يمكن الوقوف عليهم بخلاف شهود الزور. وعدم النفاذ في الحكم بنكاح منكوحة الغير أو معتدته ؛ لفوات شرط الحكم لا لزور الشهود، إذ شرط الحكم أن يكون في محل قابل له ، ومنكوحة الغير ومعتدته ليست بمحل النكاح ، ولم ينفذ باطناً في المدَّعي بلا سبب ؛ لأنَّ في أسباب الملك تزاحما، وليس تعيين بعض أولى من بعض. وإثبات الملك مطلقاً . غير سبب ليس في وسع البشر، وقال الفقيه أبو الليث من : الفتوى على قولهما، وفي «الولوالجية»: وإثم الشاهدين إثماً عظيماً.
According to them (the two companions), it takes effect outwardly: meaning the judge hands over the wife to the husband and orders her to enable him, but not inwardly: meaning it is not established between him and Allah Almighty. Their position is clear. As for Abu Hanifa's position, it is very problematic: how can absolute haram become a cause for halal between him and Allah Almighty? The response: We did not make the absolute haram—which is false testimony in that it is a false report—a cause for halal. Rather, the judge's ruling became like a new contract. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it takes effect outwardly because false testimony is a proof outwardly but not inwardly. Thus it becomes as if the witnesses were slaves, disbelievers, or those who had been flogged for slander, and as if the judge had ruled for a marriage of a man to a woman who is already married or in 'iddah for another, and as in the case of ambiguous possessions. For them (the two companions) is what is related that 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) ruled for a marriage between a man and a woman on the testimony of two witnesses. She said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, if he is not [the husband], then marry me.' He said: 'Your witnesses have married you.' He did not pay attention to her request for a new marriage even though the witnesses were false, by implication of the story, based on the fact that the judge's ruling is in the position of creating a valid contract. And because the judiciary is established to cut off dispute between them from all sides. If it did not take effect inwardly, it would be a cause for its continuation. And such effectiveness of a ruling has been known in the Shariah. Do you not see that separation by li'an takes effect inwardly even though one of them is certainly lying? Similarly, if the buyer and seller differ and they take an oath, the judge annuls the sale between them; the annulment takes effect inwardly so that it becomes permissible for the seller to have intercourse with the sold slave-girl. Likewise for other annulments. The judge is obligated according to his capacity, so he must seek what is equitable, since ascertaining the truth is impossible. Slaves, disbelievers, and those flogged for slander can be known, unlike false witnesses. And the non-effectiveness in the ruling for marriage to another's wife or a woman in 'iddah is due to the absence of a condition for the ruling, not due to the falsity of the witnesses, because the condition for the ruling is that it be on a subject capable of it, and another's wife and a woman in 'iddah are not subjects for marriage. And it does not take effect inwardly in a claim without cause, because in the causes of ownership there is competition, and designation of some as preferable to others is not possible. And establishing absolute ownership without a cause is not within human capacity. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: The fatwa is according to their view. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: The two witnesses commit a grave sin.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the enforceability of a judge's ruling based on false testimony. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the ruling is enforceable outwardly (i.e., the judge takes practical action such as handing over the wife) but not inwardly (i.e., in the sight of Allah, the woman is not actually lawful). Abu Hanifa finds this problematic because it would mean a forbidden cause (false testimony) produces a lawful effect. The response explains that the judge's ruling itself becomes a new source of legal effect, like a new contract, and the false testimony is only the external cause for the judge to act. The companions support their view with the precedent of 'Ali (ra) who did not renew the marriage despite the possibility that the witnesses were false. They also analogize to other cases where rulings take effect inwardly despite underlying falsehood, such as li'an (where one spouse is certainly lying) and the dissolution of contracts when buyers and sellers differ. The ruling does not take effect if the subject is not legally capable (e.g., a married woman), but that is due to the subject, not the false testimony. The fatwa is according to the companions' view, but the witnesses are still sinful.

Key Terms
شهادة الزور (shahadat al-zur): False testimony knowingly given about a fact.
ظاهراً (zahiran): Outwardly, in terms of the judge's practical enforcement and worldly effects.
باطناً (batinan): Inwardly, in the sight of Allah and regarding the actual legal reality (e.g., actual ownership or marital status).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the effect of a judge's ruling based on false testimony?
A) It is void both outwardly and inwardly.
B) It takes effect both outwardly and inwardly.
C) It takes effect outwardly but not inwardly.
D) It is valid only if the judge knows the witnesses are truthful.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a judge's ruling based on false testimony is enforceable outwardly but does not affect the inward reality before Allah; the witnesses are sinful but the ruling stands.

When a Judge Rules Against His Own Madhhab: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talil and tarjih on judge ruling against his madhhab in mujtahad fihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on the validity of a judge's ruling in a mujtahad fihi contrary to his own madhhab, and apply the later fatwa (according to the companions) to scenarios involving mujtahid and muqallid judges.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling against his madhhab in matters of ijtihad is void per the two companions, and this is the operative fatwa.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a judge who issues a ruling in a matter open to ijtihad (mujtahad fihi) but contrary to the opinion of his own madhhab. The two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, held that such a ruling is void regardless of whether the judge acted intentionally or forgot his madhhab. Abu Hanifa distinguished between forgetfulness and intention, but the later relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of the companions. We will dissect the reasoning, the practical consequences, and how the rule applies differently to mujtahid versus muqallid judges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the actors
Recognize that the passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The issue is the validity of a judge's ruling in a mujtahad fihi when he departs from his own madhhab.
2
State each position clearly
Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if the judge forgot his madhhab, the ruling takes effect (one narration); if intentional, there are two narrations (one takes effect, one not). The Sahibayn hold it is void in both cases.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil) behind each position
The Sahibayn argue that the judge's appointment is conditional upon ruling according to his madhhab; deviation violates the condition. Abu Hanifa gives weight to the judge's own ijtihad and the validity of the ijtihad itself.
4
Identify the tarjih (preference) and its basis
The text states that the fatwa is upon the view of the Sahibayn in later times, because intentional deviation stems from corrupt desire, and forgetfulness still contradicts the basis of appointment. This is the operative rule today.
5
Distinguish the rules for mujtahid vs. muqallid judges
The dispute applies only to a mujtahid judge. A muqallid (non-mujtahid) appointed to judge by the madhhab of Abu Hanifa cannot deviate at all; his ruling is automatically void if he does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقضاء في مُجْتَهَدٍ فيه بخلاف رأيه ناسياً مذهبه أو عامداً لا يَنْقُدُ عندهما، وبه يُفْتَى ولا يُقْضَى. تفصيلُهُ : إِنَّ القاضي لـو قـضـى في أمر مجتهد فيه مخالفاً لرأيه ناسياً مذهبه ، نفذ عند أبي حنيفة له رواية واحدة ، وإن كان عامداً ففيه روايتان، وعندهما لا ينفذ في الوجهين، والفتوى على قولهما. وذكر في الفتوى الصغرى»: إنَّ الفتوى على قوله، فقد اختلف في الفتوى، والوجه في هذا الزمان أن يفتي بقولهما ؛ لأنَّ التارك لمذهبه عمداً لا يفعله إلا لهوى باطل لا لقصد جميل، وأما الناسي ؛ فلأنَّ المقلّد ما قلده إلا ليحكم بمذهبه لا بمذهب غيره، هذا كله في القاضي المجتهد، أما المقلّد، فإنّما ولاه ليحكم بمذهب أبي حنيفة، فلا يملك المخالفة، فيكون معزولاً لا بالنسبة إلى ذلك الحكم. كذا في «الفتح» قوله : في مجتهد فيه ؛ أي في أمر يسوغ الاجتهاد فيه بأن لم يكن مخالفاً لدليل. قوله : رأيه ؛ أي أصل مذهبه كالحنفي إذا حكم على مذهب الشافعي له أو نحوه ، أو بالعكس، أما إذا حكم الحنفي بمذهب أبي يوسف له أو محمد له أو نحوهما من أصحاب الإمام فليس حكماً بخلاف رأيه. كذا في «الدرر شرح الغرر».
And judgment in a matter subject to ijtihad contrary to his own opinion, whether forgetting his madhhab or intentionally, does not take effect according to them (the two companions), and according to that fatwa is given and not [so] judged. Explanation: If a judge judges in a matter subject to ijtihad contrary to his own opinion while forgetting his madhhab, it takes effect according to Abu Hanifa in one narration, and if intentional there are two narrations. According to them (the two companions), it does not take effect in either case, and the fatwa is according to their view. It was mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Sughra that the fatwa is according to his (Abu Hanifa's) view; so there is a difference in fatwa. The correct view in this time is to give fatwa according to their view, because one who intentionally abandons his madhhab does so only for false desire, not for a praiseworthy purpose. As for the one who forgets, because the muqallid only appointed him to judge according to his madhhab, not according to another's madhhab. All this is regarding a mujtahid judge. As for a muqallid (non-mujtahid judge), he was appointed to judge according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, so he does not have the authority to contravene; thus he is dismissed (meaning his ruling is void) with respect to that judgment. As in al-Fath. He said: 'in a matter subject to ijtihad' meaning in a matter in which ijtihad is permissible, such that it does not contradict a conclusive proof. He said: 'his own opinion' meaning the basis of his madhhab, like a Hanafi judging according to the madhhab of al-Shafi'i or similar, or vice versa. As for a Hanafi judging according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad or similar from the companions of the Imam, that is not a judgment contrary to his own opinion. As in al-Durar sharh al-Ghurar.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage clarifies the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a judge who passes judgment in a matter that allows legitimate ijtihad (i.e., it is not decisively proven by a clear text), but he rules contrary to his own madhhab (i.e., the position of his own school). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the ruling is void whether he forgot his madhhab or acted intentionally. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if he forgot, the ruling takes effect; if intentional, there are two narrations (one that it takes effect, one that it does not). The commentator notes that in later times, the fatwa is upon the view of the two companions, because intentional abandonment only stems from corrupt desire, and forgetfulness still contradicts the basis of appointment (the judge is supposed to follow his own school). However, this applies only to a mujtahid judge who has the capacity for ijtihad. A non-mujtahid (muqallid) judge is strictly bound to the madhhab he was appointed to; if he deviates, his ruling is automatically void (he is 'dismissed' in that case). The passage also clarifies that 'his own opinion' means the core opinion of his school (e.g., Hanafi vs. Shafi'i), not differences within the school (e.g., between Abu Hanifa and his disciples), since such internal variations are still considered part of his school's position.

Key Terms
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fihi): A matter in which ijtihad is permissible because there is no definitive proof (qat'i) from the Quran or Sunnah.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A judge who has the capacity to derive rulings directly from evidence, not bound to follow a specific madhhab.
المقلّد (al-muqallid): A judge who does not have the capacity for ijtihad and is appointed to rule according to a specific madhhab (e.g., Abu Hanifa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the ruling if a judge intentionally rules against his own madhhab in a matter of ijtihad?
A) The ruling takes effect regardless.
B) The ruling does not take effect because the judge exceeded his authority.
C) The ruling is valid only if the judge is a mujtahid.
D) The ruling is valid but the judge is sinful.

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi position is that a judge's ruling in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his own madhhab is void, based on the condition of appointment, and this applies strictly to muqallid judges while mujtahid judges were once given more leeway.

Judge Ruling Contrary to His Own Madhhab in Ijtihadi Matters
Explains effect of judge ruling against his own madhhab in ijtihadi matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a Hanafi judge rules contrary to his madhhab in an ijtihadi matter and explain the difference between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A judge's judgment contrary to his madhhab is void according to the Sahibayn – fatwa today follows their view to prevent desire-driven rulings.

This passage addresses a critical question in Islamic judicial procedure: what happens when a judge rules against his own madhhab in a matter open to ijtihad? The text presents the ruling of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and contrasts it with Abu Hanifa's view, ultimately settling on the Sahibayn's position for fatwa in later times. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the enforceability of judicial decisions in Hanafi legal systems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
The act is a judge issuing a ruling in an ijtihadi matter contrary to his own madhhab. The initial statement says: 'لا يَنْفُذُ عندهما' – it does not execute according to the Sahibayn.
The phrase 'لا ينفذ' means the judgment has no legal effect; it is void.
2
Distinguish between forgetful and intentional
The text separates two scenarios: the judge forgot his madhhab (ناسياً مذهبه) or acted intentionally (عامداً). This distinction matters for Abu Hanifa's position but not for the Sahibayn.
Intentional deviation is considered driven by desire (هوى).
3
Contrast the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that if the judge forgot, the judgment executes in one narration; if intentional, there are two narrations. The Sahibayn hold it does not execute in either case.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf recorded in zahir al-riwayah.
4
Understand the tarjih (preference) and mufta bihi
The text states that fatwa is given on the Sahibayn's position, especially in later times, because intentional abandonment of one's madhhab is only for false desire, and even forgetfulness violates the mandate of the appointment.
The principle: 'al-muqallid ma qalladahu illa liyahkuma bi-madhhabihi' – the one who appointed him only appointed him to judge by his madhhab.
5
Apply the distinction between mujtahid and muqallid judge
All the above applies to a mujtahid judge. For a muqallid judge specifically appointed to implement the Hanafi school, he has no authority to rule otherwise; any contrary ruling is invalid and he is considered dismissed from that case.
The muqallid's mandate is even more restricted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And judgment in an ijtihadi matter contrary to his opinion, whether forgetting his madhhab or intentionally, does not execute according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and by it fatwa is given but not judged. The detail: if a judge judges in an ijtihadi matter contrary to his opinion, forgetting his madhhab, it executes according to Abu Hanifa in one narration, and if intentionally, there are two narrations; according to the two, it does not execute in both cases, and the fatwa is on their statement. And it is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: the fatwa is on his statement; there is disagreement in fatwa, and the correct view in this time is to give fatwa on their statement because the one who intentionally abandons his madhhab does so only for false desire, not for a good intention. As for the forgetful, because the muqallid only appointed him to judge by his madhhab, not by another's madhhab. All this is about the mujtahid judge. As for the muqallid, he was appointed only to judge by the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, so he does not have the right to oppose, thus he is dismissed not with respect to that ruling.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the validity of a judge's judgment when he rules contrary to his own madhhab in an ijtihadi matter. The ruling is stated: according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), such a judgment does not take effect (la yanfudhu), whether the judge was forgetful of his madhhab or intentional. Fatwa is given based on their opinion, but it is not used as a basis for judicial execution (tufta wa la yuqda). The commentary then gives a detailed breakdown: According to Abu Hanifa, if the judge forgot his madhhab, the judgment is effective in one narration; if intentional, there are two narrations. According to the Sahibayn, it is not effective in either case, and the fatwa is on their statement. However, there is disagreement in fatwa: al-Fatawa al-Sughra says the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's statement. The preferred view in the current time is to give fatwa on the Sahibayn's statement, because intentional abandonment of madhhab is done only for base desire, not for a good purpose. For the forgetful, the one who appointed the judge (muqallid) only appointed him to judge by his madhhab, not by another. This applies to a mujtahid judge. As for a muqallid (non-mujtahid) judge, he is appointed specifically to judge by Abu Hanifa's madhhab, so he has no right to oppose it; his judgment would be invalid and he would be considered dismissed from the case.

Key Terms
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fihi): An issue that is open to ijtihad, where different valid opinions exist, as opposed to matters clearly proven by definitive texts.
ناسياً مذهبه (nasiyan madhhabahu): The judge forgets the ruling of his own madhhab and rules based on another madhhab unintentionally.
عامداً (amidan): Intentionally: the judge knowingly rules contrary to his madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is valid only if he forgot his madhhab.
D) It is valid only if he is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that any judgment by a judge that contradicts his own madhhab — whether forgetful or intentional — is void and not executed.

Judgment in Absence of the Defendant (Al-Qada' 'ala al-Gha'ib)
Inter-madhhab dispute on judging the absent defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on judging against an absent defendant, explain the Hanafi proof using the hadith of Ali, and evaluate the opposing schools' arguments based on the hadith of Hind and the principle of bayyinah.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is invalid because hearing both sides is a condition for justice.

This passage addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi school and the majority (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) on whether a judge can rule against a defendant who is absent and unrepresented. The Hanafi school holds that absence prevents a fair hearing, making the judgment invalid. The opponents cite the hadith of Hind and the general principle of burden of proof. We will analyze the proofs and the Hanafi response.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that a judge must not pass judgment against an absent person, nor even for him (i.e., in his favor) without his presence.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad hold that it is permissible to judge against the absent even without a representative, basing on the hadith of Hind and the general rule 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
They cite the hadith where the Prophet told Hind to take from Abu Sufyan's wealth while he was absent, and the principle that evidence is a complete proof regardless of the opponent's presence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response relies on the hadith to Ali requiring hearing both sides, and a rational argument: testimony is probabilistic, and absence creates unresolved possibilities (the absent may challenge witnesses, admit, or claim payment).
The hadith of Hind is interpreted as advice or assistance, not a formal judgment.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: a Hanafi judge cannot rule against an absent defendant unless a representative is present; other schools can. The Hanafi school may allow an exception for maintenance cases with proven need.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقضي على غائب؛ ولا له، وقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد: يجوز القضاء على الغائب، وإن لم يحضر من يقوم مقامه؛ لأنه قضى لهند امرأة أبي سفيان بالنفقة، وأبو سفيان غائب، فقال لها: (خذي من مال أبي سفيان ما يكفيك وولدك). وقوله: «البينة على المدعي» مطلقاً من غير اشتراط حضور خصم؛ ولأنَّ الحجة على التمام، وهي البينة، وهي مبينة كاسمها، فجاز القضاء بها، كما إذا كان الخصم حاضراً. ولنا: قوله لعلي: «لا تقض لأحد الخصمين حتى يسمع كلام الآخر، فإنك إذا سمعت كلام الآخر علمت كيف تقضي»، رواه أحمد وأبو داود والترمذي بمعناه؛ ولأنَّ العمل بالشهادة لقطع المنازعة. فإنَّ الشهادة خبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب، ولا يجوز بناء الحكم على الدليل المحتمل، إلا أنَّ الشرع جعلها ضرورةً قطع المنازعة؛ ولذا إذا حضر الخصم وأقر بالحق فلا حاجة إليها، ولا منازعة بدون الإنكار، ولم يوجد. والبينة بدون حضور الخصم ليس بحجة الاحتمال أن يطعن في الشهود، أو يسلّم الدعوى، أو يدّعي الأداء؛ ولأنه يحتمل أن يقر الخصم، ويحتمل أن ينكر، وأحكامها مختلفة، فإنه بالإقرار يقتصر، وبالبينة يتعدى، فلا يجوز مع الاشتباه. والشاهد عليه أنه قال: «إذا سمعت كلام الآخر علمت كيف تقضي»، فظهر أن العلم بوجه القضاء شرط لصحة القضاء، وأنَّ الجهل بوجه القضاء يمنع القضاء، وهو لا يرتفع إلا بكلامهما. ولا حجةً لهم في حديث هند؛ لأنَّ ذلك الحكم لم يكن قضاء بل كان مشورة أو إعانة لها على أخذ مال زوجها، والشاهد عليه أنَّ تلك المرأة لم تدع الزوجية، ولم تقم البينة، فكان عالماً بأنها امرأة أبي سفيان. وكذا قوله له: « البينة على المدعي»، ليس لهم فيه حجة، بل هو حجة لنا؛ لأنَّ البينة اسم لما يحصل به البيان، ومن البين أنه ليس المراد البيان في حق المدعي، ولا في
He does not judge against an absent person, nor for him. Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said: Judging against the absent is permissible, even if no one representing him is present, because he (the Prophet) judged for Hind, wife of Abu Sufyan, regarding maintenance while Abu Sufyan was absent, and he said to her: 'Take from the wealth of Abu Sufyan what suffices you and your children.' And his statement: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff' absolutely without requiring the presence of an opponent. And because the evidence is complete, which is the testimony, and it is clarifying as its name implies, so judgment by it is permissible as if the opponent were present. For us: His statement to Ali: 'Do not judge for one of the two litigants until you hear the words of the other, for when you hear the words of the other you will know how to judge.' Reported by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Tirmidhi with its meaning. And because the operation of testimony is to end dispute. For testimony is a report that may be true or false, and it is not permissible to base a ruling on probable evidence, except that the law made it a necessity to end dispute. Hence, when the opponent appears and admits the right, there is no need for it, and there is no dispute without denial, and it does not exist. And testimony without the presence of the opponent is not a proof because of the possibility that he may challenge the witnesses, or admit the claim, or claim payment. And because it is possible that the opponent may admit or deny, and the rulings differ; for admission restricts, while testimony extends, so it is not permissible with ambiguity. And the evidence against them is his statement: 'When you hear the words of the other you know how to judge.' Thus it appears that knowledge of the basis of judgment is a condition for the validity of judgment, and ignorance of that basis prevents judgment, and it is only removed by the speech of both. And the hadith of Hind is no proof for them, because that judgment was not a ruling but rather advice or assistance to her in taking her husband's wealth, and evidence for that is that the woman did not claim marriage nor produce testimony, and he knew she was Abu Sufyan's wife. Similarly his statement: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff' is not proof for them, but rather proof for us; because 'al-bayyinah' is a name for what clarifies, and it is clear that the intended clarification is not with respect to the plaintiff, nor in
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (inter-madhhab) regarding judging an absent defendant (al-qada' 'ala al-gha'ib). The Hanafi position states that a judge should not pass judgment against an absent person, nor for him (i.e., in his favor) without his presence. The opposing schools of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad hold that it is permissible to judge against an absent person. The Hanafi text first presents the opponents' evidence: the hadith of Hind bint Utbah, where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told her to take from the wealth of her absent husband Abu Sufyan for her and her children's maintenance. They also cite the general principle that 'the burden of proof is on the plaintiff' without condition of the opponent's presence, and argue that testimony is a complete proof (hujjah) and can be acted upon regardless of the opponent's presence, just as if he were present. The Hanafi response provides two main proofs: the marfu' hadith to Ali (y) do not judge for one of the two litigants until you hear the other, for then you will know how to judge', and a rational argument based on the nature of testimony. The rational argument explains that testimony is a report (khabar) that can be true or false; the law only allows it out of necessity to resolve disputes. However, when the opponent is absent, several possibilities exist: he might challenge the witnesses' credibility, admit the claim, or claim repayment. An admission and testimony have different legal effects (admission restricts the case, testimony extends it). Since these possibilities cannot be resolved without hearing the opponent, the judgment cannot be valid. The hadith also shows that knowledge of the case's full aspects is a condition for correct judgment, which requires hearing both sides. The Hanafi response weakens the hadith of Hind: it was not a formal judgment (qada') but rather advice (mushawarah) or assistance (i'anah) because the woman did not claim marriage nor present evidence, and the Prophet knew she was Abu Sufyan's wife. Similarly, the principle 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' does not support their view because 'bayyinah' means 'that which clarifies', and the clarification is not merely in favor of the plaintiff but also in discerning the truth.

Key Terms
الغائب (al-gha'ib): A person who is absent from the court and not present to defend themselves in a lawsuit.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Clear evidence, usually testimony of witnesses, that establishes the truth in a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof against judging an absent defendant?
A) The hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff.'
B) The hadith: 'Do not judge for one of the two litigants until you hear the other.'
C) The example of Hind and Abu Sufyan.
D) The consensus of all scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires hearing both parties before judgment, making absence a bar to valid adjudication.

Judgment on an Absent Defendant in Hanafi Law
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of judgment in absentia

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi mufta bihi position on judging an absent defendant and explain the proof from the hadith of Ali.

Core Idea
A judge must hear both sides; judging in absentia is invalid unless another judge approves.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah on al-Durr al-Mukhtar addresses a critical procedural rule in Islamic judiciary: can a judge rule against someone not present? The Hanafi school, based on a specific hadith, takes a cautious stance that later authorities refined into the mufta bihi position requiring approval.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on judging an absent defendant
The mufta bihi is that judgment on an absent person is not valid. If a judge renders such a judgment, it is suspended until approved by another judge.
This is the preferred view of al-Tahtawi, Fath al-Qadir, and al-Bazzaziyyah.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Some later Hanafi scholars (e.g., Khwaharzadah) hold that the judgment is enforceable without approval.
This is a minority view, considered marjuh (weak) for fatwa.
3
Examine the Hanafi primary proof
The Prophet said to Ali: 'Do not judge for one of the litigants until you hear the words of the other, for when you hear the other's words you know how to judge.' This establishes that hearing both parties is a condition for a valid judgment.
Narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawood, and al-Tirmidhi.
4
Analyze how the Hanafi response refutes opposing evidence
The opposing evidence (hadith of Hind and 'burden of proof on plaintiff') is reinterpreted: the Hind incident was advice, not a judgment; and 'bayyinah' means clarification to the opponent, not to the judge.
Thus the opposing proofs do not override the explicit command to hear both sides.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the mufta bihi, a Hanafi judge's ruling on an absent defendant is not automatically effective; it must be presented to another judge for approval. Under the opposing view, it would be immediately enforceable.
This protects the absent party's right to be heard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء الحبس وكتاب القاضي ! القاضي

سفيان بالنفقة، وأبو سفيان غائب، فقال لها: (خذي من مال أبي سفيان ما يكفيك وولدك).
وقوله: «البينة على المدعي» مطلقاً من غير اشتراط حضور خصم؛ ولأنَّ الحجة على التمام، وهي البينة، وهي مبينة كاسمها، فجاز القضاء بها، كما إذا كان الخصم حاضراً. ولنا: قوله لعلي: «لا تقض لأحد الخصمين حتى يسمع كلام الآخر، فإنك إذا سمعت كلام الآخر علمت كيف تقضي»، رواه أحمد وأبو داود والترمذي بمعناه؛ ولأنَّ العمل بالشهادة لقطع المنازعة. فإنَّ الشهادة خبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب، ولا يجوز بناء الحكم على الدليل المحتمل، إلا أنَّ الشرع جعلها ضرورةً قطع المنازعة؛ ولذا إذا حضر الخصم وأقر بالحق فلا حاجة إليها، ولا منازعة بدون الإنكار، ولم يوجد. والبينة بدون حضور الخصم ليس بحجة الاحتمال أن يطعن في الشهود، أو يسلّم الدعوى، أو يدّعي الأداء؛ ولأنه يحتمل أن يقر الخصم، ويحتمل أن ينكر، وأحكامها مختلفة، فإنه بالإقرار يقتصر، وبالبينة يتعدى، فلا يجوز مع الاشتباه. والشاهد عليه أنه قال: «إذا سمعت كلام الآخر علمت كيف تقضي»، فظهر أن العلم بوجه القضاء شرط لصحة القضاء، وأنَّ الجهل بوجه القضاء يمنع القضاء، وهو لا يرتفع إلا بكلامهما. ولا حجةً لهم في حديث هند؛ لأنَّ ذلك الحكم لم يكن قضاء بل كان مشورة أو إعانة لها على أخذ مال زوجها والشاهد عليه أنَّ تلك المرأة لم تدع الزوجية، ولم تقم البينة، فكان عالما بأنها امرأة أبي سفيان. وكذا قوله له: «البينة على المدعي»، ليس لهم فيه حجة، بل هو حجة لنا؛ لأنَّ البينة اسم لما يحصل به البيان، ومن البين أنه ليس المراد البيان في حق المدعي، ولا في حق القاضي؛ لأنَّ المدعي عالم بحقه، والقاضي بان له بكلام المدعي، فتعين أن يكون في حق الخصم، هكذا قالوا في هذا المقام. واعلم أنَّ صاحب «الدر المختار» فسر قول الماتن: لا يقضي على غائب ولا له بقوله: أي لا يصح بل لا ينفذ على المفتى به. انتهى.
وقال شيخنا الطَّحْطَاوي: هذا الإضراب لا يتم؛ لأنه إذا كان لا يصح لا يتم، قال في «البحر»: والذي ظهر لي من كلامهم أنَّ المذهب عن أصحابنا عدمُ صحة القضاء على الغائب، وأنَّ القاضي الذي يراه إذا قضى عليه فإنه يتوقف على الإمضاء؛ لأنَّ الاختلاف في نفس القضاء، وما عدا هذا من الأقوال من تصرفات المشايخ. انتهى كلام الطحطاوي.
وقال في «المنح»: اختلف التصحيح في نفاذه، فقيل: لا ينفذ، وقيل: ينفذ، ورجح الأوّل في «فتح القدير»، وأنه لا بُدَّ من إمضاء قاض آخر؛ لأن الاختلاف في نفس القضاء. وفي «البزازية» من القضاة قال الإمامُ ظهير الدين الله في نفاذ القضاء على الغائب روايتان، ونحن نفتي بعدم النفاذ، كيلا يتطرقوا إلى إبطال مذهب أصحابنا. انتهى. والقائل بأنّ الفتوى على النفاذِ خُواهَر زَادَه الله له، وفي «البحر» نقلا عن «منية المفتي»: القضاء على الغائب بلا خصم فيه روايتان، ويفتى بعدم النفاذ، وقيل: إن رآه قاض فقضى به ينفذ. انتهى.
Judgment on the absent defendant. The Prophet said to Hind: 'Take from Abu Sufyan's wealth what suffices you and your child,' and Abu Sufyan was absent. And His statement: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff' is absolute without requiring the presence of the opponent, because proof is complete through evidence, which is clarifying, so judgment by it is permissible as if the opponent were present. But our argument: His statement to Ali: 'Do not judge for one of the litigants until you hear the words of the other, for when you hear the other's words you know how to judge'—narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawood, and al-Tirmidhi with similar meaning. And because action based on testimony is to cut off dispute. Testimony is a report that may be true or false, and it is not permissible to base a ruling on a probable proof, except that the Law made it a necessity to cut off dispute. Hence, when the opponent is present and admits the right, there is no need for it; and there is no dispute without denial. Evidence without the presence of the opponent is not a proof due to the possibility that he might impugn the witnesses, admit the claim, or claim payment. And because it is possible that the opponent admits or denies, and their rulings differ: by admission it is limited, by evidence it is extended, so it is not permissible with ambiguity. The witness to this is his statement: 'When you hear the other's words you know how to judge,' so it is clear that knowledge of the basis of judgment is a condition for the validity of judgment, and ignorance of the basis of judgment prevents judgment, and it is only removed by hearing both. And the hadith of Hind is no proof for them, because that ruling was not a judgment but rather advice or assistance for her to take her husband's wealth, and the witness to this is that the woman did not claim marriage nor produce evidence, so he knew that she was Abu Sufyan's wife. Similarly his statement: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff' is not a proof for them but rather a proof for us, because 'al-bayyinah' is the name for what clarifies, and it is clear that clarification is not intended with respect to the plaintiff nor with respect to the judge, because the plaintiff knows his right and the judge becomes clear through the plaintiff's words, so it must be with respect to the opponent. Thus they said in this regard. Know that the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar interpreted the author's statement 'Do not judge for an absent person nor against him' as: 'It is not valid, rather it is not enforceable according to the fatwa.' End. And our teacher al-Tahtawi said: This interruption is not valid, because if it is not valid it cannot be enforced. He said in al-Bahr: What appears to me from their words is that the school of our companions is that judgment on an absent person is not valid, and that the judge who rules accordingly requires approval, because the difference is in the judgment itself; other statements are mere opinions of scholars. End of al-Tahtawi's words. And it is said in al-Minh: There is disagreement on its enforcement: some say it is not enforced, some say it is enforced, and the first is preferred in Fath al-Qadir, and it must be approved by another judge because the difference is in the judgment itself. And in al-Bazzaziyyah from the judges: Imam Zahir al-Din said that there are two transmissions on enforcing judgment on an absent person, and we give fatwa of non-enforcement to prevent deviation from our school. End. And the one who says the fatwa is on enforcement is Khwaharzadah. And in al-Bahr quoting from Munyat al-Mufti: Judgment on an absent person without an opponent has two transmissions, and the fatwa is on non-enforcement; it is said that if a judge so rules and passes judgment, it is enforced. End.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity and enforceability of a judge's ruling against a defendant who is absent from court. The author begins by presenting an objection: the opposing argument claims that the Prophet's instruction to Hind (to take from Abu Sufyan's wealth while he was absent) and the principle 'the burden of proof is on the plaintiff' imply that judgment can be given without the presence of the defendant. The Hanafi response refutes this: the hadith of Hind was a directive, not a judicial ruling, and the phrase 'al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' refers to clarifying the truth to the opponent, not to the judge. The core Hanafi proof is the Prophet's command to Ali: 'Do not judge for one of the litigants until you hear the words of the other,' which establishes that hearing both parties is a condition for sound judgment. The text then navigates later Hanafi authorities. Al-Tahtawi explains that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that judgment on an absent person is not valid and if rendered, it requires approval (imda') from another judge. Al-Minh reports a disagreement on enforcement: some say it is not enforced, others say it is. Fath al-Qadir prefers the first view, requiring another judge's confirmation. Al-Bazzaziyyah quotes Imam Zahir al-Din al-Din stating two transmissions, with the fatwa given against enforcement to protect the school's integrity. Khwaharzadah is noted as supporting enforcement, but the majority and the preferred fatwa are against it. The passage concludes that the judge who rules in absentia needs to have his judgment approved; if it is approved, it may be enforced. This careful treatment reflects the Hanafi school's commitment to procedural fairness and the avoidance of potential injustice.

Key Terms
القضاء على الغائب (al-qaḍā' 'alā al-ghā'ib): Judgment passed by a judge against a defendant who is not present in court.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically witness testimony, that clarifies the truth.
الإمضاء (al-imḍā'): Approval or ratification of a previous judgment by another judge to make it enforceable.
المفتى به (al-muftā bihī): The relied-upon opinion that is given as fatwa, the operative ruling of the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school, what happens if a judge passes judgment on an absent defendant?
A) The judgment is immediately valid and enforceable.
B) The judgment is null and void with no effect.
C) The judgment requires approval (imda') from another judge to be enforceable.
D) The judgment becomes valid only if the absent defendant later accepts it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a judge cannot issue a valid judgment against an absent defendant; any such ruling must be approved by another judge to be enforceable.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The General Rule on Judging an Absent Defendant and the Hiding Exception
Ruling on judgment against an absent defendant with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on judgment against an absent defendant, identify the one scenario where it is permissible by consensus, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on its enforcement.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is forbidden unless he hides and appoints an agent.

Judging someone who is not in court seems procedurally unfair, as the absent cannot defend themselves. Hanafi law therefore establishes a strong presumption against such judgments. However, there are exceptions—most notably when a defendant deliberately hides to avoid justice. In that case, the judge may force him to appoint an agent and then proceed. This lesson unpacks the basic ruling and its key exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Locate the opening phrase: 'ليس لقاض أن يقضي على غائب إلا بحضرة نائبه' – a judge may not give judgment against an absent person except in the presence of his representative. This is the default rule.
The representative must be one of three types: actual agent, legal executor, or constructive representative.
2
Recognize the three scenarios of absence
The text lists three scenarios: (1) absent by hiding (in his house) – permissible by consensus; (2) present in the city but absent from court – impermissible by consensus (except Shafi'i); (3) absent from the city – area of disagreement. Focus on the first scenario as the only consensus exception.
The hiding scenario is treated as a form of constructive presence through an agent.
3
Analyze the hiding exception
When the defendant hides and refuses to attend, the judge sends deputies to his house and announces at his door, compelling him to appoint an agent. Once appointed, judgment is given based on the agent's testimony. This is considered judgment against the absent but with valid representation.
This is the only case where judgment against the absent is permissible unanimously among Hanafis.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on enforcement
If a judge who deems it permissible (e.g., a non-Hanafi) actually passes such a judgment, the Hanafi view is that it does not take effect unless another judge endorses it. The relied-upon fatwa (from 'Majma' al-Fatawa') says it does not take effect.
Abu Yusuf allowed judging with evidence even after confession, but the primary view requires a second endorsement.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i (in one of his two opinions) permits judgment against an absent defendant who is present in the city. The Hanafi school rejects this, holding that the absent must have the opportunity to defend himself.
The practical difference: a Hanafi judge will not give judgment, while a Shafi'i judge following that opinion would.

Source Text

كتاب القضاء / الحبس وكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي
على غائب إلا بحضرة نائبه حقيقة كالوكيل، أو شرعاً كوصي القاضي، أو حكماً بأن كان ما يُدعى على الغائب سبباً لِمَا يُدعى على الحاضر
وفي «مجمع الفتاوى» عن «المنتقى»: إنه لا ينفذ وعليه الفتوى. انتهى. فالحاصل أنه لا خلاف عندنا في عدم جواز القضاء على الغائب، وإنما الخلاف في أنه لو قضى به من يرى جوازه هل ينفد بدون تنفيذ ، أو لا بد من إمضاء قاض آخر، وحال الترجيح ما علمت آنفاً.
[1] قوله : على غائب ؛ قال في «المعدن»: المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه : ١. في وجه يجوز القضاء على الغائب اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا اختفى الخصم في بيته ولا يحضر مجلس القاضي، وبعث أمناءه إلى داره ونوادي على باب داره ، ينصب وكيلاً ثم يقضي على وجه الوكيل، ويكون ذلك قضاءً على الغائب. كذا في «الذخيرة»، وغيره. ٢. وفي وجه : لا يجوز اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا كان ظاهراً في المصر، غائباً عن المجلس إلا على أحد قولي الشافعي له. ٣. وفي وجه : خلاف، وهو إذا كان غائباً عن البلد. انتهى.
فظهر أنَّ المراد على غائب عن البلد، فكان غائباً عن المجلس بالطَّريقِ الأولى، ولو أقام المدعي البينة على خصم حاضر ، وزكّيت بينته، ثمَّ غاب المدعى عليه لا يقضي عليه حتى يحضر هو، أو من يقوم مقامه فيقضى عليه بتلك البينة من غير إعادتها، وكذا إذا غاب قبل التزكية.
ولو أقر عند الحاكم فغاب قبل أن يقضي عليه قضى عليه وهو غائب ؛ لأنَّ له أن يظهر البينة فيبطل به دون الإقرار، وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يقضي بالبينة أيضاً وهذا أرفقُ إلى الناس على ما قال السَّرَخْسِي له ، والتقييد بقوله : على غائب اتفاقي ؛ لأنه لا يقضي لغائب أيضاً. كما صرحوا به.
[2] قوله : إلا بحضرة نائبه ... الخ ؛ تفصيلُه : أَنَّ مَن يقوم مقام الغائب : إما أن يكون بإنابته كالوكيل والوصي ومتولي الوقف.
كما إذا ادعى داراً على رجل أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، فأقام البينة على ذي اليد، فإنَّ القاضي يقضي بهذه البينة على الحاضر والغائب جميعاً حتى لو حضر الغائب وأنكر لا يلتفت إلى إنكاره.
٢. أو بإنابة الشرع ، كالوصي من جهة القاضي، كما إذا كان المدعى عليه ميتاً وله ولد صغير قد نصب له وصيّاً.
٣. أو من حيث الحكم بأن يكون ما يدعيه على الغائب سبباً لما يدعيه على الحاضر، وهو على نوعين :
أحدهما : أن يكون ما يدعيه على الحاضر والغائب واحداً، مثل أن يدعي دار شخص، وأنكر ذو اليد، وادعى أنها ملكه، وأقام الخارج البينة أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، أو ادّعى في دارٍ في يد شخص شفعة ؛ لأن ذا اليد اشتراها من فلان. وقال ذو اليد : الدار داري، لم اشترها من أحد ، فأقام المدعي البينة أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، أو ادّعى على شخص ديناً على أنه كفيل عن الغائب بأمره ، فأقر الحاضر بالكفالة، وأنكر الدين، فأقام المدعي البينة أن له على الغائب ألف درهم، تقبل بينته في هذه الصور كلّها، ويثبت الحقُّ على الغائب والحاضر، حتى إذا حضر الغائبُ لزمه، ولا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة. والثاني: أن يكون ما يدعيه عليهما شيئين، مثل أن يدعي القاذف أنه عبد فلان ، فيجب عليه أربعون سوطاً، فأقام المقذوف البينة أن مولاه الغائب قد أعتقه فيجب عليه ثمانون سوطاً. أو قال المشهود عليه : الشاهدان ،عبدان، فأقام المدعي البينة أنَّ مولاهما أعتقهما، وهو يملكهما فإن بينته تقبل، ويثبت العنقُ على الغائب ؛ لأن الحقين كشيء واحد، إذ لا تنفك أحدهما . عن الآخر؛ لأنَّ ولاية الشهادةِ لا ينفك عن الحرّ، وحد الحر لا ينفك عن الأحرار، وزيادة البسط في «التبيين»، و«البحر»(۳).
[1] قوله : من فلان الغائب ؛ زادَ في «الفتح» (۳) : وهو يملكها ؛ لأنَّ مجرَّدَ الشراء لا
Book of Judicial Judgment / Imprisonment and the Judge's Letter to Judge. [Judgment is not given] against an absent person except in the presence of his representative: actually like an agent, or legally like the judge's executor, or constructively such that what is claimed against the absent is a cause for what is claimed against the present. And in 'Majma' al-Fatawa' from 'al-Muntaqa': It does not take effect, and upon it is the fatwa. End. So the conclusion is that there is no disagreement among us regarding the impermissibility of judgment against the absent; rather the disagreement is that if one who deems it permissible judges, does it take effect without implementation, or must it be endorsed by another judge? And the state of preponderance is as you previously learned. [1] His statement: 'against an absent' – said in 'al-Ma'din': The issue has three aspects: 1. There is a way in which judgment against the absent is permissible by consensus, which is when the opponent hides in his house and does not attend the judge's court, and the judge sends his deputies to his house and announces at the door of his house, and he appoints an agent, then he judges upon [the testimony of] the agent, and that is judgment against the absent. Thus in 'al-Dhakhira' and others. 2. And there is a way in which it is not permissible by consensus, which is when he is present in the city but absent from the court – except according to one of the two opinions of al-Shafi'i. 3. And there is a way of disagreement, which is when he is absent from the city. End. So it becomes clear that what is meant is absent from the city, so a fortiori absent from the court. And if the plaintiff presents evidence against a present opponent and his evidence is examined for reliability, then the defendant absents himself, judgment is not passed against him until he attends or someone who stands in his place, then judgment is passed against him with that same evidence without repeating it. Similarly, if he absents himself before examination. And if he confesses before the judge and then absents himself before judgment is passed against him, judgment is passed against him while absent, because he has the ability to present evidence that would invalidate it, unlike confession. And from Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – that he also judges with evidence, and this is more convenient for people, as al-Sarakhsi said. And the restriction by his statement 'against an absent' is agreed upon because judgment is not given for an absent person either, as they explicitly stated. [2] His statement: 'except in the presence of his representative' etc.; its detail: the one who stands in the place of the absent is either by his appointment like the agent, executor, and administrator of a waqf. As if someone claims a house from a man that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent, and presents evidence against the possessor, then the judge judges with this evidence against both the present and the absent, such that if the absent appears and denies, his denial is not heeded. 2. Or by appointment of the law, like the executor from the judge's side, as when the defendant is dead and has a young child for whom an executor has been appointed. 3. Or constructively such that what he claims against the absent is a cause for what he claims against the present, and this is of two types: One: that what he claims against the present and the absent is the same, such as claiming a house from a person, and the possessor denies and claims it is his property, and the outsider presents evidence that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent; or claims pre-emption in a house in someone's hand because the possessor bought it from so-and-so; and the possessor says: the house is mine, I did not buy it from anyone; then the plaintiff presents evidence that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent; or claims a debt against a person as a surety for the absent by his order, and the present admits the suretyship but denies the debt, then the plaintiff presents evidence that the absent owes him a thousand dirhams. His evidence is accepted in all these cases, and the right is established against both the absent and the present, such that if the absent appears, it binds him, and there is no need to repeat the evidence. The second: that what he claims against them are two things, such as the defamer claims that he is the slave of so-and-so, so forty lashes are due; then the victim presents evidence that his master who is absent has emancipated him, so eighty lashes are due. Or the one against whom testimony is given says: the two witnesses are slaves; then the plaintiff presents evidence that their master emancipated them and he owns them. Then his evidence is accepted, and emancipation is established against the absent because the two rights are like one thing, since one is not detached from the other; because the authority of testimony is not detached from freedom, and the limit for a free person is not detached from free persons. And further elaboration is in 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Bahr'. [1] His statement: 'from so-and-so who is absent' – added in 'al-Fath': and he owns it, because mere purchase does not [imply ownership].
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on judicial judgment deals with the ruling on giving judgment against an absent defendant. The basic rule is that a judge cannot pass a binding verdict against an absent person unless a representative of the absent is present in court. The representative can be of three types: (1) an actual agent appointed by the absent person himself, (2) a legal representative such as an executor (wasi) appointed by the judge for a deceased person or a minor, or (3) a constructive representative, which occurs when the claim against the absent is directly connected to the claim against the present party. The text then clarifies that among Hanafis there is unanimous agreement that judgment against an absent person is impermissible in principle. However, if a judge who holds it permissible (like some Shafi'is) actually passes such a judgment, the Hanafi view is that it does not take effect unless another judge endorses it; this is the relied-upon fatwa. The discussion then breaks down the scenarios regarding absence into three categories: first, by consensus judgment can be given if the defendant is hiding and refuses to come to court (by having him appoint an agent); second, by consensus it cannot be given if the defendant is present in the city but merely absent from the court (except in the Shafi'i view); third, there is disagreement when the defendant is absent from the city entirely. The text further explains that if a plaintiff presents evidence and the defendant later absents himself, judgment is postponed until the defendant or his representative appears. However, if the defendant confesses and then absents, judgment can be given immediately because a confession cannot be retracted. Abu Yusuf allows judgment based on evidence as well, which is considered more practical. The passage then details the three types of representation with examples. For instance, if a claim involves a property purchased from an absent person, evidence can be used to establish the right against both the present possessor and the absent seller. Similarly, in cases where the claim against the absent is a cause for a different claim against the present (like defamation where the absent's emancipation affects the penalty), the evidence is accepted and the absent is bound upon his return.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment passed by a judge against a defendant who is not present in the court, without his representative.
نائب (na'ib): A representative who stands in place of the absent defendant in court, either by appointment, legal mandate, or constructive relationship.
سبباً لما يدعى على الحاضر (sababan lima yud'a 'ala al-hadir): A scenario where the claim against the absent party is the cause or basis for a related claim against the present party, such that proving one proves the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which scenario allows judgment against an absent defendant by consensus among Hanafi scholars?
A) The defendant is present in the city but absent from the court
B) The defendant is hiding in his house and refuses to attend
C) The defendant is absent from the city entirely
D) The defendant has died with no heir

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent defendant is generally prohibited in Hanafi law, but if the defendant deliberately hides, the judge may force him to appoint an agent and then proceed.

Qada — Judiciary

Types of Representatives and Constructive Claims in Absentia Judgments
Detailed expansion on types of representatives and constructive claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of representatives that allow judgment against an absent defendant, and apply the constructive representation rule to identify when a claim against an absent party can be adjudicated based on a connected claim against a present party.

Core Idea
A representative can be actual, legal, or constructive; constructive representation links the absent's liability to a present claim.

The general rule requires a representative for the absent defendant. But what counts as a representative? The text identifies three categories: someone appointed by the absent himself, someone appointed by the judge legally, and a constructive representative where the absent's involvement is indirect. This lesson dives into the third category—constructive representation—where the claim against the absent is the cause of the claim against the present party. Understanding this mechanism is crucial for complex litigation involving absent owners, sureties, and linked rights.

Guided Path
1
List the three types of representatives
From the text: (1) حقيقة (actual): agent (wakil), executor (wasi), administrator of waqf; (2) شرعاً (legal): executor appointed by the judge for a deceased or minor; (3) حكماً (constructive): when the claim against the absent is a cause for the claim against the present.
The first two are straightforward; the third requires careful analysis.
2
Identify the two subtypes of constructive representation
The text divides constructive representation into two: (a) the claim against the present and absent is the same (e.g., ownership of a house bought from the absent); (b) the claims are different but inseparably linked (e.g., emancipation of a slave affects the defamation penalty).
In both subtypes, evidence against the present party simultaneously establishes the right against the absent party.
3
Work through the examples for subtype (a)
Example: A claims a house from B (possessor). B denies. A proves that B bought the house from C (absent). The evidence is accepted against both B and C. If C later appears and denies, his denial is ignored. Other examples include pre-emption and surety cases.
The key is that the claim against the absent is the very basis of the claim against the present.
4
Work through the examples for subtype (b)
Example: A defamer claims he is a slave of C (absent), so only 40 lashes are due. The victim proves that C (absent) had emancipated the defamer, making him free, so 80 lashes are due. Similarly, if witnesses are claimed to be slaves, and the plaintiff proves they were emancipated by their master (absent). In both, the absent's liability is established indirectly.
The two rights (e.g., freedom and penalty) are considered inseparable.
5
Determine the effect on evidence and burden
In all these cases, the evidence is accepted once, and it binds the absent upon his return. There is no need to repeat the evidence. This is based on the principle that the claim against the absent is the cause (سبب) of the claim against the present.
This is a significant procedural concession that prevents multiplicity of trials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء / الحبس وكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي
على غائب إلا بحضرة نائبه حقيقة كالوكيل، أو شرعاً كوصي القاضي، أو حكماً بأن كان ما يُدعى على الغائب سبباً لِمَا يُدعى على الحاضر
وفي «مجمع الفتاوى» عن «المنتقى»: إنه لا ينفذ وعليه الفتوى. انتهى. فالحاصل أنه لا خلاف عندنا في عدم جواز القضاء على الغائب، وإنما الخلاف في أنه لو قضى به من يرى جوازه هل ينفد بدون تنفيذ ، أو لا بد من إمضاء قاض آخر، وحال الترجيح ما علمت آنفاً.
[1] قوله : على غائب ؛ قال في «المعدن»: المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه : ١. في وجه يجوز القضاء على الغائب اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا اختفى الخصم في بيته ولا يحضر مجلس القاضي، وبعث أمناءه إلى داره ونوادي على باب داره ، ينصب وكيلاً ثم يقضي على وجه الوكيل، ويكون ذلك قضاءً على الغائب. كذا في «الذخيرة»، وغيره. ٢. وفي وجه : لا يجوز اتفاقاً، وهو ما إذا كان ظاهراً في المصر، غائباً عن المجلس إلا على أحد قولي الشافعي له. ٣. وفي وجه : خلاف، وهو إذا كان غائباً عن البلد. انتهى.
فظهر أنَّ المراد على غائب عن البلد، فكان غائباً عن المجلس بالطَّريقِ الأولى، ولو أقام المدعي البينة على خصم حاضر ، وزكّيت بينته، ثمَّ غاب المدعى عليه لا يقضي عليه حتى يحضر هو، أو من يقوم مقامه فيقضى عليه بتلك البينة من غير إعادتها، وكذا إذا غاب قبل التزكية.
ولو أقر عند الحاكم فغاب قبل أن يقضي عليه قضى عليه وهو غائب ؛ لأنَّ له أن يظهر البينة فيبطل به دون الإقرار، وعن أبي يوسف له أنه يقضي بالبينة أيضاً وهذا أرفقُ إلى الناس على ما قال السَّرَخْسِي له ، والتقييد بقوله : على غائب اتفاقي ؛ لأنه لا يقضي لغائب أيضاً. كما صرحوا به.
[2] قوله : إلا بحضرة نائبه ... الخ ؛ تفصيلُه : أَنَّ مَن يقوم مقام الغائب : إما أن يكون بإنابته كالوكيل والوصي ومتولي الوقف.
كما إذا ادعى داراً على رجل أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، فأقام البينة على ذي اليد، فإنَّ القاضي يقضي بهذه البينة على الحاضر والغائب جميعاً حتى لو حضر الغائب وأنكر لا يلتفت إلى إنكاره.
٢. أو بإنابة الشرع ، كالوصي من جهة القاضي، كما إذا كان المدعى عليه ميتاً وله ولد صغير قد نصب له وصيّاً.
٣. أو من حيث الحكم بأن يكون ما يدعيه على الغائب سبباً لما يدعيه على الحاضر، وهو على نوعين :
أحدهما : أن يكون ما يدعيه على الحاضر والغائب واحداً، مثل أن يدعي دار شخص، وأنكر ذو اليد، وادعى أنها ملكه، وأقام الخارج البينة أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، أو ادّعى في دارٍ في يد شخص شفعة ؛ لأن ذا اليد اشتراها من فلان. وقال ذو اليد : الدار داري، لم اشترها من أحد ، فأقام المدعي البينة أنه اشتراها من فلان الغائب، أو ادّعى على شخص ديناً على أنه كفيل عن الغائب بأمره ، فأقر الحاضر بالكفالة، وأنكر الدين، فأقام المدعي البينة أن له على الغائب ألف درهم، تقبل بينته في هذه الصور كلّها، ويثبت الحقُّ على الغائب والحاضر، حتى إذا حضر الغائبُ لزمه، ولا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة. والثاني: أن يكون ما يدعيه عليهما شيئين، مثل أن يدعي القاذف أنه عبد فلان ، فيجب عليه أربعون سوطاً، فأقام المقذوف البينة أن مولاه الغائب قد أعتقه فيجب عليه ثمانون سوطاً. أو قال المشهود عليه : الشاهدان ،عبدان، فأقام المدعي البينة أنَّ مولاهما أعتقهما، وهو يملكهما فإن بينته تقبل، ويثبت العنقُ على الغائب ؛ لأن الحقين كشيء واحد، إذ لا تنفك أحدهما . عن الآخر؛ لأنَّ ولاية الشهادةِ لا ينفك عن الحرّ، وحد الحر لا ينفك عن الأحرار، وزيادة البسط في «التبيين»، و«البحر»(۳).
[1] قوله : من فلان الغائب ؛ زادَ في «الفتح» (۳) : وهو يملكها ؛ لأنَّ مجرَّدَ الشراء لا
Book of Judicial Judgment / Imprisonment and the Judge's Letter to Judge. [Judgment is not given] against an absent person except in the presence of his representative: actually like an agent, or legally like the judge's executor, or constructively such that what is claimed against the absent is a cause for what is claimed against the present. And in 'Majma' al-Fatawa' from 'al-Muntaqa': It does not take effect, and upon it is the fatwa. End. So the conclusion is that there is no disagreement among us regarding the impermissibility of judgment against the absent; rather the disagreement is that if one who deems it permissible judges, does it take effect without implementation, or must it be endorsed by another judge? And the state of preponderance is as you previously learned. [1] His statement: 'against an absent' – said in 'al-Ma'din': The issue has three aspects: 1. There is a way in which judgment against the absent is permissible by consensus, which is when the opponent hides in his house and does not attend the judge's court, and the judge sends his deputies to his house and announces at the door of his house, and he appoints an agent, then he judges upon [the testimony of] the agent, and that is judgment against the absent. Thus in 'al-Dhakhira' and others. 2. And there is a way in which it is not permissible by consensus, which is when he is present in the city but absent from the court – except according to one of the two opinions of al-Shafi'i. 3. And there is a way of disagreement, which is when he is absent from the city. End. So it becomes clear that what is meant is absent from the city, so a fortiori absent from the court. And if the plaintiff presents evidence against a present opponent and his evidence is examined for reliability, then the defendant absents himself, judgment is not passed against him until he attends or someone who stands in his place, then judgment is passed against him with that same evidence without repeating it. Similarly, if he absents himself before examination. And if he confesses before the judge and then absents himself before judgment is passed against him, judgment is passed against him while absent, because he has the ability to present evidence that would invalidate it, unlike confession. And from Abu Yusuf – may Allah have mercy on him – that he also judges with evidence, and this is more convenient for people, as al-Sarakhsi said. And the restriction by his statement 'against an absent' is agreed upon because judgment is not given for an absent person either, as they explicitly stated. [2] His statement: 'except in the presence of his representative' etc.; its detail: the one who stands in the place of the absent is either by his appointment like the agent, executor, and administrator of a waqf. As if someone claims a house from a man that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent, and presents evidence against the possessor, then the judge judges with this evidence against both the present and the absent, such that if the absent appears and denies, his denial is not heeded. 2. Or by appointment of the law, like the executor from the judge's side, as when the defendant is dead and has a young child for whom an executor has been appointed. 3. Or constructively such that what he claims against the absent is a cause for what he claims against the present, and this is of two types: One: that what he claims against the present and the absent is the same, such as claiming a house from a person, and the possessor denies and claims it is his property, and the outsider presents evidence that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent; or claims pre-emption in a house in someone's hand because the possessor bought it from so-and-so; and the possessor says: the house is mine, I did not buy it from anyone; then the plaintiff presents evidence that he bought it from so-and-so who is absent; or claims a debt against a person as a surety for the absent by his order, and the present admits the suretyship but denies the debt, then the plaintiff presents evidence that the absent owes him a thousand dirhams. His evidence is accepted in all these cases, and the right is established against both the absent and the present, such that if the absent appears, it binds him, and there is no need to repeat the evidence. The second: that what he claims against them are two things, such as the defamer claims that he is the slave of so-and-so, so forty lashes are due; then the victim presents evidence that his master who is absent has emancipated him, so eighty lashes are due. Or the one against whom testimony is given says: the two witnesses are slaves; then the plaintiff presents evidence that their master emancipated them and he owns them. Then his evidence is accepted, and emancipation is established against the absent because the two rights are like one thing, since one is not detached from the other; because the authority of testimony is not detached from freedom, and the limit for a free person is not detached from free persons. And further elaboration is in 'al-Tabyin' and 'al-Bahr'. [1] His statement: 'from so-and-so who is absent' – added in 'al-Fath': and he owns it, because mere purchase does not [imply ownership].
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on judicial judgment deals with the ruling on giving judgment against an absent defendant. The basic rule is that a judge cannot pass a binding verdict against an absent person unless a representative of the absent is present in court. The representative can be of three types: (1) an actual agent appointed by the absent person himself, (2) a legal representative such as an executor (wasi) appointed by the judge for a deceased person or a minor, or (3) a constructive representative, which occurs when the claim against the absent is directly connected to the claim against the present party. The text then clarifies that among Hanafis there is unanimous agreement that judgment against an absent person is impermissible in principle. However, if a judge who holds it permissible (like some Shafi'is) actually passes such a judgment, the Hanafi view is that it does not take effect unless another judge endorses it; this is the relied-upon fatwa. The discussion then breaks down the scenarios regarding absence into three categories: first, by consensus judgment can be given if the defendant is hiding and refuses to come to court (by having him appoint an agent); second, by consensus it cannot be given if the defendant is present in the city but merely absent from the court (except in the Shafi'i view); third, there is disagreement when the defendant is absent from the city entirely. The text further explains that if a plaintiff presents evidence and the defendant later absents himself, judgment is postponed until the defendant or his representative appears. However, if the defendant confesses and then absents, judgment can be given immediately because a confession cannot be retracted. Abu Yusuf allows judgment based on evidence as well, which is considered more practical. The passage then details the three types of representation with examples. For instance, if a claim involves a property purchased from an absent person, evidence can be used to establish the right against both the present possessor and the absent seller. Similarly, in cases where the claim against the absent is a cause for a different claim against the present (like defamation where the absent's emancipation affects the penalty), the evidence is accepted and the absent is bound upon his return.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment passed by a judge against a defendant who is not present in the court, without his representative.
نائب (na'ib): A representative who stands in place of the absent defendant in court, either by appointment, legal mandate, or constructive relationship.
سبباً لما يدعى على الحاضر (sababan lima yud'a 'ala al-hadir): A scenario where the claim against the absent party is the cause or basis for a related claim against the present party, such that proving one proves the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, in which of the following cases does constructive representation (حكماً) apply?
A) A plaintiff claims a debt from a present defendant who is a surety for an absent person, and proves the debt.
B) A plaintiff claims a house from a present defendant who inherited it from an absent relative.
C) A plaintiff claims a tort from a present defendant who is the brother of the absent wrongdoer.
D) A plaintiff claims ownership of a car from a present defendant who rented it from an absent owner.

Key Takeaway
Constructive representation allows judgment against an absent party when his liability is the direct cause of the claim against the present party, preventing multiplicity of litigation.

Accepting Evidence Against an Absent Party: The Rule of Sabab vs Shart
Explains when a judge accepts evidence against an absent party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence against an absent party and distinguish between a cause (sabab) and a condition (shart) in qada.

Core Idea
Evidence against an absent is accepted only for a cause, not a condition, unless the condition does not nullify the absent's right.

In Hanafi qada, claims involving an absent party require careful analysis of the link between the present and absent. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between a cause (sabab) — which directly produces a legal effect — and a condition (shart) — which does not. You will learn the general rule, its application in typical scenarios, and the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a condition can ever suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its basis
Start by recognizing that the judge accepts evidence against the absent party only when the claim against the present is based on a cause (sabab) that directly links to the absent. The present acts as a representative (na'ib) of the absent due to this causal link.
The key word is 'sabab' — a direct causative link.
2
Examine the two types of claims accepted
Read the scenarios: (1) the claim against present and absent is one thing (e.g., purchase from absent); (2) the claims are two things but inseparable (e.g., slavery and emancipation affecting punishment). In both, evidence is accepted because the right is interdependent.
Note the example of slander: if the slanderer is a slave, the punishment is different if he was freed; the emancipation is a cause for the increased penalty.
3
Distinguish sabab from shart using the Hanafi reasoning
The correct position (al-sahih) is that evidence is not accepted for a condition (shart) because a condition is merely a prerequisite, not the operative cause. The present cannot represent the absent for something that does not directly cause the claim.
The slave's emancipation conditioned on divorce is an example of shart — the divorce is a condition, not a cause.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi
Some later scholars (Fakhr al-Islam, al-Uzjandi) argued that a condition is like a cause because the claim depends on it. The majority position rejects this, but makes an exception: if the condition does not nullify the absent's right (e.g., suspending divorce on an entry), it may be accepted.
This exception is narrow: the condition must not negate the absent's existing right.
