إبطال حق الغائب ، أما إذا لم يكن كما إذا علّق طلاق امرأتِهِ بدخول زيدٍ في الدَّارِ تُقْبَل.
As when a person claims a house from a man, alleging that he bought it from an absent person (Fulan al-gha'ib), and produces evidence against the possessor: the judge rules by this evidence against both the present and absent parties, such that if the absent party appears and denies, his denial is not considered. Or by appointment of the law, like a guardian appointed by the judge, as when the defendant is dead and has a young son for whom a guardian has been appointed. Or by way of the ruling, that what he claims against the absent party is a cause (sabab) for what he claims against the present party. This is of two types: First: that what he claims against the present and absent is one thing, like claiming a house from a person, and the possessor denies and claims it is his own, and the outsider produces evidence that he bought it from the absent person, or claims pre-emption in a house in someone's hand because the possessor bought it from someone. And the possessor says: 'The house is mine, I did not buy it from anyone,' then the plaintiff produces evidence that he bought it from the absent person, or claims a debt from a person as a guarantor for the absent by his order, so the present admits the guarantee but denies the debt, then the plaintiff produces evidence that he has a thousand dirhams against the absent; his evidence is accepted in all these scenarios, and the right is established against the absent and present, so that when the absent appears, it is binding on him, and he does not need to repeat the evidence. And the second: that what he claims against them are two things, like a slanderer (qadhif) claims that he is the slave of so-and-so, so forty lashes are due, and the victim (maqdhouf) produces evidence that his master (the absent) has emancipated him, so eighty lashes become due. Or the one against whom testimony is given says: 'The two witnesses are slaves,' and the plaintiff produces evidence that their master has emancipated them and he owns them; then his evidence is accepted, and emancipation is established against the absent, because the two rights are like one thing, since one is inseparable from the other, because the capacity for testimony is inseparable from freedom, and the limit of freedom is inseparable from free persons. And further detail is in al-Tabyin and al-Bahr. Saying: 'From the absent person' — it is added in al-Fath: 'and he owns it', because mere purchase does not establish ownership due to the possibility that it belonged to someone other than the seller and he is a fuduli (unauthorized agent). Saying: 'and the correct (al-sahih)' — this is in contrast to what is reported in al-Fath and others from some later scholars like Fakhr al-Islam and al-Uzjandi, that they gave fatwa that the present person is appointed as a representative, so the condition (shart) in their view is like a cause, because the plaintiff's claim depends on the condition just as it depends on the cause. 'If it is a condition, it is not valid' — meaning: if what he claims against the absent is a condition for what he claims against the present, as when a slave claims against his master that he conditioned his emancipation upon his divorcing Zaid's wife, and he produces evidence of the divorce in the absence of Zaid; the scholars differed, and the correct is that it is not accepted, and it is only accepted in the case of a cause (sabab) and not a condition, because the cause is the origin relative to the effect, so the present person acts as a representative of the owner of the cause, who is the absent, like an agent, but not so in the case of a condition. And the judge does not rule against the absent in the case of a condition if it involves nullifying the right of the absent; but if not, as when he conditioned the divorce of his wife upon the entry of Zaid into the house, it is accepted.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from a Hanafi fiqh commentary, explaining the conditions under which a judge accepts evidence in cases involving an absent party. The author begins by presenting a scenario where a plaintiff claims a house, alleging purchase from an absent person, and produces evidence against the present possessor. The judge rules based on this evidence against both present and absent parties, and if the absent later denies, his denial is disregarded. This is because the present person effectively represents the absent in the claim. The text then outlines two types of claims: first, where the claim against present and absent is the same (e.g., purchase from absent, pre-emption, debt guarantee); second, where the claims are different but interdependent (e.g., slave status and emancipation affecting punishment). In both types, the evidence is accepted and the right is established against the absent. The commentary then discusses a key distinction between a 'cause' (sabab) and a 'condition' (shart). The correct Hanafi position is that evidence is accepted only when the claim is based on a cause, not a condition. Some later scholars equated condition with cause, but the relied-upon view rejects that, because a cause directly produces the effect, whereas a condition is merely a prerequisite. However, if the condition does not nullify the absent's right, it may be accepted, as in the example of suspending divorce on a future event. Within Hanafi madhhab, the correct position is that of Abu Hanifa (supported by al-Sadr al-Shahid and others), while the dissenting view was held by Fakhr al-Islam and al-Uzjandi. The authority weight of this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in the foundational texts.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause that directly produces a legal effect; in this context, the link between the claim against the present and absent.
شرط (shart): A condition that must exist for a ruling to apply but does not directly produce it.
غائب (gha'ib): The absent party against whom the claim is indirectly made, who is not present in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a judge accept evidence in a case involving an absent party?
A) When the claim against the present is based on a condition (shart) of the absent.
B) When the claim against the present is based on a cause (sabab) of the absent.
C) Only when the absent party is present in court.
D) When the claim is for a fixed amount of money.

Key Takeaway
Evidence against an absent party is accepted only for a cause (sabab), not a condition (shart), unless the condition does not nullify the absent's right.

The Ruling on a Judge Lending Orphan's Property
States the ruling on a judge lending orphan's wealth with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (hukm) on a judge lending orphan's money and identify the conditions that must be met for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Lending orphan's money to trustworthy persons is recommended, not obligatory, and requires documentation.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary explains the ruling when a judge must preserve an orphan's wealth. Due to the judge's many responsibilities, he cannot safeguard it personally, so he may entrust it to others. The preferred method is lending (iqrad) because it guarantees the orphan's property, unlike a deposit (wadi'ah) which is a trust. However, this is not always permissible; specific conditions must be met. Let's explore the exact ruling and its prerequisites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Hukm Level
Determine what action the judge is permitted to take and whether it is obligatory, recommended, or merely permissible.
The commentary uses 'yustahabb' (recommended), indicating sunnah, not wajib.
2
Understand the Rationale
Why is lending preferred over deposit?
A loan is guaranteed (damanat); a deposit is an amanah (trust) not guaranteed.
3
Extract Conditions for the Borrower
To whom may the judge lend?
Must be known for diyanah (religiosity) and amanah (trustworthiness), and also solvent (mali') and of good conduct.
4
Examine Procedural Requirements
What must the judge do to protect the orphan's property?
Write down the loan (mention of the right) and preserve the record due to risk of forgetfulness.
5
Identify Restrictive Conditions
When does the judge lack authority to lend?
If there is a guardian (wasi) for the orphan, even if appointed by the judge, lending is impermissible. Also, if a more capable person (ghalabah) exists, the judge cannot lend.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقرض مال اليتيم، ويكتب ذِكْرَ الحق. قوله: ويقرض؛ يعني يستحب للقاضي الإقراض؛ لأنه لكثرة أشغاله لا يمكنه أن يباشر الحفظ بنفسه، فلا بد له من الدفع لغيره، والدفع بالقرض أنظر لليتيم ونحوه، لكونه مضموناً، والوديعة أمانة، ولا يقرض إلا من يعرفه بالديانة والأمانة، ويكتب عليه ذلك ويحفظه خوف النسيان؛ لكثرة أشغاله. وفي «البناية» معزياً إلى تاج الشريعة: «يقرض القاضي من الثقات، والثقة المليء الحسن المعاملة، وفي «الأقضية»: إنما يملك القاضي الإقراض إذ لم يحصل غلبة لليتيم، أما إذا وجد فلا يملكه، هكذا روي عن محمد. وينبغي أن يشترط لجواز إقراض القاضي عدم وصي اليتيم، فإن كان له وصي، ولو منصوب القاضي لم يجز؛ لأنه من التصرف في ماله، وهو ممنوع عنه مع وجود
And he (the judge) lends from the orphan's wealth, and writes a mention of the right. Commentary: 'He lends' means it is recommended (mustahabb) for the judge to lend, because due to his many occupations he cannot personally preserve it, so he must give it to someone else; giving as a loan is more prudent for the orphan etc. because it is guaranteed, while a deposit is a trust. He only lends to one whom he knows to be religious and trustworthy, and writes it down and preserves it for fear of forgetfulness due to his many occupations. In al-Binaya, citing Taj al-Shari'ah: 'The judge lends from trustworthy persons, the trustworthy one being solvent and of good conduct.' And in al-Aqdiyyah: 'The judge only has the authority to lend if no one more dominant for the orphan [is available]; if there is, he does not have that authority, as related from Muhammad. And it is required for the permissibility of the judge lending that there be no guardian of the orphan; if there is a guardian, even if appointed by the judge, it is not permissible, because it is a disposal of his wealth, and he is prevented from it when there is' (text cut off).
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the permissibility and procedure for a judge to lend money from an orphan's estate. The text is from a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqaya. The matn states: 'And he (the judge) lends from the orphan's wealth, and writes a mention of the right.' The commentator explains that this is a recommendation (istihbab), not an obligation. The reason is the judge's busy schedule; he cannot personally safeguard the orphan's wealth, so he must entrust it to someone else. Lending (iqrad) is chosen over deposit (wadi'ah) because a loan is guaranteed (damanat), ensuring the orphan's property is not lost. The judge must only lend to individuals known for their religiosity and trustworthiness, and he must document the transaction to avoid forgetfulness. The commentary then cites al-Binaya, quoting Taj al-Shari'ah, that the judge should lend to trustworthy persons who are solvent (mali') and of good conduct. Another source, al-Aqdiyyah, states that the judge has authority to lend only if there is no person more capable (ghalabah) for the orphan's benefit; if such a person exists, the judge cannot lend. This is related from Imam Muhammad. Finally, a condition is added: the judge may only lend if there is no guardian (wasi) of the orphan. If a guardian exists, even one appointed by the judge, the judge cannot lend, as it would be an unauthorized disposal of the orphan's property. The text cuts off before completing the condition. This ruling belongs to the later fatawa (mutakhkhirin) tradition, as it draws on al-Binaya and al-Aqdiyyah.

Key Terms
إقراض (iqrad): Lending money to someone with the contract that it will be returned; here, the judge lends orphan's wealth.
يتيم (yatim): A minor child whose father has died; the orphan's property is managed by a guardian or judge.
وصي (wasi): A guardian appointed over the orphan's property; if present, the judge cannot lend.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a condition for the judge to lend orphan's property?
A) The judge must personally safeguard the property if possible.
B) The borrower must be known for religiosity and trustworthiness.
C) A guardian (wasi) must be consulted before lending.
D) The loan must be interest-bearing to preserve the orphan's wealth.

Key Takeaway
A judge may lend orphan's money only as a recommended option, under strict conditions including the borrower's trustworthiness, documentation, and the absence of a guardian or more capable person.

Accepting Evidence for a Condition vs. Cause in Absentia
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence acceptance for condition vs cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a condition (shart) and a cause (sabab) in Hanafi procedural law and apply the ruling that evidence for a condition is not accepted against an absent defendant when it invalidates their right.

Core Idea
Evidence is accepted for a cause, not a condition, because the present witness acts as an agent for the absent cause-owner.

This lesson explores a procedural nuance in Hanafi qada: when a plaintiff brings a witness to prove a claim against an absent defendant, the admissibility of that evidence depends on whether the claim involves a cause (sabab) or a condition (shart). The correct view (al-sahih) distinguishes them based on agency, leading to different procedural outcomes. Understanding this distinction safeguards the rights of absent parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that the dispute is about accepting evidence from a present witness to prove a claim against an absent defendant. The claim may relate to a cause (sabab) or a condition (shart).
The passage gives the example of a slave claiming his emancipation was conditioned on Zayd divorcing his wife.
2
Define cause (sabab) and condition (shart) in Hanafi jurisprudence
A cause is the direct origin of the legal effect (e.g., sale causes ownership). A condition is an external event upon which the effect is suspended but does not directly produce it (e.g., a promise conditional on an event).
In procedure, a cause creates a nexus; the present party can be treated as an agent for the absent cause-owner.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the correct view
Because the cause is the origin, the present person is like a deputy (wakil) for the absent party. For a condition, there is no such agency, so the absent party's rights cannot be invalidated based on the witness's testimony alone.
The text states: 'al-sabab asl... fa al-hadir na'ib 'an sahib al-sabab.'
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement and the preferred view
Some later scholars (Fakhr al-Islam, al-Uzjandi) argued that a condition is like a cause because the plaintiff's claim depends on it. However, the correct view (al-sahih) rejects this, preserving the absent party's right to be heard.
The tarjih is in favor of non-acceptance for conditions to protect the absent person.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان شرطاً لا يصح، ويقرض مال اليتيم، ويكتبُ ذِكْرَ الحق (فإن كان شرطاً لا يصح ) : أي إن كان ما يدَّعي على الغائب شرط يدعي على الحاضر، كما إذا ادعى عبد على مولاه أَنه علق عتقه بتطليق زيدٍ زوجته، وأقام بينةً على التطليق بغيبة زيدٍ، اختلف فيه المشايخ والصحيح أنَّه لا يُقْبَلُ، وإنما يقبلُ في السَّبب دونَ الشَّرطِ ؛ لأنَّ السَّبب أصل بالنسبة إلى المسبب فيكون الحاضر نائباً عن صاحب السبب، وهو الغائب كالوكيل، ولا كذلك إذا كان شرطاً، وإنما لا يقضي عن الغائب في صورةِ الشَّرط إذا كان فيه إبطال حق الغائب ، أما إذا لم يكن كما إذا علّق طلاق امرأتِهِ بدخول زيدٍ في الدَّارِ تُقْبَل. [1] قوله : والصحيح؛ هذا مقابل ما حكاه في «الفتح»(۲) ، وغيره : عن بعض المتأخرين كفخر الإسلام الله والأوزجنْدِي له أنهم أفتوا فيه بانتصاب الحاضر خصماً، فالشرط عـ عندهم كالسبب ؛ لأنَّ دعوى المدعي كما يتوقف على السبب كذلك يتوقف على الشرط.
Then if it is a condition, it is not valid. And the judge lends the orphan's property and writes a mention of the right. (Then if it is a condition, it is not valid): That is, if what is claimed against the absent person is a condition claimed against the present person, such as when a slave claims against his master that he suspended his emancipation upon Zayd divorcing his wife, and he establishes evidence of the divorce in Zayd's absence. The scholars differed about it, and the correct view is that it is not accepted. It is only accepted for the cause, not for the condition, because the cause is an origin relative to the effect, so the present person acts as a deputy for the owner of the cause—the absent person—like an agent. It is not the same if it is a condition. It is not ruled for the absent person in the case of a condition if it invalidates the absent person's right. But if not, as when he suspends his wife's divorce upon Zayd's entering the house, it is accepted. [1] His statement: 'and the correct one'—this is in contrast to what was reported in al-Fath and others from some later scholars like Fakhr al-Islam and al-Uzjandi that they gave fatwa that the present person is appointed as a litigant, so the condition for them is like the cause, because the plaintiff's claim depends on the condition just as it depends on the cause.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the acceptance of evidence from a present third party when the defendant is absent. The original ruling states that if the claim involves a condition (shart) rather than a cause (sabab), it is not valid. The commentator clarifies: the distinction is that a cause is the origin of the effect, so the present person acts as an agent for the absent party. For a condition—which is external—there is no such agency. The example given: a slave claims his master suspended his emancipation upon Zayd divorcing his wife. The slave produces evidence of the divorce in Zayd's absence. Since the divorce is a condition (not a cause), the evidence is not accepted. However, if the condition does not invalidate the absent person's right—like a husband suspending divorce on Zayd's entry—then the evidence is accepted. The footnote mentions that some later scholars (Fakhr al-Islam, al-Uzjandi) held that a condition is like a cause, but the correct view (al-sahih) is the opposite.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition external to the act, upon which a legal effect is suspended.
سبب (sabab): A cause that is the origin of the effect, making the present person a deputy.
غائب (gha'ib): The absent defendant in a legal claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (al-sahih), when is evidence from a present person accepted against an absent defendant?
A) Only when it involves a condition
B) Only when it involves a cause
C) Both cause and condition
D) Neither cause nor condition

Key Takeaway
The correct view (al-sahih) in the Hanafi school is that evidence from a present person is accepted only for a cause, not for a condition, when it invalidates the absent defendant's right.

Permissibility of Judge Lending Orphan's Property
Details the recommended lending of orphan's property by the judge with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a judge lending orphan's property and identify the conditions that must be met for the loan to be valid.

Core Idea
A loan is better than a deposit for orphan's property because it is guaranteed.

When a judge is unable to personally safeguard an orphan's property due to his many duties, Islamic law permits a practical solution: lending the property to a trustworthy person. This passage explains why a loan is preferred over a deposit and lays down conditions to protect the orphan's interests. Let us examine the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the judge is doing (lending the orphan's property) and the legal classification (mustahabb/recommended).
The text says 'yustahabb' (it is recommended), not wajib.
2
Understand the rationale
Compare a loan (qard) with a deposit (wadi'a). A loan is guaranteed (daman) so the borrower bears loss, while a deposit is a trust (amana) without liability. Thus, lending is more protective of the orphan's property.
This reasoning is based on the judge's inability to preserve the property himself.
3
List the conditions
Identify the prerequisites: (1) borrower known for religion and trustworthiness, (2) no guardian (wasi) for the orphan, (3) writing down the loan to avoid forgetfulness, (4) no loss to the orphan from the loan.
The existence of a guardian is a preventer (mani).
4
Address the objection
Consider the objection: if both judge and guardian are prohibited from lending, how can the orphan benefit? The solution is that the guardian may lend with the judge's permission, analogous to a waqf trustee.
This shows flexibility while maintaining safeguards.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi texts (e.g., from Muhammad) restrict the judge's permission to cases where no loss occurs. The mufta bihi is permissibility with conditions.
This ikhtilaf does not affect the core ruling but adds a nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : ويقرض ؛ يعني يستحب للقاضي الإقراض ؛ لأنه لكثرة أشغاله لا يمكنه أن يباشر الحفظ بنفسه، فلا بُدَّ له من الدفع لغيره، والدفع بالقرض أنظر لليتيم ونحوه، لكونه مضموناً، والوديعة ،أمانة، ولا يقرض إلا مَن يعرفه بالدِّيانَةِ والأمانة ، ويكتب عليه ذلك ويحفظُهُ خوف النسيان ؛ لكثرة أشغاله. وفي «البناية» معزياً إلى تاج الشريعة : «يقرض القاضي من الثقات، والثقة المليء الحسن المعاملة، وفي «الأقضية»: إنّما يلك القاضي الإقراض إذ لم يحصل غلبة لليتيم، أما إذا وجد فلا يملكه هكذا روي عن . محمد (۳) وينبغي أن يشترط لجواز إقراض القاضي عدم وصي اليتيم، فإن كان له وصي، ولو منصوب القاضي لم يجز ؛ لأنه من التصرف في ماله، وهو ممنوع عنه مع وجودِ وصيّة. كما في «بيوع» «القُنْية))) ، وهو الصحيح. كما في «جامع الفصولين»(۳). كذا في «البحر) يجوز للقاضي إقراض مال اليتيم ؛ لأنه محافظةٌ ، والقاضي قادر على أخذه متى شاء ، ولا يجوز للوصى لليتيم. (۳) فإن قيل : إذا لم يجز الإقرار من القاضي، والوصي ممنوع منه، فامتنع النظر (0) قلنا : أفتى في «وصايا» «الفتاوى الخيرية» (٤) : بأنَّ للوصي إقراض مال اليتيم بأمرِ القاضي أخذاً مما في «وقف» «البحر» عن «القُنْية» من أنّ للمتولي إقراض ما المسجدِ بأمر القاضي، قال: والوصي مثل القيم ، لقولهم : الوصية والوقف أخوان، فلم يمتنع النظر لليتيم بهذه الجهة. (۱) قوله : لأنه محافظة ؛ يعني لأن المال بالقرض يكون محفوظاً مضموناً ، ١ ] والقاضي قد يعجز عن الحفظ بنفسه لكثرة أشغاله ، وبالوديعة وإن حصل الحفظ لكن لم تكن مضمونة بالهلاكِ بخلاف القرض. [٢] قوله : والقاضي ... الخ ؛ دفع دخل مقدَّر ، تقريرُهُ : أَنا نُسَلِّم أَنَّ المالَ بالقرضِ يكون محفوظاً مضموناً، لكن لم يؤمن من التَّوَى لجحود المستقرض، وحاصل الدفع أنَّ القاضي يقدر على أخذ المال من المستقرض ؛ لكونه معلوماً، وبالكتابة يحصل الحفظ ، وينتفي النسيان. [٣]قوله : ولا يجوز ؛ أي إقراض مال اليتيم للموصي، فلو فعل لا يعد خيانة، فلا يعزل به، وكذا ليس له أن يستقرض لنفسه على الأصح ، فلو فعل ثمّ أنفق على اليتيم مدَّة يكون متبرعاً إذا صار ضامناً ، فلا يتخلص ما لم يرفع الأمر إلى الحاكم ويملك الإيداع والبيع نسيئة. كذا في «البحر»
[2] His statement: 'and he lends' means it is recommended for the judge to lend because due to his many occupations he cannot personally preserve it, so he must pay it to someone else. Payment by loan is more considerate for the orphan etc. because it is guaranteed, while a deposit is a trust. He only lends to one he knows to be religious and trustworthy, and he writes that down and preserves it for fear of forgetfulness due to his many occupations. In al-Binaya attributed to Taj al-Shari'a: 'The judge lends from trustworthy people, and the trustworthy person is wealthy of good dealing.' In al-Aqdiyya: 'The judge only has authority to lend if no loss occurs for the orphan; if loss occurs, he does not have authority.' Thus related from Muhammad. It is appropriate to stipulate for the permissibility of the judge lending the absence of a guardian for the orphan; if he has a guardian, even one appointed by the judge, it is not permissible, because it is disposal of his property, and he is prohibited from that in the presence of a will. As in the Buyu of al-Quniya, and it is the correct view. As in Jami' al-Fusulayn. Likewise in al-Bahr: 'It is permissible for the judge to lend the orphan's property because it is preservation, and the judge is able to take it back whenever he wishes, and it is not permissible for the guardian to lend for the orphan.' (3) If it is said: If the judge's lending is not permissible and the guardian is prohibited from it, then consideration for the orphan is blocked? We respond: It was given fatwa in the Wasiyya of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya that the guardian may lend the orphan's property by order of the judge, taking from what is in the Waqf of al-Bahr from al-Quniya that the mutawalli may lend the mosque property by order of the judge. He said: 'The guardian is like the trustee, because they say: Wasiyya and waqf are brothers.' So consideration for the orphan is not blocked through this means. (1) His statement: 'because it is preservation' means that the property by loan is preserved and guaranteed, and the judge may be unable to preserve it himself due to many occupations; by deposit, although preservation is achieved, it is not guaranteed against destruction, unlike a loan. [2] His statement: 'and the judge... etc.' addresses a hypothetical objection: We concede that property by loan is preserved and guaranteed, but it is not safe from loss due to the borrower's denial. The gist of the response: The judge is able to take the property from the borrower because he is known, and by writing, preservation is achieved and forgetfulness is removed. [3] His statement: 'and it is not permissible' means the guardian may not lend the orphan's property; if he does, it is not considered treachery so he is not removed. Likewise, he may not borrow for himself in the correct view. If he does and then spends on the orphan for a period, he becomes a donor when he becomes a guarantor, so he is not free unless he raises the matter to the judge and is given authority to deposit and sell on credit. Thus in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with conditions on the judge lending orphan's property. The rule: it is recommended (mustahabb) for the judge to lend the orphan's property to a trustworthy person because this guarantees the property (daman) unlike a deposit (wadi'a) which is a trust (amana). The judge must know the borrower's integrity. The commentary includes conditions: the judge lacks a guardian for the orphan; if there is a guardian (wasi), the judge cannot lend. If the orphan's property is lent, the judge can recover it. Later, a question arises: if both judge and guardian are prohibited, how can the orphan's interest be served? The answer: the guardian may lend with the judge's permission, analogizing to a mutawalli of a waqf. The footnote explains that the guardian lending his own property to the orphan is invalid; he becomes a guarantor and must seek the judge's permission to be released.

Key Terms
إقراض (iqrad): Lending (qard) of property; transferring ownership for use with obligation to return its equivalent.
وصي (wasi): Guardian appointed by a will or judge to manage the orphan's property.
وديعة (wadi'a): Deposit; property entrusted for safekeeping without guarantee.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; property is assured against loss (e.g., in a loan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the judge recommended to lend orphan's property rather than deposit it?
A) Because the judge can earn profit from the loan
B) Because a loan is guaranteed while a deposit is not
C) Because the orphan's property is cursed and must be disposed of
D) Because the judge must make the property productive for the orphan

Key Takeaway
A judge may lend orphan's property to a trustworthy person when no guardian exists, because a loan provides guarantee, but strict conditions apply.

Ruling on Lending Orphan's Money: Permissibility for Qadi and Prohibition for Wasi and Father
Ruling on lending orphan's money by qadi, wasi, and father with proofs and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on lending orphan's money for the qadi, wasi, and father, and explain the rationale and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the father.

Core Idea
The judge may lend orphan's money for preservation; the guardian may not; the father's ruling is disputed, with the relied-upon view being prohibition.

In Hanafi fiqh, the preservation of an orphan's property is a key concern. Lending the property can be a form of preservation because it creates a guaranteed claim against the borrower. However, not all guardians have the authority to undertake such a transaction. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores the distinctions between the judge (qadi), the appointed guardian (wasi), and the father, each with different levels of authority and liability. We will trace the rulings, the rationale, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the father's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parties and their rulings
State the ruling for qadi (permissible), wasi (prohibited), and father (two narrations, mufta bihi is prohibited).
2
Understand the rationale for qadi's permission
Lending is preservation (hifz) and qadi can retrieve it due to his authority and knowledge.
3
Understand the prohibition for wasi
Wasi cannot retrieve easily, risk of loss; but he may lend by qadi's order.
4
Examine the father's two narrations
One narration treats father like wasi (prohibited) due to limited retrieval ability; another treats him like qadi due to comprehensive authority.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its consequence
The relied-upon view is prohibition; if father lends, he becomes liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز للقاضي إقراض مال اليتيم ؛ لأنه محافظةٌ ، والقاضي قادر على أخذه متى شاء ، ولا يجوز للوصى وصيّة. كما في «بيوع» «القُنْية»، وهو الصحيح. كما في «جامع الفصولين». كذا في «البحر) لليتيم. فإن قيل : إذا لم يجز الإقرار من القاضي، والوصي ممنوع منه، فامتنع النظر لليتيم. قلنا : أفتى في «وصايا» «الفتاوى الخيرية» : بأنَّ للوصي إقراض مال اليتيم بأمرِ القاضي أخذاً مما في «وقف» «البحر» عن «القُنْية» من أنّ للمتولي إقراض مال المسجدِ بأمر القاضي، قال: والوصي مثل القيم ، لقولهم : الوصية والوقف أخوان، فلم يمتنع النظر لليتيم بهذه الجهة.

[١] قوله : لأنه محافظة ؛ يعني لأن المال بالقرض يكون محفوظاً مضموناً ، والقاضي قد يعجز عن الحفظ بنفسه لكثرة أشغاله ، وبالوديعة وإن حصل الحفظ لكن لم تكن مضمونة بالهلاكِ بخلاف القرض.

[٢] قوله : والقاضي ... الخ ؛ دفع دخل مقدَّر ، تقريرُهُ : أَنا نُسَلِّم أَنَّ المالَ بالقرضِ يكون محفوظاً مضموناً، لكن لم يؤمن من التَّوَى لجحود المستقرض، وحاصل الدفع أنَّ القاضي يقدر على أخذ المال من المستقرض ؛ لكونه معلوماً، وبالكتابة يحصل الحفظ ، وينتفي النسيان.

[٣] قوله : ولا يجوز ؛ أي إقراض مال اليتيم للموصي، فلو فعل لا يعد خيانة، فلا يعزل به، وكذا ليس له أن يستقرض لنفسه على الأصح ، فلو فعل ثمّ أنفق على اليتيم مدَّة يكون متبرعاً إذا صار ضامناً ، فلا يتخلص ما لم يرفع الأمر إلى الحاكم ويملك الإيداع والبيع نسيئة. كذا في «البحر»

ثم قال: لعدم قدرته على الأخذ، وكذا الأبُ في الأصح" ، فلو فعل يَضْمَن، وإذا أقرض القاضي كتب في ذلك وثيقة.

[١] قوله : لعدم قدرته ؛ معنى أنّ الحفظ والضمان وإن كان موجودين بالإقراض هاهنا إلا أنَّ مخافة الهلاك مانعة عن الجواز ؛ لعدم كون الوصي قادراً على الأخذ، فإنّه ليس كل قاض يعدل، ولا كل بينة تعدل.

(٢) قوله : في الأصح ؛ لعجزه عن الاستخراج وفي رواية : إنه كالقاضي ؛ لأنَّ ولاية الأب تعمُّ النفس والمال كولاية القاضي، وشفقتُه تمنعه من ترك النظر له، والظاهر أنه يقرضُهُ ممن يأمن جهوده.

قال في «المنح» : وفي الأب روايتان أظهرهما أنه كالوصي، وهو الصحيح، كما في «الخانية»، وفي «الخلاصة» و «خزانة الفتاوى» الصحيح أن الأب كالقاضي، فقد اختلف التصحيح، والمعتمد ما في المتون. انتهى.

وقال العيني له في «شرح الكنز» : قال شمس الأئمة له : في الأب روايتان : أظهرهما أنه ليس له أن يقرض ، وهل يقرضُ لنفسه، فقيل : له ذلك ، وعن الحسن له عن أبي حنيفة له ليس له ذلك. انتهى.
It is permissible for the judge to lend the orphan's property because it is preservation, and the judge is able to take it back whenever he wishes. And it is not permissible for the guardian (wasī) [to lend] as a will (waṣiyya). As in the 'Buyū'' of al-Qunya, and it is the correct [view]. As in Jāmi' al-Fuṣūlayn. Likewise in al-Baḥr. If it is said: When the judge is not permitted to lend, and the guardian is prohibited from it, then consideration for the orphan is prevented. We reply: A fatwa in the 'Waṣāyā' of al-Fatāwā al-Khayriyya states that the guardian may lend the orphan's property by order of the judge, based on what is in the 'Waqf' of al-Baḥr from al-Qunya that the mutawallī (administrator) may lend the property of the mosque by order of the judge. He said: The guardian is like the executor (qayyim) because they say: 'Will and endowment are brothers.' Thus consideration for the orphan is not prevented through this avenue.

[1] His statement: 'because it is preservation' means that the property through lending is preserved and guaranteed, and the judge may be unable to preserve it himself due to his many occupations. And with a deposit (wadī'a), although preservation is achieved, it is not guaranteed against destruction, unlike a loan.

[2] His statement: 'and the judge... etc.' is a rebuttal of an assumed objection. Its explanation: We concede that the property through lending is preserved and guaranteed, but one is not safe from loss due to the denial of the borrower. The gist of the rebuttal is that the judge is able to take the property from the borrower because he is known, and through writing preservation is achieved and forgetfulness is eliminated.

[3] His statement: 'and it is not permissible' means lending the orphan's property to the guardian (muṣī). If he does so, it is not considered betrayal, so he is not removed [from guardianship] due to it. Similarly, he may not borrow for himself according to the most sound view. If he does so, then spends on the orphan for some time, he becomes a voluntary donor (mutabarri') when he becomes a guarantor; thus he is not relieved unless he brings the matter to the judge and gains ownership of deposit and sale on credit. Likewise in al-Baḥr.

Then he said: 'due to his inability to take it back, and likewise the father in the most sound [view]; if he does so he becomes liable (ḍāmin). And when the judge lends, he writes a document for it.'

[1] His statement: 'due to his inability' means that preservation and guarantee, even if present through lending here, yet fear of destruction prevents permissibility because the guardian is not able to take it back, for not every judge is just, nor every evidence is just.

(2) His statement: 'in the most sound [view]' due to his inability to recover it. And in one narration: he is like the judge because the father's authority encompasses both person and property like the authority of the judge, and his compassion prevents him from neglecting consideration for him. And the apparent [view] is that he lends to one from whom he is secure from his efforts.

He said in al-Minaḥ: 'Concerning the father, there are two narrations. The more apparent is that he is like the guardian, and it is the correct [view], as in al-Khāniyya. And in al-Khulāṣa and Khizānat al-Fatāwā, the correct [view] is that the father is like the judge. Thus there is difference in correction (taṣḥīḥ), and the relied-upon [view] is what is in the texts (mutūn).' End.

Al-'Aynī said in his commentary on al-Kanz: Shams al-A'imma said: 'Concerning the father, there are two narrations: the more apparent is that he may not lend. And as for lending to himself, it is said he may do so, and from al-Ḥasan from Abū Ḥanīfa: he may not do so.' End.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discourse on lending orphan's property. It opens by stating the general rule: the judge (qāḍī) may lend orphan's money because it achieves preservation (ḥifẓ) and the judge can retrieve it at any time. The guardian (waṣī) is prohibited from lending because he cannot guarantee retrieval. However, the commentary clarifies that if the guardian lends by order of the judge, it becomes permissible, drawing an analogy with the mutawallī of a mosque lending mosque property by judicial order. The passage then addresses an objection: if both judge and guardian are restricted, then the orphan's interest is neglected. The response cites a fatwa allowing the guardian to lend by judicial order. Next, the text explains the rationale for the judge's permission: lending ensures a guaranteed claim, unlike a deposit. The judge's ability to retrieve is assured because the borrower is known and written records exist. For the guardian, the prohibition remains because he cannot enforce retrieval, and not all judges or evidence are reliable. Finally, the passage discusses the father's position. Two narrations exist: one treating the father like the guardian (prohibited from lending), and another like the judge (permitted). The relied-upon view (al-muʿtamad) is that the father is like the guardian, as stated in the primary texts (mutūn). Al-ʿAynī reports two narrations from Abū Ḥanīfa: the more apparent prohibits the father from lending orphan's money, and regarding lending to himself, there is disagreement.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): The guardian appointed by the testator to manage the orphan's property; prohibited from lending unless by qadi's order.
إقراض (iqrāḍ): Lending property with the obligation of repayment; considered a form of preservation (ḥifẓ) for the judge but not for the guardian.
الأصح (al-aṣaḥḥ): An expression used in Hanafī fiqh to indicate the more correct view among transmitted opinions; here applied to the father's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible and he is not liable.
B) It is prohibited and he becomes liable (ḍāmin).
C) It is prohibited but he is not removed from guardianship.
D) It is permissible only if he writes a document.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the guardian's authority to lend orphan's property is strictly limited; only the judge can lend freely, and the father's position is disputed, with the relied-upon view prohibiting it to avoid risk of loss.

Father's Authority to Lend Orphan's Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father's authority to lend

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on whether a father may lend his minor child's property and explain the basis of the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
In lending an orphan's property, a father is like a guardian, not a judge—so he is liable if he lends.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: can a father lend the property of his minor child? The text compares the father's authority to that of a guardian (wasi) and a judge (qadi). While some narrations grant the father broad power similar to a judge, the relied-upon (mu'tamad) position restricts his authority, equating him with the guardian. Understanding this tarjih is crucial for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
State the central question: May a father lend his minor child's property? Identify the two narrations: (1) father is like a guardian (cannot lend), (2) father is like a judge (can lend).
The issue arises because the father's guardianship is comprehensive, but lending involves risk.
2
Analyze the proofs for each position
The first view reasons by analogy to the guardian: the father lacks ability to extract property. The second view analogizes to the judge: the father's comprehensive authority and affection prevent negligence.
The passage criticizes the judge analogy: not every judge is just, nor every evidence reliable.
3
Determine the relied-upon (mu'tamad) position
The text states that the mu'tamad is what is in the core texts (mutun): the father is like a guardian. This is confirmed by Shams al-Aimmah and al-Ayni.
Despite conflicting tas'hih (correct attributions), the matn's position prevails.
4
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the father lends and the property is lost, according to the first view he is liable; according to the second, he is not liable if he acted prudently.
The difference affects whether the father must compensate the child.
5
Apply the mufta bihi rule
For fatwa, the operative ruling is that the father cannot lend; if he does, he is liable. Exceptions may apply for necessity or personal benefit (disputed).
The father may lend for himself? Some say yes, others no (from Hasan from Abu Hanifa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لعدم قدرته على الأخذ، وكذا الأبُ في الأصح، فلو فعل يَضْمَن، وإذا أقرض القاضي كتب في ذلك وثيقة. قوله : لعدم قدرته ؛ معنى أنّ الحفظ والضمان وإن كان موجودين بالإقراض هاهنا إلا أنَّ مخافة الهلاك مانعة عن الجواز ؛ لعدم كون الوصي قادراً على الأخذ، فإنّه ليس كل قاض يعدل، ولا كل بينة تعدل. قوله : في الأصح ؛ لعجزه عن الاستخراج وفي رواية : إنه كالقاضي ؛ لأنَّ ولاية الأب تعمُّ النفس والمال كولاية القاضي، وشفقتُه تمنعه من ترك النظر له، والظاهر أنه يقرضُهُ ممن يأمن جهوده. قال في «المنح» : وفي الأب روايتان أظهرهما أنه كالوصي، وهو الصحيح، كما في «الخانية»، وفي «الخلاصة» و«خزانة الفتاوى الصحيح أن الأب كالقاضي، فقد اختلف التصحيح، والمعتمد ما في المتون. انتهى. وقال العيني له في «شرح الكنز» : قال شمس الأئمة له : في الأب روايتان : أظهرهما أنه ليس له أن يقرض، وهل يقرضُ لنفسه، فقيل : له ذلك، وعن الحسن له عن أبي حنيفة له ليس له ذلك. انتهى.
Due to his inability to take possession (for safekeeping), and likewise the father in the soundest opinion, so if he does it he is liable. If the judge lends, he writes a document for that. Explanation of 'due to his inability': The meaning is that although custody and guarantee exist through lending here, yet fear of loss prevents permissibility, because the executor is not able to take possession, since not every judge is just, and not every evidence is just. Explanation of 'in the soundest opinion': Due to his inability to extract (the property), and in another narration: he is like a judge, because the father's authority over person and property is comprehensive like the judge's authority, and his affection prevents him from neglecting his interest, and apparently he lends it to one whose evil he can be safe from. He said in al-Munah: Regarding the father there are two narrations, the more apparent of which is that he is like the executor, and that is the correct view as in al-Khaniyyah; and in al-Khulasah and Khizanat al-Fatawa the correct view is that the father is like the judge, thus correct attribution differs, and the relied-upon is what is in the texts. End quote. And al-Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz: Shams al-Aimmah said: Regarding the father there are two narrations: the more apparent is that he is not allowed to lend, and whether he may lend for himself, it was said he may, and from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that he may not. End quote.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the commentary of Al-Wiqayah. It addresses the ruling of a guardian (wasi) lending the property of an orphan. The text begins by stating that if the guardian is unable to physically safeguard the property, lending it to a trustworthy person is permissible to avoid loss, and the guardian would not be liable if he lends to someone he trusts. However, if he lends unnecessarily, he is liable. Then it discusses the father's position: in the soundest opinion (al-ashahh), the father is like the guardian and cannot lend. Another narration (riwayah) says the father is like a judge (qadi) because his authority over the child's person and property is comprehensive. The relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that the father is like the guardian, as stated in the matn (core text). However, there is a difference of opinion: some scholars like the author of Al-Khaniyyah and Al-Khulasah consider the father akin to a judge. The passage ends with a quote from Al-Ayni in Sharh al-Kanz that the manifest opinion is that the father may not lend, but regarding lending for his own benefit, there are two narrations. The text presents these differing views without settling the issue definitively, but indicates the relied-upon view is that the father is like the guardian.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasi): The executor or guardian appointed to manage the property of an orphan or minor.
الأب (al-ab): The biological father, who by default has guardianship over his minor child's person and property.
الأصح (al-ashahh): The soundest or most correct opinion among multiple narrations within the Hanafi school.
المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon position for issuing fatwa, as opposed to other weaker opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon opinion (al-mu'tamad) in this passage, can a father lend the property of his minor child?
A) Yes, like a judge he may lend freely.
B) No, he is like a guardian and cannot lend.
C) Yes, but only for his own benefit.
D) He may lend only if the child is an orphan.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a father cannot lend his minor child's property; he is like a guardian and liable if he does.

Definition and Pillar of Arbitration
Defines arbitration and its pillar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the technical definition of arbitration (tahkim) and identify its pillar (rukn).

Core Idea
Arbitration is the appointment of a judge by consent; its pillar is a valid expression of appointment with acceptance.

Arbitration is a form of dispute resolution that is subordinate to general qada. This lesson clarifies its precise definition and the essential element (rukn) that makes it valid. Understanding this foundation is crucial before learning the conditions that regulate it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic and technical definitions
Read the Persian and Arabic explanations of tahkim. Note that linguistically it means delegating judgment, and technically it means two or more litigants appointing a person to judge between them.
The technical definition is the operative one for fiqh.
2
Extract the pillar (rukn)
The pillar is the expression of appointment (e.g., 'judge between us') coupled with the acceptance of the arbitrator. Without acceptance, the appointment is ineffective.
This is a pillar (rukn), not a condition (shart).
3
Understand the consequence of missing pillar
If the arbitrator does not accept, the appointment is void unless renewed. This shows the consensual nature of arbitration.
This contrasts with a qadi who does not need consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم قوله : باب التحكيم ؛ لما كان التحكيمُ من فروع القضاء، وكان أحط رتبة منه، فإنَّ القاضي يقضي فيما لا يقضي المحكم؛ لاقتصار حكمه على من رضي بحكمه، وعموم ولاية القاضي، أخَّرَه في الذكر، التحكيم في اللغة : بازداشتن ستوررا از ایخه خواهد و حاكم كروايندك كسى را در مال خوليش. كذا في «الصراح». فيقال : حكمت الرجل بالتشديد : فوَّضتُ الحكم إليه. كذا في «المصباح»، وفي الاصطلاح : تولية الخصمين فصاعداً حاكماً يحكم بينهما. وركنه : اللفظ الدال عليه : كاحكم بيننا، أو جعلناك حكماً، أو حكمناك في كذا مع قبول الآخر، فلو لم يقبل لم يجز حكمه إلا بتجديد التحكيم. كذا في «البحر» نقلا عن «المحيط». وشرطه : ١. من جهة المحكم (بالكسر) : العقل لا الحرية، فتحكيم العبد والمكاتب والمأذون صحيح، ولا يشترط الإسلام [فيه]، فصح تحكيمُ ذمي ذميّاً ؛ لأنه أهل للشهادة بين أهل الذمة دون المسلمين، وتحكيمُ المرتد موقوف عند أبي حنيفة، فإن حكم ثم قتل أو لحق بطل، وإن أسلم نفذ، وعندهما : جائز بكل حال. كذا في «المحيط». ٢. ومن جهة المحكم (بالفتح) : صلاحيته للقضاء بكونه أهلاً للشهادة، فلو حكما عبداً أو صبياً أو ذميّاً أو محدوداً في قذف لم يصح. وهو مشروع بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع : أما الكتاب : فقوله تعالى : { وإن خفتم شقاق بينهما فابعثوا حكما من أهله وحكما من أهلها إن يريدا إصلاحا يوفق الله بينهما }، والمراد منه تحكيم الزوجين لاختيار الفرقة. وأما السنة : فما روي عن قتيبة قال : حدثني يزيد وهو ابن المقدام بن شريح
Chapter of Arbitration. Explanation of 'Chapter of Arbitration': Because arbitration is a branch of qada and is lower in rank, for the judge passes judgment in matters that the arbitrator does not, due to the limitation of his judgment to those who consent to his judgment, and the generality of the judge's authority, he (the author) has delayed it in mention. Linguistically, arbitration is ... (Persian explanation). It is said: hakkamtu al-rajul with tashdid means I delegated judgment to him. This is in al-Misbah. And in technical terminology: the appointment by two or more litigants of a person to judge between them. Its pillar (rukn) is the expression indicating it, like 'judge between us', 'we have made you a judge', or 'we have appointed you as judge in such-and-such' with acceptance by the other, for if he does not accept, his judgment is not valid except by renewing the arbitration. This is in al-Bahr quoting al-Muhit. Its condition: 1. Regarding the arbitrator (muhakkam with kasra): sanity, not freedom, so arbitration of a slave, mukatab, or ma'dhun is valid. And Islam is not required in him, so arbitration of a dhimmi for a dhimmi is valid because he is qualified for testimony among dhimmis but not among Muslims. And the arbitration of an apostate is suspended according to Abu Hanifa: if he passes judgment then dies in apostasy or joins enemy territory, it is void; if he returns to Islam, it is effective. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is valid in any case. This is in al-Muhit. 2. Regarding the appointer (muhakkam with fatha): his qualification for qada by being qualified for testimony, so if they appoint a slave, a minor, a dhimmi, or one flogged for slander, it is not valid. And it is legislated by the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma: As for the Book, His statement: 'And if you fear dissension between them, send an arbitrator from his family and an arbitrator from her family; if they desire reconciliation, Allah will reconcile them.' And the intent is arbitration of the spouses by choosing separation. As for the Sunnah: what is narrated from Qutayba who said: Yazid, who is Ibn al-Miqdam ibn Shuraih, narrated to me...
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on arbitration (tahkim). It explains why arbitration is placed after general qada: because it is a branch of qada and inferior in rank. The judge's authority is general, whereas the arbitrator's authority is limited to those who consent. The definition is given linguistically (with a Persian explanation) and technically: 'authorization of two or more litigants of a person to judge between them.' The pillar (rukn) is any expression indicating appointment, like 'judge between us,' with acceptance by the arbitrator. The conditions are divided into two categories: (1) Regarding the arbitrator (al-muhakkam, with kasra on the kaf): he must be sane; freedom is not required, so a slave or mukatab can be appointed. Islam is not a condition per se, but a dhimmi can arbitrate only between dhimmis, not Muslims. There is a special ruling for an apostate: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, his arbitration is suspended (mawquf); if he returns to Islam it is effective, otherwise null. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid in all cases. (2) Regarding the litigant who appoints (al-muhakkam, with fatha on the kaf): he must be qualified to testify, so a child, slave, dhimmi, or one who has been flogged for false accusation cannot appoint. The chapter concludes by citing proofs for the legitimacy of arbitration from the Quran (verse on family arbitration), Sunnah (a hadith transmitted through Qutayba), and Ijma.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-tahkim): Technically: the appointment by two or more litigants of a person to judge between them.
المحكم بالكسر (al-muhakkam bi al-kasr): The arbitrator: the person appointed to judge between the parties.
المحكم بالفتح (al-muhakkam bi al-fath): The appointer: the litigant who appoints the arbitrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum requirement for the pillar (rukn) of arbitration?
A) A written contract signed by both parties
B) An expression of appointment and the arbitrator's acceptance
C) The arbitrator must be a Muslim
D) The dispute must involve money

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is defined as consensual delegation of judgment; its pillar is a clear expression of appointment plus acceptance.

intermediate

Conditions of the Arbitrator (Muhakkam)
Lists conditions for the arbitrator and discusses apostate dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required in the arbitrator (muhakkam bi al-kasr) and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding apostate arbitrators.

Core Idea
An arbitrator must be sane; freedom and Islam are not absolute conditions, but apostate arbitration is suspended per Abu Hanifa.

Not everyone can serve as an arbitrator. The Hanafi school outlines specific conditions for the person appointed (al-muhakkam bi al-kasr). This lesson details those conditions and highlights a notable disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples on the effect of apostasy on arbitration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of sanity
The text explicitly states 'al-'aql' (sanity) as a condition for the arbitrator. Insanity would invalidate the appointment.
This is the only condition explicitly required.
2
Note what is NOT required: freedom
The text says freedom is not a condition, so slaves, mukatab, or ma'dhun may serve as arbitrators.
This contrasts with the appointer condition.
3
Analyze the condition of Islam
Islam is not an absolute condition. A dhimmi can arbitrate between dhimmis, but not between Muslims. The arbitrator for Muslims must be Muslim.
This is a restricted condition based on the parties.
4
Understand the apostate ruling
Abu Hanifa holds that an apostate's arbitration is suspended (mawquf): if he returns to Islam it is effective, if he dies as an apostate it is void. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is valid in all cases.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text

Conditions of the Appointer and Proofs of Arbitration
States condition for appointer and provides proofs of arbitration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the appointer (muhakkam bi al-fath) and identify the proofs of arbitration from the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

Core Idea
The appointer must be qualified to testify; arbitration is legislated by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma.

Just as the arbitrator has conditions, the person appointing must also meet a key condition: they must be legally qualified to testify. This lesson covers that condition and then surveys the textual and consensus-based proofs that establish the legitimacy of arbitration in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the appointer
The text states the appointer must be 'qualified for qada by being qualified for testimony' (ahl al-shahada). This excludes certain categories.
This is a single condition but with documented exceptions.
2
List who cannot appoint a valid arbitrator
The text explicitly mentions: a slave, a minor (sabi), a dhimmi, or one flogged for qadhf (false accusation). Their appointments are invalid.
These are examples of persons not qualified to testify.
3
Recognize the proofs of legislation
The text then states arbitration is legislated by the Quran (verse 4:35), the Sunnah (a hadith from Qutayba), and Ijma (consensus).
The Quran verse is quoted partially; the hadith is referenced but incomplete.
4
Understand the Quranic evidence
The verse commands sending arbitrators from the families of a quarreling couple. The command (fa-ib'athu) indicates recommendation, not obligation.
This is a specific application but used as general proof.

beginner

Arbitration in Hanafi Fiqh: Definition, Pillar, and Conditions
Defines arbitration, its pillar, and conditions for arbitrator and appointing party.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of tahkim, identify its pillar, and list the conditions for both the arbitrator and the appointing party in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Arbitration is a valid judge-appointment contract; the arbitrator need only be sane, but the appointing party must be witness-eligible.

Arbitration (tahkim) is a branch of qada' and a practical tool for dispute resolution. This passage from a Hanafi commentary defines arbitration, explains its single pillar (expression plus acceptance), and sets forth the conditions for the arbitrator (sanity, not freedom or Islam) and for the appointing party (witness eligibility). A key intra-Hanafi difference on the apostate arbitrator is also noted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Grasp that tahkim linguistically means delegation of judgment, and technically is the appointment by two or more litigants of a person to judge between them.
The technical definition emphasizes that arbitration is voluntary and binding only on those who consent.
2
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The sole pillar is the expression of appointment coupled with the arbitrator's acceptance. Without acceptance, the arbitration is invalid unless renewed.
Example: saying 'We appoint you as judge' and the other saying 'I accept'.
3
Distinguish conditions of arbitrator vs. appointing party
For the arbitrator (muhakkam): sanity is required, but not freedom or Islam. For the appointing party (muhakkim): eligibility to be a witness is required.
Thus a slave or dhimmi can be an arbitrator, but a minor cannot.
4
Apply conditions to examples
Test scenarios: two Muslims appointing a dhimmi (invalid for Muslims), two dhimmis appointing a dhimmi (valid), adults appointing a slave (valid if sane).
Appointing a minor or a person convicted of false accusation (qadhf) is invalid.
5
Note the ikhtilaf on the apostate arbitrator
Abu Hanifa suspends the judgment of an apostate pending his return to Islam or death; the Sahibayn consider it immediately valid.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم
[١] قوله : باب التحكيم ؛ لما كان التحكيمُ من فروع القضاء، وكان أحط رتبة منه، فإنَّ القاضي يقضي فيما لا يقضي المحكم؛ لاقتصار حكمه على من رضي بحكمه، وعموم ولاية القاضي، أخَّرَه في الذكر، التحكيم في اللغة : بازداشتن ستوررا از ایخه خواهد و حاكم كروايندك كسى را در مال خوليش. كذا في «الصراح». فيقال : حكمت الرجل بالتشديد : فوَّضتُ الحكم إليه. كذا في «المصباح، وفي الاصطلاح : تولية الخصمين فصاعداً حاكماً يحكم بينهما.
وركنه : اللفظ الدال عليه : كاحكم بيننا ، أو جعلناك حكماً، أو حكمناك في كذا مع قبول الآخر، فلو لم يقبل لم يجز حكمه إلا بتجديد التحكيم. كذا في «البحر» (۲) نقلا عن «المحيط».
وشرطه :
١. من جهة المحكم : - بالكسر - العقل لا الحرية، فتحكيم العبد والمكاتب والمأذون صحيح، أولا يشترط الإسلام [فيه]، فصح تحكيمُ ذمي ذميّاً ؛ لأنه أهل للشهادة بين أهل الذمة دون المسلمين، وتحكيمُ المرتد موقوف عند أبي حنيفة ، فإنّ حكم ثمَّ قتل أو لحق بطل، وإن أسلم نفذ، وعندهما : جائز بكل حال. كذا في «المحيط». ٢. ومن جهة المحكم : - بالفتح - : صلاحيته للقضاء بكونه أهلاً للشهادة ، فلو حكما عبداً أو صبياً أو ذمياً أو محدوداً في قذف لم يصح.
Chapter of arbitration. [1] His words: Chapter of arbitration; since arbitration is a branch of judgment and is of lower rank, because the judge judges what the arbitrator does not judge due to the restriction of his judgment to those who consent to his judgment, and the generality of the judge's authority, he delayed it in mention. Arbitration linguistically: [Persian: restraining a cow from... and making someone a judge over his property]. So in al-Sirah. It is said: hakkamtu al-rajul (with tashdid): I delegated judgment to him. So in al-Misbah. And technically: the appointment of two or more litigants of a judge to judge between them. Its pillar: the expression indicating it, like 'judge between us' or 'we have made you a judge' or 'we have appointed you as judge over this' with the acceptance of the other. If he did not accept, his judgment is not valid unless with renewal of arbitration. So in al-Bahr, quoting from al-Muhit. Its conditions: 1. On the part of the arbitrator (with kasr): sanity, not freedom, so arbitration of a slave, a mukatib, and a ma'dhun (permitted slave) is valid. Also Islam is not a condition [in it], so arbitration of a dhimmi by a dhimmi is valid, because he is eligible for testimony among the dhimmis but not Muslims. And arbitration of an apostate is suspended according to Abu Hanifa: if he then judges and then is killed or joins (the enemy), it is void; if he becomes Muslim, it is executed. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): it is permissible in all cases. So in al-Muhit. 2. On the part of the one who appoints (with fatha): his eligibility for judgment by being eligible for testimony. So if they appoint a slave, a minor, a dhimmi, or one who was subjected to hadd for qadhf, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on arbitration (tahkim) from a Hanafi fiqh commentary. The author first explains why arbitration is placed after the chapter on qada': because it is a branch of judgment and of lower rank. He then gives the linguistic definition of tahkim, citing Persian and Arabic dictionaries, and the technical definition: the appointment by two or more litigants of a person to judge between them. The pillar (rukn) of arbitration is the expression of appointment coupled with acceptance by the appointee. If the appointee does not accept, the arbitration is invalid unless renewed. The conditions are divided into two: conditions relating to the arbitrator (muhakkam, with kasr) and conditions relating to the appointing party (muhakkim, with fatha). For the arbitrator, sanity is required but not freedom or Islam; thus a slave, mukatib, or dhimmi can arbitrate. The case of an apostate arbitrator is disputed: Abu Hanifa suspends his judgment pending his return to Islam or death, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider it immediately valid. For the appointing party, he must be eligible to be a witness, so appointing a slave, minor, dhimmi, or one convicted of false accusation (qadhf) is invalid. These conditions ensure that arbitration mirrors the qualifications of a judge, though with less stringent requirements.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-tahkim): The appointment by two or more litigants of a person to judge between them.
المحكم (بالكسر) (al-muhakkam (bi al-kasr)): The arbitrator appointed by the parties.
المحكم (بالفتح) (al-muhakkim (bi al-fath)): The party who appoints the arbitrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the validity of arbitration?
A) The arbitrator must be sane.
B) The arbitrator must be Muslim.
C) The appointing party must be eligible to be a witness.
D) The arbitrator must accept the appointment.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration in Hanafi fiqh is valid with a sane arbitrator and a witness-eligible appointing party, without requiring freedom or Islam for the arbitrator.

Proofs and Conditions for Arbitration (Tahkim) in Hanafi Fiqh
Presents scriptural evidence and conditions for arbitration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sources of legislation for arbitration, identify the conditions required for a valid arbitrator, and explain why an arbitrator's ruling is binding on the parties.

Core Idea
Arbitration is legislated by Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma; the arbitrator must be eligible like a judge, and his ruling binds the parties.

In this lesson, we delve into the Hanafi rulings on arbitration (tahkim). The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the scriptural proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, then details the conditions for a valid arbitrator and the binding effect of his judgment. Understanding these foundations is essential for distinguishing arbitration from ordinary judgment and for ensuring its proper application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sources of legislation
The passage lists the Quranic verse (4:35), the hadith of Hani becoming Abu Shuraih, and the consensus of the Companions as evidence for the permissibility of arbitration.
The Quranic verse specifically addresses marital disputes, but the Hanafis generalize its application.
2
Extract the conditions for arbitrator eligibility
Note that the arbitrator must be fit to be a judge (ahlan lil-qada), which includes being free, adult, Muslim, with integrity (not subjected to hadd for qadhf). Eligibility is required at both appointment and judgment.
The text states: 'يشترط فيه ما يشترط في القاضي من كونه أهلاً للشهادة'
3
Understand the effect of a change in status
If the arbitrator's status changes (slave freed, minor becomes adult, dhimmi converts) before judgment, the judgment is not executed because eligibility wasn't present at both times.
The passage gives examples: 'فلو حكما عبداً فعتق... ثمَّ حكم لا ينفذ حكمه'
4
Recognize the binding nature of the arbitrator's ruling
The arbitrator's judgment is binding like a judge's, even if the arbitrator is later removed. Once issued, it cannot be rescinded.
The text compares: 'كما أنَّ القاضي إذا حكم ثم عزل فحكمه لازم'
5
Analyze the arbitrator's testimony about confessions
His informing of a confession made during his tenure equals the testimony of two men; after tenure, it equals only one witness, requiring another.
This is due to his authority (wilayah) during tenure.

[١] قوله : وصح تحكيم الخصمين من صلح قاضياً ولزمهما حكمه بالبينة، والنكول، والإقرار، وإخباره بإقرار أحد الخصمين وبعدالة شاهدين حال ولايته. أي صح إخباره بإقرار أحد الخصمين وبعدالة الشاهدين في زمان ولايته؛ لأنَّ إخباره بإقرار حال ولايته قائم مقام شهادة رجلين بخلاف ما إذا أُخْبرَ بعدَ الولاية : لأنه التحق بواحدٍ من الرعايا فلا بُدَّ من الشاهد الآخر.

[٣] قوله : لزمهما حكمه : أي حكم الحكم؛ لأنَّ حكمه صدر عن ولاية عليهما كما أنَّ القاضي إذا حكم ثم عزل فحكمه لازم.
[٤] قوله : وإخباره : أي وصح إخبارُ الحكم بإقرار أحد الخصمين مثلاً إذا قال : فلا بُدَّ من الشاهد الآخر.
And it is legislated by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book: His saying, Exalted: 'And if you fear dissension between the two, send an arbitrator from his family and an arbitrator from her family. If they both desire reconciliation, Allah will cause it between them.' (4:35). The intended meaning is arbitration between spouses for choosing separation. As for the Sunnah: what was narrated from Qutaybah: he said: Yazid – and he is Ibn al-Miqdam ibn Shuraih – narrated to me, from his father al-Miqdam, from his father Shuraih ibn Hani, from his father Hani, that when he came to the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and heard them calling Hani' Abu al-Hakam, the Messenger of Allah ﷺ called him and said: 'Indeed, Allah is the Judge, and to Him belongs judgment. Why do you call yourself Abu al-Hakam?' He said: 'My people, if they dispute about something, they come to me and I judge between them, and both parties are pleased.' He said: 'How good that is. What children do you have?' He said: 'Shuraih, Abdullah, and Muslim.' He said: 'Who is the eldest?' He said: 'Shuraih.' He said: 'Then you are Abu Shuraih.' And he prayed for him and his children.' Narrated by al-Nasa'i. As for consensus: the Companions were unanimous on its permissibility. [1] His words: 'And the arbitration of the litigants is valid from the one who is fit to be a judge, and his judgment is binding on them by evidence, oath-refusal, confession, and his informing of the confession of one of the litigants and of the integrity of two witnesses during his tenure.' That is, his informing of a confession during his tenure is valid because his informing of a confession during his tenure stands in place of the testimony of two men, unlike if he informs after tenure: because he becomes like one of the subjects, so the other witness is necessary. [2] His words: 'from the one who is fit to be a judge' – because the arbitrator is like a judge; the same conditions required for a judge regarding eligibility to testify are required for him. So if they appoint a slave, a minor, or one subjected to hadd for qadhf, it is not valid, as mentioned. Eligibility is required at the time of appointment and at the time of judgment. So if they appoint a slave who then is freed, a minor who then reaches puberty, or a dhimmi who then becomes Muslim, and then he judges, his judgment is not executed. It is also required that the arbitrator be known; so if they appoint the first one to enter the mosque, it is not valid by consensus due to ignorance. [3] His words: 'his judgment is binding on them' – that is, the judgment of the arbitrator; because his judgment issues from authority over them, just as when a judge judges and is then removed, his judgment is binding. [4] His words: 'and his informing' – that is, the informing of the arbitrator of the confession of one of the litigants, for example, when he says: then the other witness is necessary.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage establishes the legitimacy of arbitration (tahkim) through three sources: Quran (the verse on appointing arbitrators in marital disputes), Sunnah (the hadith about Hani becoming Abu Shuraih, which shows the Prophet's approval of his arbitration among his people), and Ijma' (Companion consensus). Then the commentator expands on the statement 'the arbitration of litigants is valid from the one who is fit to be a judge...'. He explains that the arbitrator must possess the same qualifications required for a judge, particularly eligibility to testify. This includes being free from the hadd penalty for qadhf, being adult, etc. Importantly, eligibility must exist at both the time of appointment and the time of judgment; if the arbitrator's status changes (e.g., slave freed, minor becomes adult, dhimmi converts), the judgment does not take effect because the condition was not present at the time of judgment. Also, the arbitrator must be a known person; delegating to an unknown person like 'the first to enter the mosque' is invalid due to ignorance. The binding nature of the arbitrator's ruling is likened to a judge's ruling: once issued, it cannot be rescinded even if the arbitrator is later removed. Finally, the arbitrator's statement about a confession made in his presence during his tenure is equivalent to testimony of two men, but if he reports after his tenure, it is only like one witness, requiring another witness.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal to take an oath; the arbitrator can rule based on a party's refusal to swear.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession; the arbitrator's ruling can be based on a party's confession.
الولاية (al-wilayah): Authority; the arbitrator exercises authority over the parties during his tenure, analogous to a judge's authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if parties appoint a slave as arbitrator, and later the slave is freed before giving judgment?
A) The judgment is valid because the slave became free.
B) The judgment is not executed because eligibility must be present at both appointment and judgment.
C) The judgment is valid if the parties consent.
D) The slave may not judge at all.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is a valid Islamic institution supported by clear proofs; its validity hinges on the arbitrator meeting the same qualifications as a judge at the time of both appointment and judgment.

Validity of Arbitration and Conditions of the Arbitrator
States validity of arbitration and conditions for arbitrator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid arbitration (tahkim) in Hanafi fiqh, identify the proof, and apply the conditions to determine whether a given arbitrator appointment is valid.

Core Idea
Arbitration is valid only if the arbitrator meets the qualifications of a judge (eligible for testimony) and is known to the parties.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi ruling on arbitration. It establishes that two litigants may appoint an arbitrator, and his decision is binding on them regarding evidence, oaths, and confessions. The ruling is supported by a hadith and consensus. Crucially, the arbitrator must satisfy the same conditions as a judge, and these conditions must be present both at the time of appointment and at the time of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is arbitration (tahkim). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih) and binding on the litigants regarding specific matters (evidence, refusal to swear, confession, and the arbitrator's report of a confession or witness integrity during his tenure).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Sunan al-Nasa'i (the story of Abu Shuraih) and the consensus of the Companions. The hadith shows the Prophet's approval of Hani's arbitration among his people.
The verse mentioned in the beginning is not quoted but referenced.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's approval ('How good is this!') indicates permissibility. The consensus of the Sahaba confirms that arbitration is legitimate in Islamic law.
The exact wording of the verse is not given, but the hadith provides explicit approval.
4
List conditions for the arbitrator
The passage enumerates: the arbitrator must be suitable to be a judge (ahliyyat al-shahadah: free, adult, Muslim, just, not slandered); must be known (not unknown); eligibility must exist at both the time of appointment and the time of ruling.
If eligibility is lost between appointment and ruling (e.g., slave freed, child matures), the ruling is not valid unless they are reappointed.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a scenario, test whether the conditions are met. For example, appointing a slave or minor is invalid; appointing an unknown person is invalid due to jahalah (ignorance).
The condition of 'being known' prevents uncertainty in the contract.
