The Book of Judging, Chapter of Arbitration and Judging in Inheritance.  487. It is valid for the two litigants to appoint an arbitrator who is suitable to be a judge, and his ruling is binding upon them regarding evidence, refusal to take an oath, confession, and his informing about the confession of one of the litigants and the integrity of two witnesses during his term of office. (It is valid for the two litigants to appoint an arbitrator who is suitable to be a judge, and his ruling is binding upon them regarding evidence, refusal to take an oath, confession, and his informing about the confession of one of the litigants and the integrity of two witnesses during his term of office): i.e., it is valid for him to inform about the confession of one of the litigants and the integrity of the two witnesses during the time of his term of office; because his informing about a confession during his term of office stands in the place of the testimony of two men, unlike if he informs after the term of office: because he becomes one of the subjects, so the other witness is necessary. 6. Ibn Hani from his father al-Miqdam from his father Shuraih bin Hani from his father Hani that when he came to the Messenger of Allah and heard them calling Hani' Abu al-Hakam, the Messenger of Allah called him and said to him: 'Indeed, Allah is the Judge (al-Hakam), and to Him is Judgement, so why do you call yourself Abu al-Hakam?' He said: 'My people, when they differ in something, they come to me and I judge between them, and both parties are pleased.' He said: 'How good is this! What children do you have?' He said: 'Shuraih, Abdullah, and Muslim.' He said: 'Who is the eldest?' He said: 'Shuraih.' He said: 'Then you are Abu Shuraih,' and he prayed for him and his children.' Narrated by al-Nasa'i. 3. And as for Ijma: the Companions were unanimous on its permissibility. [1] His saying: 'It is valid'; because of the verse and hadith we mentioned; and because they have authority over themselves, so their arbitration is valid, and his ruling is executed upon them because he is like a judge in their case. (2) His saying: 'Who is suitable to be a judge'; because the ruling between them is like a judge, so the conditions required in a judge are required in him, such as being qualified for testimony. So if they appoint a slave, a child, or one who has been lashed for slander, it is not valid, as has passed. Eligibility is required at the time of arbitration and at the time of ruling. So if they appoint a slave who then is freed, a child who then reaches puberty, or a dhimmi who then becomes Muslim, then he rules, his ruling is not executed. It is also required that the arbitrator be known. So if they appoint the first person to enter the mosque, it is not permissible by consensus, due to ignorance (of identity).
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, ruling on the validity of arbitration (tahkim). The matn states that two litigants may appoint an arbitrator who is suitable to be a judge, and his ruling on evidence, refusal to take an oath, confession, and his report of a confession or witness integrity during his tenure is binding. The commentator explains that the arbitrator's report of a confession during his term is like the testimony of two men; after his term, he becomes a private person and his report requires another witness. The proof is provided via the Quranic verse (Allah commands judging with truth), the hadith of Abu Shuraih (where the Prophet changed his kunya from Abu al-Hakam to Abu Shuraih, implicitly approving his arbitration among his people), and the consensus of the Sahaba. The commentator further elaborates that the arbitrator must meet the same conditions as a judge: he must be qualified for testimony (free, adult, Muslim, just, etc.). If the litigants appoint a slave, a child, or one who has been punished for slander, the arbitration is invalid. Eligibility must exist at both the time of appointment and the time of ruling—changes in status (e.g., a slave freed, a child reaching puberty, a dhimmi converting) after appointment require fresh appointment. Additionally, the arbitrator must be known; appointing 'the first person to enter the mosque' is void due to uncertainty.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkim): The appointment of a third party by two litigants to settle their dispute; arbitration.
حكم (hakam): The person appointed to judge between the litigants; the arbitrator.
أهلية الشهادة (ahliyyat al-shahadah): The legal capacity to give testimony; requires being free, adult, Muslim, just, with intact senses.
الجهالة (al-jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty about the identity of the arbitrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two litigants appoint a slave as an arbitrator, what is the ruling?
A) Valid if the slave is just and knowledgeable.
B) Invalid because a slave is not qualified for testimony.
C) Valid only if the slave is freed before ruling.
D) Valid only if both parties consent after appointment.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration (tahkim) is valid only when the arbitrator satisfies the conditions of a judge (ahliyyat al-shahadah) and his identity is known to the parties.

Binding Nature of the Arbitrator's Ruling
This section explains the binding nature of the arbitrator's ruling using an analogy to a judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the arbitrator's ruling is binding upon the parties and apply the analogy to a judge's ruling.

Core Idea
The arbitrator's ruling binds both parties because his delegated authority mirrors a judge's authority.

In Hanafi fiqh, arbitration is a mechanism where parties choose a third party to resolve their dispute. This lesson explores why the arbitrator's ruling becomes binding once issued, drawing a parallel to a judge's ruling. The key principle is that the arbitrator derives authority from the parties' consent, and once exercised, the ruling is final.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the matn phrase: 'His ruling is binding upon them.' Recognize that this is a clear statement of the binding nature.
The phrase 'لزمهما حكمه' directly states the ruling.
2
Understand the Basis
The commentary explains that the arbitrator issues his ruling with authority over the parties, just as a judge does. Identify the phrase 'صدر عن ولاية عليهما' as the basis for the analogy.
Authority (wilayah) is the key concept.
3
Apply the Analogy
The text compares the arbitrator to a judge: 'just as when a judge rules and then is dismissed, his ruling is binding.' Understand that the analogy transfers the judge's binding authority to the arbitrator.
The analogy is explicit in the Arabic: 'كما أن القاضي إذا حكم ثم عزل فحكمه لازم'.
4
Recognize the Limit
Note that the binding nature applies only to the specific dispute. The arbitrator's authority is limited to the case he was appointed for. Withdrawal before ruling is allowed, as mentioned later.
This lesson focuses on the ruling after it is issued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) في «سنن النسائي الكبرى»(٣: ٤٦٦)، وغيرها.

وبخلاف ما إذا أُخبر بأنَّه قد حَكَمَ ؛ لأنه إذا حَكَمَ انعزل ، فلا يُقْبَلُ إِخباره.
[٢]
(ولكل منها أن يرجع قبل حكمه ، ولا يصح حُكْمُ المحكم والمولى لأبويه وولده وعرسه) ، كما لا يصح الشهادة لهؤلاء ) ، (ولا التحكيم في حدٌ وَقَوَدِ) ؛ لهؤلاء"، لأنهما ) لا يَمْلِكان دَمَهُما ؛ ولهذا لا يملكان إباحته
لأحد الخصمين : قد أقررت عندي لهذا بكذا وكذا ، أو قال : قامت عندي عليك بينة بهذا بكذا وكذا، فعدلوا عندي وقد ألزمتك ذلك ، وحكمت به لهذا عليك، وأنكر المقضي عليه ذلك، يقبلُ قول الحكم ، ولا يلتفت إلى إنكار المنكر، وينفذ القضاء عليه ؛ لأنَّ الحكم يملك إنشاء الحكم عليه بذلك، فيملكُ الإقرار كالقاضي، وقد سبق ذكره بخلاف ما إذا أخبر بالحكم ، وقال : كنتُ حكمت عليك لهذا بكذا لا يصدق ولا يقبل. [١] قوله : لأنه إذا حكم ؛ صار معزولاً ، كما سيذكره الشارح ، كما إذا قال
القاضي بعد عزله : حكمت عليك لهذا بكذا ، لا يقبل قوله ؛ لكونه معزولاً.
[3] His saying: 'His ruling is binding upon them'; i.e., the ruling of the arbitrator; because his ruling issues from authority over them, just as when a judge rules and then is dismissed, his ruling is binding. [4] His saying: 'And his informing'; i.e., it is valid for the arbitrator to inform about the confession of one of the litigants, for example when he says: (1) In 'Sunan al-Nasa'i al-Kubra' (3:466), etc. And unlike if he informs that he has ruled; because when he rules he becomes dismissed, so his informing is not accepted. [2] (And each of them may withdraw before his ruling, and the ruling of the arbitrator and the master over his parents, his child, and his spouse is not valid, as the testimony for these is not valid), and (arbitration is not valid in hadd and retaliation); because the two (the litigants) do not own their own lives; hence they cannot allow it. For one of the two litigants: 'He has confessed to me for this for such-and-such', or he says: 'A legal proof has been established with me against you for this for such-and-such, so they (the witnesses) became qualified with me and I have imposed that upon you, and I have ruled by it for this against you,' and the condemned party denies that, the arbitrator's statement is accepted, and the denial of the denier is not heeded, and the judgment is executed against him; because the arbitrator has the power to create the ruling against him thereby, so he possesses the power of confession like the judge, and this has preceded. Unlike if he informs about the ruling and says: 'I ruled against you for this for such-and-such,' he is not believed and not accepted. [1] His saying: 'Because when he rules, he becomes dismissed', as the commentator will mention, just as when the judge after his dismissal says: 'I ruled against you for this for such-and-such,' his statement is not accepted because he is dismissed.

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn concerning arbitration. The first part explains that the arbitrator's ruling is binding on the parties because he derives his authority from their consent, analogous to a judge whose ruling remains valid even after his dismissal. The second part discusses the arbitrator's report: if he informs during his tenure that a party confessed or that witnesses were qualified, his word is accepted because he has the power to create the ruling. In contrast, if he reports after his tenure has ended (after he has ruled), he is considered dismissed and his report is not accepted, similar to a judge after dismissal. The passage then transitions to another ruling (which will be detailed in the next lesson) but also includes a brief mention of withdrawal and prohibitions.

Key Terms
لزوم (luzum): Binding nature; the ruling is legally obligatory to follow.
حكم الحكم (hukm al-hakam): The ruling of the arbitrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The arbitrator derives authority from the parties, like a judge derives authority from the state.
C) The parties cannot withdraw consent after the ruling.
D) The arbitrator has the same powers as a judge in all matters.

Key Takeaway
The arbitrator's ruling is binding upon the parties because his authority to decide, once exercised, is final and analogous to a judge's ruling.

Validity of the Arbitrator's Report
This section focuses on the validity of the arbitrator's report and the distinction between before and after his ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the arbitrator's accepted report during tenure and the rejected report after ruling, and explain the reason based on his dismissal.

Core Idea
The arbitrator's report is accepted only while he holds office; after ruling he is dismissed and his word is like a dismissed judge's.

An arbitrator often communicates facts like confessions or witness qualifications. The Hanafi school holds that his report is accepted without further corroboration during his tenure, because he has the power to create a ruling based on that report. However, once he has issued a ruling, he is considered dismissed and his subsequent report is not accepted. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Define the Report
The arbitrator's report (إخبار الحكم) is his statement about a fact, such as 'the defendant confessed' or 'the witnesses are qualified,' made during the arbitration process.
The text gives examples: confession or witness qualification.
2
State the Rule for Report During Tenure
The commentary says his report is accepted and the denial of the opponent is ignored. The ruling is executed based on his report. Identify the phrase 'يقبل قول الحكم'.
No further evidence is needed because he is the authority.
3
Apply the Analogy to Power to Create Ruling
The reason given: 'because the arbitrator has the power to create the ruling against him thereby, so he possesses the power of confession like the judge.' Understand that since he could have issued a ruling on that fact, his report is equivalent to a judge's acceptance of a claim.
This is the illa for accepting his report.
4
State the Exception: Report After Ruling
If the arbitrator reports after he has already issued a ruling, his report is not accepted. The text says: 'بخلاف ما إذا أخبر بالحكم' and gives the example of saying 'I ruled against you for this.'
He is dismissed after ruling, so his word carries no weight.
5
Understand the Reason
The commentary explains: 'because when he rules, he becomes dismissed.' A dismissed judge's report is also not accepted. The dismissal terminates his authority.
Use the analogy to the judge: 'كما إذا قال القاضي بعد عزله : حكمت عليك لهذا بكذا ، لا يقبل قوله'.

Limits of Arbitration: Withdrawal and Forbidden Subjects
Three rulings on arbitration: revocation, prohibition for relatives, and prohibition for hudud/qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circumstances under which arbitration may be revoked and distinguish the cases in which arbitration is invalid, applying the Hanafi principles of revocable agency and the lack of authority over life and limb.

Core Idea
Arbitration is a revocable agency; it cannot be applied to hudud and qisas because the parties have no authority over their own lives.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text explains three key limitations on arbitration: the right of either party to withdraw before a ruling, the prohibition of an arbitrator judging in favor of close relatives, and the invalidity of arbitration in cases of fixed punishments and retaliation. These rules are grounded in the nature of arbitration as a revocable appointment and the principle that a person cannot delegate authority over matters they do not personally possess.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
Read the passage and list the three separate legal rulings: (1) revocation before ruling, (2) prohibition for relatives, (3) invalidity for hudud/qisas.
2
Understand the nature of arbitration
Analyze why revocation is allowed: the arbitrator is an appointee of the parties, like a deputy (muqallad), and arbitration is a permissible (ja'iz) contract, not a binding one, so one party may revoke.
Compare with partnership, agency, and company—all revocable.
3
Apply the tuhma (suspicion) principle
Examine why an arbitrator cannot judge in favor of his parents, child, or spouse: the same tuhma that invalidates testimony for them applies to arbitration. Note that judging against them is valid because there is no suspicion of bias.
This mirrors the rules of testimony (shahada).
4
Grasp the limit for hudud and qisas
Understand why arbitration is invalid for hudud and qisas: the litigants do not own their own lives, so they cannot authorize another to permit their death. Arbitration is akin to a settlement (sulh), and one cannot settle regarding life and limb.
The same reasoning prohibits arbitration in diyat (blood money) on the 'aqila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولكل منها أن يرجع قبل حكمه ، ولا يصح حُكْمُ المحكم والمولى لأبويه وولده وعرسه) ، كما لا يصح الشهادة لهؤلاء ) ، (ولا التحكيم في حدٌ وَقَوَدِ) ؛ لهؤلاء"، لأنهما ) لا يَمْلِكان دَمَهُما ؛ ولهذا لا يملكان إباحته
[٢) قوله : ولكل منهما أن يرجع قبل حكمه ؛ لأنَّ الحَكَمَ مقلد من جهة الخصمين، فكان لهما عزله قبل أن يحكم بينهما كما أن المقلد من جهة الإمام له أن يعزله قبل أن يحكم بين الناس.
فإن قيل : التحكيمُ قد ثبت بتوافقهما ، فوجب أن لا يعزل بأحدهما. أجيب : بأن التحكيم من الأمور الجائزة دون اللازمة، فيستبد أحدهما بنقضه كالمضاربة والشركة والوكالة [٣] قوله : كما لا يصح الشهادة لهؤلاء ؛ لمكان التهمة، فكذلك لا يصح حكم المحكم والمولى، بخلاف ما إذا حكم عليهم ؛ لأنه تقبل ؛ لشهادته عليهم؛ لانتفاء التهمة.
[٤]قوله : لأنهما ... الخ ؛ تحريره: إن تحكيمهما بمنزلة الصلح بدلالة أنهما توافقا على الرضاء بما يحكم به عليهما، وليس لهما ولاية على دمهما ؛ ولهذا لا يملكان إباحته، وكذا لا يصح التحكيمُ في دِيَةٍ على العاقلة ؛ لأنهما ليس ولاية لهما على
(And each of them may withdraw before his ruling, and the ruling of the arbitrator and the master over his parents, his child, and his spouse is not valid) as the testimony for these is not valid, (and arbitration is not valid in hadd and retaliation); because the two (the litigants) do not own their own lives; hence they cannot allow it. [2) His saying: 'And each of them may withdraw before his ruling'; because the arbitrator is appointed at the direction of the two litigants, so they have the right to dismiss him before he rules between them, just as the appointee of the imam may be dismissed before he rules among the people. If it is said: Arbitration is established by their mutual agreement, so it should not be voided by one of them. The answer: Arbitration is from permissible matters, not necessary ones, so each may independently revoke it, like partnership, company, and agency. [3] His saying: 'As the testimony for these is not valid'; because of suspicion (tuhma), so likewise the ruling of the arbitrator and the master (over relatives) is not valid, unlike if he rules against them because his testimony against them is accepted, due to absence of suspicion. [4] His saying: 'Because the two... etc.'; its summary: Indeed, their arbitration is in the nature of a settlement (sulh), evidenced by their mutual agreement to accept whatever he rules, and they have no authority over their own lives; hence they cannot allow it. Similarly, arbitration is not valid in diyah (blood money) upon the 'aqila (kinsmen), because they have no authority over
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage addresses three sub-rulings: First, either party may revoke the arbitrator's authority before he issues a ruling. This is because arbitration is a revocable appointment (like a proxy or partnership), and the consent of both parties is the basis; one party's withdrawal suffices. Second, an arbitrator (or a master) may not judge in favor of his own parents, children, or spouse, just as his testimony for them is invalid due to suspicion (tuhma). However, it is permissible to judge against them, as there is no suspicion. Third, arbitration is not valid in cases of hadd punishments and qisas (retaliation), because the litigants do not own their own lives; they cannot authorize another to permit their death. This is because arbitration is akin to a settlement (sulh), and one cannot settle regarding life and limb. A similar prohibition applies to diyat on the 'aqila (collective blood money), as the litigants have no authority over that either.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallad): One appointed by others; the arbitrator is appointed by the litigants, like a judge appointed by the imam.
حد وقود (hadd wa qisās): Fixed corporal punishments (hadd) and retaliation (qisas).
تهمة (tuhmah): Suspicion of bias; prevents an arbitrator from judging in favor of close relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is arbitration invalid in cases of hadd and qisas according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the parties cannot afford the costs of arbitration.
B) Because the parties do not own their lives and cannot authorize another to permit their death.
C) Because only a qualified qadi can judge such cases.
D) Because the arbitrator must be a relative of the parties.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is a revocable agency limited to matters over which the parties have authority; it cannot be used for hudud, qisas, or in favor of close relatives.

Arbitration: Revocation, Invalid Judgments, and Exclusions
States rulings on arbitration revocation and invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which an arbitration judgment is invalid according to the Hanafi school and apply the rule of revocation.

Core Idea
Arbitration is revocable before judgment, invalid when the arbitrator is a close relative, and invalid in hudud and qisas.

Arbitration (tahkim) is a valid method of dispute resolution in Islamic law, but its validity is subject to specific conditions. This passage from the Hanafi text outlines three key limitations: the parties may revoke the arbitrator before judgment, the arbitrator may not judge for close relatives, and arbitration is not permissible in cases involving fixed punishments (hudud) or retaliation (qisas). Understanding these rules is essential for properly applying arbitration in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
Extract from the passage the three distinct rules: revocation before judgment, invalidity for relatives, invalidity in hudud/qisas.
The passage explicitly states each.
2
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil) for each
For each ruling, find the reason given: for relatives, it's tuhma (suspicion); for hudud/qisas, it's lack of ownership of blood; for revocation, it's that arbitration is ja'iz (permissible) not lazim (obligatory).
The text provides analogies to agency and partnership.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on hudud/qisas
Note the minority view (Shams al-A'imma, al-Dhakhira) that allows arbitration in qisas and hadd of slander, and compare their evidence (rights of servants) with the majority evidence (no ownership of blood).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a case where two parties appoint an arbitrator for a qisas dispute, determine the validity and outcome according to the mufta bihi position.
The judgment would be invalid; the qadi must decide.

Source Text

[١]
(ولكل منها أن يرجع قبل حكمه ، ولا يصح حُكْمُ المحكم والمولى لأبويه وولده وعرسه) ، كما لا يصح الشهادة لهؤلاء ) ، (ولا التحكيم في حدٌ وَقَوَدِ) ؛ لهؤلاء"، لأنهما ) لا يَمْلِكان دَمَهُما ؛ ولهذا لا يملكان إباحته
لأحد الخصمين : قد أقررت عندي لهذا بكذا وكذا ، أو قال : قامت عندي عليك بينة بهذا بكذا وكذا، فعدلوا عندي وقد ألزمتك ذلك ، وحكمت به لهذا عليك، وأنكر المقضي عليه ذلك، يقبلُ قول الحكم ، ولا يلتفت إلى إنكار المنكر، وينفذ القضاء عليه ؛ لأنَّ الحكم يملك إنشاء الحكم عليه بذلك، فيملكُ الإقرار كالقاضي، وقد سبق ذكره بخلاف ما إذا أخبر بالحكم ، وقال : كنتُ حكمت عليك لهذا بكذا لا يصدق ولا يقبل. [١] قوله : لأنه إذا حكم ؛ صار معزولاً ، كما سيذكره الشارح ، كما إذا قال
القاضي بعد عزله : حكمت عليك لهذا بكذا ، لا يقبل قوله ؛ لكونه معزولاً. [٢) قوله : ولكل منهما أن يرجع قبل حكمه ؛ لأنَّ الحَكَمَ مقلد من جهة الخصمين، فكان لهما عزله قبل أن يحكم بينهما كما أن المقلد من جهة الإمام له أن يعزله قبل أن يحكم بين الناس.
فإن قيل : التحكيمُ قد ثبت بتوافقهما ، فوجب أن لا يعزل بأحدهما. أجيب : بأن التحكيم من الأمور الجائزة دون اللازمة، فيستبد أحدهما بنقضه كالمضاربة والشركة والوكالة [٣] قوله : كما لا يصح الشهادة لهؤلاء ؛ لمكان التهمة، فكذلك لا يصح حكم المحكم والمولى، بخلاف ما إذا حكم عليهم ؛ لأنه تقبل ؛ لشهادته عليهم؛ لانتفاء
التهمة.
[٤]قوله : لأنهما ... الخ ؛ تحريره: إن تحكيمهما بمنزلة الصلح بدلالة أنهما توافقا على الرضاء بما يحكم به عليهما، وليس لهما ولاية على دمهما ؛ ولهذا لا يملكان إباحته، وكذا لا يصح التحكيمُ في دِيَةٍ على العاقلة ؛ لأنهما ليس ولاية لهما على
العاقلة، فلا ينفذ حكم من حكماه على عاقلته، ولا على القاتل ؛ لعدم التزام العاقلة
حكمه. كذا في «التبيين» ، وغيره. وقال شمس الأئمة : من أصحابنا من قال : التحكيم في حد القذف والقصاص جائز، وذكر في «الذخيرة» عن «صلح» «الأصل»: إن التحكيم في القصاص جائز ؛ لأ ؛ لأن الاستيفاء لهما، وهما من حقوق العباد ، فيجوز التحكيم كما في الأموال، وذكر الخصافُ له أنَّ التحكيم لا يجوز في الحدود والقصاص، واختاره القُدوري ، وصاحب «الهداية» (۳)، و«الكنز» (4) ، وغيرهم من أصحاب المتون.
And each of them may revoke before his judgment, and the judgment of the arbitrator and the guardian is not valid for his parents, his child, and his wife, nor is arbitration valid in hadd and qisas. And contrary to when it is informed that he has judged; because when he judged, he becomes removed, so his information is not accepted. [1] (And each of them may revoke before his judgment, and the judgment of the arbitrator and the guardian is not valid for his parents, his child, and his wife), just as testimony for these is not valid, (and arbitration is not valid in hadd and qisas); for these two, because they do not own their blood; therefore they do not own its permission. If one of the two litigants says: I have confessed before him for this one for so much, or he said: There is evidence against you before me for this for so much, and they are upright before me, and I have required that for you, and I have judged for this one against you, and the one against whom judgment was made denies that, the word of the judge is accepted, and the denial of the denier is not heeded, and the judgment is executed upon him; because the judge has the authority to initiate judgment against him by that, so he has the authority of acknowledgment like the qadi, as previously mentioned. Contrary to when he informs of the judgment and says: I had judged against you for this one for so much, he is not believed and is not accepted. [1] His saying: because when he judged, he becomes removed, as the commentator will mention, just as if the qadi after his removal said: I judged against you for this one for so much, his saying is not accepted because he is removed. [2] His saying: And each of them may revoke before his judgment; because the arbitrator is a delegate from the two litigants, so they have the right to remove him before he judges between them, just as the delegate from the imam has the right to remove him before he judges among the people. If it is said: Arbitration is established by their mutual agreement, so it should not be revoked by one of them. The answer: Arbitration is among permissible matters, not obligatory ones, so each one independently may break it, like mudarabah, partnership, and agency. [3] His saying: just as testimony for these is not valid; due to the presence of suspicion, so likewise the judgment of the arbitrator and the guardian is not valid, contrary to when he judges against them; because his testimony against them is accepted; due to the absence of suspicion. [4] His saying: because they two... etc.; its explanation: Their arbitration is like sulh (conciliation) as indicated by their mutual agreement to be satisfied with what the arbitrator judges for them, and they have no authority over their blood; therefore they do not own its permission. Similarly, arbitration is not valid in blood money upon the 'aqilah; because they have no authority over the 'aqilah, so the judgment of one who arbitrates for them does not take effect upon his 'aqilah, nor upon the killer; due to the lack of the 'aqilah's acceptance of his judgment. This is in 'al-Tabyin', etc. And Shams al-A'imma said: Among our companions, some said: Arbitration in slander hadd and qisas is permissible, and it is mentioned in 'al-Dhakhira' from 'Sulh' of 'al-Asl': Arbitration in qisas is permissible; because the execution of qisas is for them, and they are among the rights of the servants, so arbitration is permissible as in property. And al-Khassaf mentioned that arbitration is not permissible in hudud and qisas, and al-Quduri and the author of 'al-Hidayah' (3) and 'al-Kanz' (4) and other authors of texts chose this opinion.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the rules of arbitration (tahkim). It presents several core rulings: First, each party to arbitration may revoke the arbitrator's authority before judgment is issued. Once judgment is issued, the arbitrator becomes removed (ma'zul) and cannot later confirm the judgment. Second, the judgment of an arbitrator who is a close relative (such as a parent, child, or spouse) of one party is invalid, analogous to the prohibition of testifying for close relatives due to suspicion (tuhma). Third, arbitration is not valid in cases involving hadd penalties (fixed punishments like slander) or qisas (retaliation), because the parties do not have ownership of their own blood and thus cannot delegate authority over it. The commentary provides refinements: if the arbitrator announces after judgment, it is accepted as he had the power to initiate it; but if he later reports having judged, it is not accepted. The reason is that once he judges, he is removed. The analogies to the qadi's removal, the permissibility of revocation like in mudarabah or agency, and the exception to testimony when judging against a relative (no suspicion) are explained. Finally, the passage notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: some Hanafi scholars (e.g., Shams al-A'imma, and a report in al-Dhakhira) allow arbitration in qisas and slander hadd because these are rights of the servants, analogous to financial matters, while the majority (al-Khassaf, al-Quduri, al-Marghinani) hold it invalid. The relied-upon position is the majority view (mufta bihi), which is that arbitration in hudud and qisas is invalid.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkim): Arbitration: both parties agree to appoint a third party to settle their dispute; the arbitrator's judgment is binding.
حدّ (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment prescribed by the Shari'a for specific crimes (e.g., zina, qadhf, theft).
قود (qisas): Retaliation: the right of the victim or heirs to demand equal retaliation for murder or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following makes an arbitration judgment invalid according to the passage?
A) Arbitrator is a stranger to both parties.
B) Arbitrator is the father of one party.
C) Arbitrator is a qualified scholar.
D) Both parties consent to the arbitration.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is a revocable contract that is invalid when the arbitrator is a close relative or when the case involves hudud or qisas.

Arbitration in Ijtihadi Matters and Fatwa Restriction
States arbitration is valid in all ijtihadi matters but not given as fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of arbitration in ijtihadi matters and explain why fatwa is withheld to prevent public misuse.

Core Idea
Arbitration is valid in all ijtihadi matters, but fatwa is withheld to prevent misuse by the public.

This passage clarifies the scope of valid arbitration (tahkim) in Hanafi law: it is permissible in all matters where independent legal reasoning (ijtihad) applies. However, the ruling is deliberately not publicized as a fatwa to prevent common people from abusing the flexibility of ijtihadi rulings. The commentary explains the logical inference and lists examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the phrase 'وصح في سائر المجتهدات' – what is the legal status of arbitration in ijtihadi matters? The verb 'صح' indicates validity (sahih).
The ruling is not just permissible (ja'iz) but valid (sahih), meaning it produces legal effects.
2
Understand the basis
The ruling is derived from the negative implication (mafhum al-mukhalafah) of a previous statement: 'لا يجوز التحكيم في الحدود والقصاص' – since it is prohibited in hudud and qisas, it is valid elsewhere.
This is a form of qiyas: the exception proves the general rule.
3
Define 'ijtihadi matters'
Ijtihadi matters (mujtahadat) are those not explicitly and categorically decided by Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma. Examples given: kinayah, annulment of oaths, divorce, emancipation, sales, etc.
Matters clearly established by primary texts are even more valid for arbitration, but the passage focuses on ijtihadi scope.
4
Analyze the fatwa restriction
The passage says 'ولا يُفتى به دفعاً لتجاسر العوام'. Why is fatwa withheld? To prevent common people from becoming audacious and misusing the ruling. This is a maslaha-based restriction.
The ruling itself is valid, but public dissemination is controlled.
5
Note intra-madhhab nuances
Commentators mention that arbitration is restricted in certain details: e.g., arbitrator cannot perform li'an; judgment does not bind absent parties; some rulings are concealed entirely.
These are not contradictions but additional limitations based on specific texts or public interest.

Source Text

قالوا : وصح في سائر المجتهدات ولا يُفتى به دفعاً لتجاسر العوام
[۱]
٤٨٩

(۲)

Book of Judicature, Chapter of Arbitration and Judging Inheritance. They said: And it is valid in all ijtihadi matters, but it is not given as a fatwa to prevent audacity of the common people. [1] (They said: And it is valid in all ijtihadi matters, but it is not given as a fatwa to prevent audacity of the common people), our sheikhs said: Indeed, specifying this narration, which is his saying: 'And arbitration is not permissible in hudud and qisas' indicates the permissibility of arbitration in all ijtihadi matters such as kinayah (allusions), annulment of oaths and the like. And specifying ijtihadi matters is not to negate the ruling from other than them, for that in which there is no scope for ijtihad, like that established by the Book, the well-known Sunnah, or consensus, there is no doubt about the validity of arbitration in that. (1) His saying: in all ijtihadi matters; i.e., matters in which ijtihad is permissible, and from the rights of the servants such as divorce, emancipation, kitabah, sponsorship, pre-emption, maintenance, debts, and sales, contrary to what contradicts the Book, Sunnah, or consensus. And al-Barjandi said: It is stated in al-Mabsut that the arbitrator cannot perform li'an between the spouses. And in al-Khazanah: The judgment of the arbitrator does not take effect on the absent person. Also in it: If a man swore divorce that he would not marry a woman, and they appointed a man (a Shafi'i) as arbitrator, and he judged the oath to be invalid, it does not take effect with respect to them, and it is said: it takes effect, so that neither of them has the authority to revoke his judgment, but this is concealed and not given as a fatwa to prevent the common people from audacity to such things. End. [2] His saying: Specifying... etc.; this is a refutation of what could be imagined that specifying ijtihadi matters...
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on arbitration and inheritance. It states that arbitration (tahkim) is valid in all matters where ijtihad (independent legal reasoning) is permissible, i.e., matters that are not categorically determined by primary texts (Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma). However, the ruling is not given as a fatwa to the general public (la yufta bihi) in order to prevent the common people from becoming audacious and misusing arbitration. The commentary explains: The fact that previous texts exempted hudud and qisas implies that everything else is permissible. Ijtihadi matters include rights of individuals (huquq al-‘ibad) like divorce, emancipation, kitabah, sponsorship, pre-emption, maintenance, debts, and sales. Matters clearly decided by Quran, Sunnah, or ijma (where no ijtihad is possible) are even more clearly valid for arbitration, but the specification is not exclusive. The commentary also cites restrictions from other sources: the arbitrator cannot perform li'an (mutual cursing) between spouses; his judgment does not bind an absent party; and there is a special case regarding oaths of divorce where some rulings are concealed to prevent misuse. The overall purpose is to uphold the validity of arbitration in a wide range of cases while protecting the legal system from abuse by the uninformed common people.

Key Terms
مجتهدات (mujtahadat): Matters in which ijtihad (independent legal reasoning) is permissible; areas not explicitly and categorically decided by primary texts.
فتوى (fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion given to an inquirer; here, 'la yuta bihi' means that although the ruling is valid, issuing a fatwa to the public is prohibited to prevent misuse.
تجاسر العوام (tajāsir al-'awāmm): The audacity or overreaching of common people who may misuse the ruling if it becomes widely known.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To prevent common people from becoming audacious and misusing the ruling.
C) Because ijtihadi matters are not suitable for arbitration.
D) Because the ruling only applies to scholars, not the public.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is valid in all ijtihadi matters, but fatwa may be withheld to prevent public misuse.

Validity of Arbitration in Ijtihad Matters and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Hudud and Qisas
Ruling on arbitration with intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of arbitration in ijtihad matters and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding hudud and qisas.

Core Idea
Arbitration is valid in all ijtihad matters but prohibited in hudud and qisas according to the majority Hanafi view.

Arbitration (tahkīm) is a private dispute resolution mechanism. The Hanafi school generally upholds its validity in matters open to ijtihad. However, a notable intra-madhhab dispute exists regarding its application to hudud (fixed penalties) and qisas (retaliation). This passage from a sharh presents the majority ruling and the dissenting opinion, along with the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on arbitration
The passage begins by stating that arbitration is valid in all ijtihad-based matters (mujtahidāt). Note that this is the foundation: matters not definitively fixed by clear text are open to arbitration.
Ijtihad matters include those subject to scholarly interpretation, such as kinayah (implicit divorce), dissolution of oaths, etc.
2
Understand the proof from the riwaya
The proof for the generality of arbitration comes from a riwaya that specifies prohibition only in hudud and qisas. By implication (mafhum al-mukhalafah), other matters are permissible.
The text also clarifies that even matters established by Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma are not subject to ijtihad but arbitration applies them directly.
3
Recognize the exception: hudud and qisas
The riwaya explicitly prohibits arbitration in hudud and qisas. This is the exception that triggers the intra-Hanafi dispute.
Hudud are fixed punishments (e.g., for theft, zina); qisas is retaliation for murder or injury.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (as reported by Shams al-A'imma) allowed arbitration in hudud and qisas on the grounds that these involve rights of individuals (huquq al-ibad). However, the majority (al-Khassaf, al-Quduri, matn authors) prohibit it, relying on the explicit text.
The opposing view: since qisas is a personal right, it can be subject to arbitration like financial claims.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its consequence
The relied-upon position is that arbitration in hudud and qisas is not valid. This means any ruling by an arbitrator on these matters is null and must be overturned by a state judge.
The text also notes that if an arbitrator rules against the 'aqila (clan), it is not binding because the 'aqila did not consent to the arbitration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وصح في سائر المجتهدات ولا يُفتى به دفعاً لتجاسر العوام قال مشايخنا له : إن تخصيص هذه الرواية، وهي قوله : ولا يجوزُ التَّحكيم في الحدودِ والقصاص يدلُّ على جواز التحكيم في جميع المجتهدات كالكنايات، وفسخ اليمين ونحوهما، وتخصيص المجتهدات بالذكر ليس لنفي الحكم عما عداه ، فإن ما ليس للاجتهاد فيه مساعٌ : كالثَّابت بالكتاب، أو السنة المشهورة، أو الإجماع لا شك في صحةِ التَّحكيم في ذلك العاقلة، فلا ينفذ حكم من حكماه على عاقلته، ولا على القاتل ؛ لعدم التزام العاقلة حكمه. كذا في «التبيين» ، وغيره. وقال شمس الأئمة : من أصحابنا من قال : التحكيم في حد القذف والقصاص جائز، وذكر في «الذخيرة» عن «صلح» «الأصل»: إن التحكيم في القصاص جائز ؛ لأن الاستيفاء لهما، وهما من حقوق العباد ، فيجوز التحكيم كما في الأموال، وذكر الخصافُ له أنَّ التحكيم لا يجوز في الحدود والقصاص، واختاره القُدوري ، وصاحب «الهداية»، و«الكنز» ، وغيرهم من أصحاب المتون.
They said: It is valid in all ijtihad-based matters, but a fatwa is not given to prevent common people from becoming bold. Our mashayikh said to him: Indeed, the specification of this narration, which is his statement: 'Arbitration is not permissible in hudud and qisas,' indicates the permissibility of arbitration in all ijtihad-based matters such as kinayah, dissolution of oaths, and the like. The specification of ijtihad matters in the mention is not to negate the ruling from what is other than them, for what has no room for ijtihad—like what is established by the Book, the famous Sunnah, or consensus—there is no doubt about the validity of arbitration in that. [As for] the 'aqila, the ruling of the one they appointed as arbitrator does not execute against his 'aqila, nor against the killer, due to the lack of commitment of the 'aqila to his ruling. Thus it is in al-Tabyin and others. And Shams al-A'imma said: Among our companions, some said that arbitration in the hadd of qadhf and qisas is permissible. And it is mentioned in al-Dhakhira from Sulh al-Asl: Verily arbitration in qisas is permissible, because the right of retribution belongs to them, and they are among the rights of the servants, so arbitration is permissible as in financial matters. And al-Khassaf mentioned that arbitration is not permissible in hudud and qisas, and al-Quduri and the author of al-Hidayah and al-Kanz and other matn authors chose that.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the ruling on arbitration in ijtihad-based matters. It begins by stating that arbitration is valid in all matters that are subject to ijtihad (i.e., matters not clearly fixed by definitive texts), but the fatwa is not given openly to prevent ill-informed laypeople from taking liberties. The commentator then explains that the specification of the riwaya stating 'arbitration is not permissible in hudud and qisas' actually implies its permissibility in other ijtihad matters. The proof is that the exclusion of hudud and qisas from arbitration implies inclusion of everything else. However, matters established by the Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma are not subject to ijtihad, but arbitration in those matters is also valid, as the arbitrator simply applies the known ruling. The passage then transitions to a specific case: if an arbitrator is appointed and rules that the 'aqila (clan) must pay diya, that ruling does not bind the 'aqila because they did not consent to the arbitration. Similarly, if the ruling is to pay diya on the killer himself, it is not binding. This is cited from al-Tabyin. Then the passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding arbitration in hudud and qisas. Some Hanafi scholars (as reported by Shams al-A'imma) allow it because these are rights of individuals. Others, like al-Khassaf, al-Quduri, and the authors of al-Hidayah and al-Kanz, prohibit it. This dispute is important because it affects whether an arbitrator can rule on such serious matters. The relied-upon position is that arbitration in hudud and qisas is not valid, as adopted by the majority of matn authors.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkīm): Appointment of a private arbitrator by disputing parties to settle their dispute, as opposed to a state-appointed judge.
مجتهدات (mujtahidāt): Matters that are open to ijtihad, i.e., not definitively established by clear textual evidence from Quran, mutawatir Sunnah, or ijma.
حدود (ḥudūd): Fixed punishments prescribed by Shariah for specific crimes such as theft, zina, qadhf, etc.
قصاص (qiṣāṣ): Retaliation, specifically the right of the victim or his family to demand equivalent punishment for the offender in cases of murder or injury.
عاقلة (ʿāqila): A group of relatives (usually male agnates) who collectively pay blood money (diya) on behalf of a member who commited unintentional homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, is arbitration valid in matters of hudud and qisas?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, not valid.
C) It is valid if both parties agree.
D) Only in qisas.

Key Takeaway
Arbitration is valid in ijtihad matters, but the majority Hanafi position prohibits it in hudud and qisas, relying on an explicit riwaya.

Enforceability of Arbitrator's Ruling on Diya and Benefit of Arbitration
Explains enforceability of arbitrator's ruling on diya and benefit of arbitration in compelling parties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when an arbitrator's ruling regarding diya is binding and when it is not, and explain the practical benefit of arbitration in compelling the parties who appointed him.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's ruling on the 'aqila is not binding because they did not consent; its true benefit is to compel the appointing parties.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a specific question: if an arbitrator rules that the 'aqila (clan responsible for diya) must pay blood money in a case of accidental killing, is that ruling enforceable? The answer is no, because the 'aqila did not consent to the arbitration. However, the arbitrator's role is still valuable: he can compel the litigants to fulfill their obligations, as in a sale. The passage also notes an exception when the ruling is on the killer directly and is based on clear evidence, and a caution about issuing fatwa for widespread arbitration to preserve the authority of judges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text explicitly states that the arbitrator's ruling on the 'aqila for accidental killing does not execute (la yunfadh). The judge must overturn it and rule against the 'aqila himself.
This is the central ruling in the passage.
2
Understand the reason
The reason given is direct: 'because the 'aqila did not appoint him'. Since the 'aqila is not a party to the arbitration agreement, the arbitrator has no authority over them.
This highlights the consensual nature of arbitration in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize the benefit (fa'idah) of arbitration
The benefit of arbitration is to compel the parties who appointed the arbitrator to fulfill obligations, e.g., a buyer to pay and a seller to deliver. The arbitrator can even detain a refusing party.
This answers the hypothetical objection: why arbitrate when the ruling is not binding on third parties?
4
Note the exception
The exception is when the arbitrator rules diya on the killer himself (not the 'aqila) and the killing is proven by confession or the wound evidence shows the amount is within the killer's liability. In that case, the ruling is binding.
The exception is cited from al-Bahr and others.
5
Be aware of the fatwa caution
Although arbitration is valid in all cases in principle, the later Hanafi scholars refrained from giving fatwa on that, fearing that common people would bypass the judge, undermining judicial authority. They required the judge's ruling for hudud and qisas.
This is a practical consideration (maslaha), not a doctrinal invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the judge overturns it and rules against the 'aqila. Its benefit is to bind the litigant. Thus it is in al-Bahr and others.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on arbitration, focusing on the enforceability of an arbitrator's ruling regarding blood money (diya) in cases of accidental homicide (khat'). The key point is that if the arbitrator rules that the 'aqila (the clan responsible for paying diya on behalf of the killer) must pay the diya, that ruling is not binding on the 'aqila. This is because the 'aqila did not consent to the arbitration; only the actual parties to the dispute (e.g., the killer and the victim's family) appointed the arbitrator. Therefore, the qadi (judge) must overturn the arbitrator's ruling and issue a new ruling that is binding on the 'aqila. Similarly, if the arbitrator rules that the killer himself must pay the diya, that ruling is also not binding because the killer might not have the means or the liability might otherwise be on the 'aqila. The commentary then explains the benefit (fa'idah) of arbitration: even though in matters definitively established by revelation the arbitrator's ruling is not based on ijtihad, the benefit lies in compelling the litigants to fulfill their obligations. For example, in a sale, the arbitrator can enforce the exchange of price and goods, and detain those who refuse. The passage then reiterates the caution that despite the validity of arbitration, fatwa is not given to permit it in all cases to prevent laypeople from seeking arbitration excessively, which could undermine the authority of judges. Finally, there is an exception: if the arbitrator rules diya on the killer directly, and the killer confesses or the evidence clearly shows the amount is within his liability, then the ruling may be binding. This exception is cited from al-Bahr.

Key Terms
محكم (muḥakkam): A private arbitrator appointed by the disputing parties to resolve their case.
دم خطأ (dam khaṭa’): Unintentional killing, accidental homicide, which entails payment of diya (blood money) on the 'aqila.
دية (diya): Blood money paid as compensation for killing or injury, fixed at a certain amount by Shariah.
عاقلة (ʿāqila): A group of relatives (usually male agnates) who collectively pay blood money on behalf of a member who committed unintentional homicide.
فائدة (fā’ida): The benefit or utility of a rule; here, the practical consequence of arbitration being to compel parties to fulfill obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, the 'aqila must pay.
B) No, because the 'aqila did not consent to the arbitration.
C) Yes, if the killer is poor.
D) Only if the judge confirms.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's ruling on the 'aqila is not binding without their consent; the primary benefit of arbitration is to compel the appointing parties, not to bind third parties.

Enforcing an Arbitrator's Decision Before a Second Judge
Ruling on enforcing an arbitrator's decision when brought to another judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an arbitrator's decision is brought to a second judge and explain why the judge's enforcement makes it binding.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's decision is not binding until a judge enforces it; once enforced, it cannot be overturned by another judge.

This passage from the Book of Judiciary explains the Hanafi view on the authority of an arbitrator's (muhakkam) ruling. Unlike a judge's (qadi) judgment, the arbitrator's decision is not automatically binding. When a case comes before a different judge, he may enforce the decision only if it agrees with his own madhhab; otherwise, he nullifies it. The hadith of Hammal ibn Malik provides the textual evidence that the arbitrator cannot override a clear textual ruling, such as the Prophet's assignment of blood-money to the 'aqila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a case decided by an arbitrator that is brought to a different judge. The ruling: if the decision agrees with the judge's school (madhhab), he enforces it; otherwise, he nullifies it.
The phrase 'إن وافق مذهبه أمضاه' clearly sets the condition.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith of Hammal ibn Malik (reported by al-Mughirah ibn Shu'bah) in which the Prophet made the 'aqila liable for blood-money, establishing a fixed ruling that cannot be contradicted by an arbitrator.
The hadith is cited in the footnote and is a sabab (occasion) for the rule.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that the Prophet's ruling on blood-money is binding; an arbitrator who decides otherwise (e.g., not imposing it on the 'aqila) contradicts the text, so the judge must nullify such a decision.
The arbitrator's lack of authority to oppose clear textual evidence is the core reason.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage distinguishes the arbitrator's ruling from a judge's judgment: a judge's ruling is binding on all and cannot be overruled by another judge even if it disagrees with his madhhab, due to the judge's general jurisdiction (wilāyah 'āmmah).
This contrast reinforces that the arbitrator's authority is limited and conditional.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is that the arbitrator's decision is not automatically binding; it requires ratification by a judge who agrees with it. Once ratified, the decision becomes like the judge's own judgment and cannot be overturned by a later judge.
This view is grounded in the principle that the arbitrator's role is delegated and revocable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء باب التحكيم والقضاء بالمواريث
فإن رفع إلى قاض آخر إن وافق مذهبه أمضاه، وإلا أبطله.
لأنَّ حُكْمَ المحكم مخالف لمذهب القاضي، ومخالف للنَّص، وهو قوله : «قوموا فَدُوه» ، ومعنى عدم نفاذه على العاقلةِ أن للمُحَكَمَ لا يكون ولاية طلب الدِّيةِ من العاقلة، وحبسهم إن امتنعوا، فإن رُفِعَ إلى قاض آخر إن وافق مذهبه أمضاه ، وإلا أبطلَهُ : أي ليس حُكْمُ المُحَكّم مثلَ حُكْمِ المولى في أن المختلف فيه يصير مجمعاً عليه. قوله : أمضاه ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه ثم إبرامه على الوجه الذي حكم به المحكم ، وفائدة إمضائه أنه لو رفع إلى حاكم يخالف مذهبه لم يتمكن من نقضه، ولو لم يمض لتمكن ؛ لأنّ إمضاء الأوّل بمنزلة حكم نفسه، كما ذكروه. وإلا أي وإن لم يوافق مذهبه عطله ؛ لأنَّ حكم المحكم لا يلزم القاضي ؛ لعدم التحكيم منه بخلاف حكم الحاكم، فإنّه لا يبطل الثاني، وإن كان مخالفاً لمذهبه ؛ لكون ولايته عامة، فكان قضاؤه حجَّةً في حق الكل فلا يجوز للثاني ردّه.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith of Hammal ibn Malik. It addresses the authority of an arbitrator's decision when brought before a different judge. The ruling states that the second judge has discretion: if the arbitrator's decision agrees with his own madhhab, he may enforce it; if not, he nullifies it. The reason given is that the arbitrator's decision is contrary to the judge's own school and to the textual evidence (the hadith). The commentary explains that the arbitrator's decision is not binding like a judge's judgment. The judge's enforcement is beneficial because it prevents the case from being easily overturned if later brought to another judge. The footnote provides the full hadith evidence, which involves a case where the Prophet assigned blood-money to the agila of the killer. This illustrates the principle that the arbitrator's decision cannot override clear textual rulings.

Key Terms
تحكيم (tahkim): The process of arbitration where parties appoint an arbitrator to resolve a dispute.
محكم (muhakkam): The arbitrator appointed by parties to decide a case.
قاض (qadi): A judge appointed by the ruler with general jurisdiction.
عاقلة (aqila): The male paternal relatives of a killer who are collectively liable for paying blood-money (diyah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He must send it back to the arbitrator.
D) He can only enforce it if no other judge has seen it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an arbitrator's decision is not binding until a judge of similar madhhab enforces it; once enforced, it becomes as conclusive as the judge's own ruling.

Reconciling Contradictory Claims in Court: Gift and Purchase Evidence
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sufficiency of possibility of reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that evidence of purchase after a claimed gift is accepted if reconciliation is possible, and distinguish the four Hanafi positions on the sufficiency of mere possibility.

Core Idea
Evidence is accepted if claims can be reconciled, not simply because they seem contradictory.

In judicial proceedings, a litigant may appear to contradict himself by first claiming a gift and later proving a purchase. The Hanafi school examines when such evidence should be accepted. This lesson unpacks the core scenario and the nuanced debate among Hanafi scholars on whether mere possibility of reconciliation suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory claims
Read the scenario: Zayd claims a house as a gift; later he provides evidence of purchase. First, note the timing: purchase after gift or before?
Contradiction arises only if purchase precedes gift.
2
Apply the rule of acceptance
The rule: if purchase after gift, evidence is accepted because claims can be reconciled (the gift is admission of donor's prior ownership; purchase later is new transaction). If purchase before gift, contradiction is confirmed and evidence rejected.
This is the settled position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyze the four intra-Hanafi positions
The commentary presents four views on whether mere possibility of reconciliation (imkan al-tawfiq) suffices or actual reconciliation is required. Classify each according to its scope.
These positions apply to any contradictory testimony, not just gift/purchase.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
For each position, consider the practical difference: does the judge accept evidence or not? Focus on how each ruling changes the outcome.
The preferred view (Shaykh al-Islam) accepts possibility absolutely.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentary implies the second position (kifayat al-imkan mutlaqan) is strongest. Consider why it might be preferred: it aligns with facilitating evidence and avoiding false rejection.
This tarjih is attributed to later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله : أو لم يقل ذلك ; ومفاده الاكتفاء بإمكان التوفيق، وهاهنا أقوال أربعة : الأول : إنّه لا بدَّ من التوفيق بالفعل، ولا يكفي الإمكان. والثاني : كفاية الإمكان مطلقاً سواء كان من المدعي أو المدعى عليه، وتعدد وجه التوفيق إذا تحدّد، وهو مختار شيخ الإسلام له. الثالث : إنه يكفي من المدعى عليه لا من المدعي ; لأنه مستحق، وذاك دافع، وهو مختارُ الحُجَنْدي. والرابع : كفاية الإمكان إن اتَّحد وجه التوفيق لا إن تعددت وجوهه، وهذا الخلاف يجري في كل موضع حصل فيه التناقض من المدعي أو من شهوده، أو من المدعى عليه، والتفصيل في «البحر» (1)
And whoever claims a gift at a time and is asked for evidence, and says: 'He denied it to me, so I bought it from him.' (1) That, as Fakhr al-Islam related from al-Faqih Abu Ja'far. If he provides evidence of purchase before the time of the gift, his evidence is not accepted because the contradiction is confirmed, since the claim of gift is an admission that the gifted property was the donor's property before the gift, so the claim of purchase at the time of the gift is not accepted. (1) 'Ramz al-Haqa'iq' (2:93-94). Or if he did not say that, but provides evidence of purchase after the time of the gift, it is accepted, and before it, no. His statement: 'So he provided evidence of purchase after the time of the gift, it is accepted, and before it, no' refers to the two scenarios: i.e., whether he said 'he denied me' or not. Because the claim of gift is an admission that the gifted property was the donor's property before the gift, so the claim of purchase before the gift time is not accepted. As for the claim of purchase after the gift time, there is no contradiction because it establishes his ownership after the gift. (His statement: 'Or if he did not say that' – its purport is sufficiency of the possibility of reconciliation, and here there are four statements: First: Actual reconciliation is necessary, possibility is not enough. Second: Possibility is absolutely sufficient, whether from the plaintiff or defendant, even if multiple ways of reconciliation exist as long as one is specific, and this is the choice of Shaykh al-Islam. Third: It is sufficient from the defendant but not from the plaintiff, because he is the claimant and the other is repelling, and this is the choice of al-Hujandi. Fourth: Possibility is sufficient only if there is a single way of reconciliation, not if there are multiple ways. This disagreement applies to every situation where contradiction arises from the plaintiff, his witnesses, or the defendant. The detail is in 'al-Bahr' (1).
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage begins as a commentary on the matn about contradictory claims. It presents a scenario: Zayd claims a house as a gift from Bakr, but when asked for evidence, he says Bakr denied the gift, so he bought it from him. The commentary explains that if Zayd provides evidence of purchase after the time of the gift, it is accepted because the two claims can be reconciled: the gift claim is an admission that the property was the donor's before the gift, so the purchase after the gift is a new transaction. If evidence of purchase before the gift time is presented, it is contradictory and rejected. The commentary then discusses four scholarly positions (aqwal arba'ah) within the Hanafi school regarding the sufficiency of mere possibility of reconciliation (imkan al-tawfiq) versus actual reconciliation. These positions vary, with some requiring actual reconciliation, others accepting possibility absolutely, others from the defendant only, and others depending on whether the possibility has a single or multiple aspects. The discussion is relevant to how judges handle apparent contradictions in claims and testimony. This is a Hanafi legal analysis of judicial procedure.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift transfer of ownership without compensation.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony in court.
إمكان التوفيق (imkan al-tawfiq): The possibility of reconciling two apparently contradictory statements or claims.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction in a party's claims or testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the four Hanafi positions on imkan al-tawfiq, which one is attributed to Shaykh al-Islam?
A) Actual reconciliation is necessary.
B) Possibility is absolutely sufficient.
C) Only from the defendant.
D) Only if a single way of reconciliation.

Key Takeaway
Evidence of purchase after a claimed gift is accepted because the two claims can be reconciled, and the preferred Hanafi view accepts mere possibility of reconciliation absolutely.

Believing the Muqirr's Claim of Counterfeit Dirhams
Ruling on believing a muqirr who claims received dirhams were counterfeit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a muqirr who admits receiving dirhams but claims they were counterfeit is believed with his oath, and apply this principle to similar admissions involving ambiguous property.

Core Idea
Admission of receipt does not admit quality; the muqirr is believed with oath.

Imagine someone tells you, 'I received ten dirhams from you, but they were counterfeit.' Would you believe him? In Hanafi law, such an admission is accepted as sincere because the word 'dirhams' covers both good and bad coins. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, which balances the binding nature of iqrar with the ambiguity of the term 'dirhams.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is an admission (iqrar) of receiving dirhams, combined with a claim that they were counterfeit (zuyuf or nabarhajah). The ruling: the muqirr is believed, but only with his oath.
This is a Hanafi position in the chapter of qada (judicial proceedings).
2
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason given is that 'dirhams' applies to both genuine and counterfeit coins. Therefore, the claim of counterfeit does not contradict the admission of receipt; it only clarifies the quality received.
The text says 'li-anna al-darāhima taqa'u 'alayhimā' (because dirhams apply to both).
3
Identify the condition (oath)
The muqirr is believed 'with his oath.' This means he must swear that the dirhams were indeed counterfeit; otherwise, his bare statement would not be sufficient.
If he refuses to take the oath, the plaintiff may demand genuine dirhams.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools require the muqirr to produce evidence of counterfeitness, but Hanafis accept his claim with oath because the term 'dirhams' is ambiguous.
This is an area of legitimate ikhtilaf.

Commentary
This passage rules that if a person admits he received ten dirhams from someone but claims the dirhams were counterfeit (zuyuf or nabarhajah), he is believed in that claim with his oath. The reasoning is that the term 'dirhams' applies to both genuine and counterfeit coins; so his claim does not contradict his admission. The commentary adds that he is believed with his oath. This is a Hanafi ruling in the law of testimony and claims (iqrar).

Key Terms
مقر (muqirr): A person who makes an admission (iqrar) against himself.
زبوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit coins, specifically dirhams that are base metal.
نبهْرجة (nabarhajah): Counterfeit or worn-out dirhams, often without full silver content.

Evidence of Purchase After a Gift: The Hanafi Ruling on Contradictory Claims
Ruling on acceptance of evidence of purchase after a gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on accepting evidence of purchase after a gift and explain why timing determines acceptance.

Core Idea
A claim of gift admits the donor's prior ownership; so purchase evidence after the gift is accepted, but before it is not.

This lesson examines a Hanafi legal principle regarding contradictory claims in court. When a person claims a gift from another, that claim inherently acknowledges that the donor owned the property before the gift. If the same person later brings evidence of purchase, the timing relative to the gift becomes critical. Understanding this distinction is essential for judges and litigants to correctly evaluate evidence without logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is presenting evidence of purchase after having claimed a gift. The ruling is that evidence after the gift is accepted; evidence before the gift is rejected.
This applies whether or not the donee claimed the donor denied the gift.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is rational (dalil aqli). The claim of gift is an admission (iqrar) that the donor owned the property before the gift. A claim of purchase before the gift contradicts this admission, so it is rejected. A claim of purchase after the gift does not contradict because it asserts a new ownership event.
No direct Quranic or hadith text is cited; the reasoning is based on logical consistency.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The contradiction is clear: an admission of past ownership cannot coexist with a claim that someone else bought it at the same time. The later purchase is a separate incident and does not conflict.
This is a case of 'admission prevents contradictory evidence' (al-iqrar yamna' al-bayyinah al-munafiyah).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is well-established. Some schools may have different rules on contradictory evidence, but the Hanafi ruling emphasizes the logical implications of the admission.
While some schools may accept certain contradictory evidence under dhawahir, the Hanafi school prioritizes the clear contradiction.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is mufta bihi. It is relied upon in Hanafi courts.
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded on this point.

Source Text

(Commentary:) His saying: 'established evidence of the purchase after the time of the gift, it is accepted, and before it, no' refers to the two scenarios: i.e., whether he said 'he has denied me' or not. For the claim of gift is an acknowledgment that the gifted item was the property of the donor before the gift, so the claim of purchase before the time of the gift is not accepted. As for the claim of purchase after the time of the gift, there is no contradiction because it establishes his ownership after the gift.

Commentary
This passage from 'Amdat al-Ri'ayah explains a ruling from Al-Wiqayah about contradictory claims. The matn states: if a person does not say that he was denied, and then brings evidence of purchase after the time of the gift, it is accepted; but evidence of purchase before the time of the gift is not accepted. The commentator clarifies that this applies whether the donee claimed the donor denied him or not. The logic is: the claim of gift implies that the donor owned the item before the gift, so a claim of purchase before the gift contradicts that. However, claiming purchase after the gift is not contradictory because it asserts ownership acquired after the gift. Thus, the evidence is accepted for the later purchase.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A gift, transferring ownership without compensation.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses.
ملك (milk): Ownership; proprietary right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the donor has no right to sell
D) Because the donee denied the purchase

Key Takeaway
The timing of evidence relative to a prior claim determines its admissibility: evidence that does not contradict the logical implications of the claim is accepted.

Claims About Debased Dirhams: When Is the Claimant Believed?
Ruling on trustworthiness of claimant regarding quality of dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a person who acknowledges receiving dirhams is believed when claiming they were debased, and explain why the classification of dirham types (jiyad, ziyuf, nabhrajah, sutuqah) determines the outcome.

Core Idea
Claimant is believed for ziyuf and nabhrajah, but not sutuqah, because sutuqah is not a type of dirham.

When a person acknowledges receiving ten dirhams and later claims they were of poor quality, the court must decide whether to accept that claim. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling based on the type of debasement claimed. This lesson unpacks the four categories of dirhams and explains why some claims are plausible and others are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment and the claim
Start with the fact that the person has already acknowledged receipt of ten dirhams. Then consider the claim about quality: he says they were ziyuf, nabhrajah, or sutuqah.
The key is whether the claim contradicts the acknowledgment or is consistent with it.
2
Classify the type of dirham claimed
Use the four-fold Hanafi classification: jiyad (excellent), ziyuf (slightly debased but still silver-dominant), nabhrajah (more debased, rejected by merchants), and sutuqah (predominantly alloy, not genuine dirhams).
Ziyuf and nabhrajah are still considered dirhams; sutuqah is not.
3
Apply the ruling on consistency
If the claimed type is still a kind of dirham (ziyuf or nabhrajah), the claim is consistent with the acknowledgment and the claimant is believed (with an oath). If the claimed type is not a kind of dirham (sutuqah), the claim is contradictory and not accepted.
The ruling also depends on whether the claim is made in the same utterance (connected) or separately (disconnected) – see the commentary on connected/disconnected statements.
4
Analyze the proof (istidlal)
The proof is based on the nature of dirhams: the term 'dirham' in the acknowledgment is understood according to common usage (urf). Ziyuf and nabhrajah are types that circulate, so claiming them is plausible. Sutuqah is not treated as dirhams in transactions, so the claim is implausible.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the definition and types of dirhams.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on reconciliation
The commentary mentions four opinions among later scholars on how to reconcile contradictions when they occur (e.g., connected vs. disconnected statements). However, the core ruling on dirham types remains consistent.
The mufta bihi position is that the claim is accepted for ziyuf and nabhrajah, and the distinction between connected and disconnected statements is applied when the claim relates to quality after acknowledging specific good dirhams.

Commentary
This passage deals with a person who acknowledges receiving ten dirhams and then claims they were of inferior quality. The ruling is that he is believed with an oath if he claims they were 'ziyuf' (slightly debased) or 'nabhrajah' (more debased), but not if he claims they were 'sutuqah' (heavily alloyed, not real dirhams). The commentary explains the types of dirhams: (1) jiyad (excellent), (2) ziyuf (silver predominant but less), (3) nabhrajah (even less silver, rejected by merchants), (4) sutuqah (mostly alloy, not considered dirhams). The reasoning is that ziyuf and nabhrajah are still types of dirhams, so the claim is plausible; sutuqah is not a type of dirham, so the claim is inconsistent. The commentary also discusses the difference between connected and disconnected statements: if the acknowledgment and the claim are made in one utterance, it may be accepted as an exception; if separate, it is contradictory. The four opinions about reconciliation of contradictions are noted.

Key Terms
زيوف (ziyuf): Dirhams that are debased but still have silver predominant; not accepted by treasury but circulate among merchants.
نبهرجة (nabhrajah): Dirhams with poor silver content, rejected by merchants; considered bad quality but still a type of dirham.
ستوقة (sutuqah): Dirhams predominantly alloyed, not considered genuine dirhams.
جياد (jiyad): Dirhams of excellent quality, high silver content.

Revocation of Acknowledgment and Acceptance of Evidence of Discharge
Ruling on revocation of acknowledgment and acceptance of evidence of discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a contradictory denial nullifies an acknowledgment and explain why plaintiff's evidence of the debt is accepted even if the defendant claims payment or release.

Core Idea
A clear denial after acknowledgment revokes it; evidence of debt outweighs an unproven discharge claim.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses two procedural rulings in Hanafi fiqh. First, if a person acknowledges a debt and later says 'I have nothing of yours' (i.e., denies the debt), the original acknowledgment is nullified by the contradiction. Second, if the defendant denies the debt ever existed but the plaintiff brings evidence of the debt, and the defendant then claims it was paid or forgiven, the plaintiff's evidence is accepted because it proves the debt's existence, shifting the burden to the defendant to prove the discharge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal scenarios in the passage
Find the two distinct situations: (1) a person acknowledges a sum then denies it, and (2) a defendant denies any debt, plaintiff brings evidence, defendant raises discharge.
The passage uses 'قط' for 'ever' in the second scenario.
2
Analyze the effect of a contradictory denial after acknowledgment
When the same person says 'I have nothing of yours' after acknowledging a thousand, his first statement is nullified because the second contradicts it. The Hanafi school treats it as a revocation.
If the denial is not clear (e.g., ambiguous), it may not nullify the acknowledgment.
3
Understand the ruling on evidence when discharge is claimed
If the defendant says 'you never had anything against me' and then claims the debt was paid or released, the plaintiff's evidence of the debt is still accepted. The evidence proves the debt; the defendant must prove the discharge.
The ruling applies to both qada (payment) and ibra (release).
4
Distinguish between the two rulings
Notice that in the first scenario, the denial is from the same person who acknowledged; in the second, the denial is from the defendant at the start of the claim, and evidence is brought.
In the first, the denial cancels the previous acknowledgment; in the second, the denial is countered by evidence.
5
Apply the rulings to a simple hypothetical
Example: If Zayd says 'I owe Amr 100' then later says 'I owe him nothing', the second statement cancels the first. If in court Amr sues for 100 and Zayd says 'he never lent me anything', Amr's evidence of the loan is accepted, and Zayd must prove payment.
The burden of proof for discharge is on the defendant.

Source Text

( وقولُه : ليس لي عليك شيءٌ للمقر بألف يُبْطِلُ إقراره، ويل لي عليك ألف بعده بلا حجة لغو. فإن قال المدعى عليه عقيب دعوى مال : ما كان لك علي شيءٍ قط ، فأقام المدعي بينة على ألف ، وهو على القضاء، أو الإبراء، قبلت هذه) واختلفوا في تفسير النبهرجة، قيل : هي التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان، والزيوف : هي المغشوشة، والسوقة : صفر مموّه بالفضة، وقال عامة المشايخ : الجياد : فضة خالصة تروج في التجارات، وتوضع في بيت المال. والزيوفُ ما زيفه : أي ردّه بيت المال، ولكن يأخذه التجار في التجارات، ولا بأس بالشراء بها ، لكن ينبغي أن يبين البائع أنها زيوف، والنَّهْرَجةُ ما يردُّه التجار، والسوقة: أن يكون الطاقُ الأعلى والأسفل ،فضة ، وبينهما صفر، وليس لهما حكم الدراهم. كذا في «الخانية» وغيرها ، وقد حققنا ذلك فيما سبق.
And His statement: 'I have nothing of yours against me' from a person who acknowledged a thousand nullifies his acknowledgment, and 'Woe to me, I have a thousand against you' after that without proof is pointless. If the defendant after a claim of property says: 'You never had anything against me,' then the plaintiff presents evidence of a thousand, and it is with regard to payment or release, this is accepted. And in Al-Maghrib: 'I did not find it with the nun.' And as-sutooqah is an arabicization of 'sutuyah': meaning its interior is copper plated with silver... If the defendant after a claim of property says: 'You never had anything against me,' then the plaintiff presents evidence of a thousand, and it is with regard to payment or release, this is accepted. And they differed in the interpretation of an-nabahrajah; it is said: it is what is struck outside the sultan's mint; and az-zuyuf: they are counterfeit; and as-suuqa: copper plated with silver; and the generality of mashayikh said: al-jiyad is pure silver that circulates in trade and is placed in the treasury. And az-zuyuf is what the treasury rejects, but merchants take it in trade, and there is no harm in buying with it, but the seller should clarify that they are zayf. And an-nahrajah is what merchants reject. And as-suuqa is that the upper and lower layers are silver and between them is copper, and they do not have the ruling of dirhams.
