Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage addresses two interrelated topics: the revocation of an acknowledgment and the acceptance of evidence of discharge. First, if a person acknowledges a debt of one thousand and then says 'I have nothing of yours against me,' his original acknowledgment is nullified because the later statement contradicts and rejects it. However, if after that he says 'Woe to me, I have a thousand against you' without any proof, it is meaningless. Second, if the defendant in a property claim says 'You never had anything against me,' and then the plaintiff brings evidence of a one thousand debt, but the defendant claims it was a payment (qada) or a release (ibra), the evidence (presumably the plaintiff's evidence of the debt) is accepted because it supports the existence of the debt, and the burden of proof for the discharge falls on the defendant. The passage then revisits the definitions of dirham types, repeating earlier explanations about nabahrajah, zayf, sutooqah, and jiyad, with additional details about the layers of coins. This part appears as a marginal note or summary from other sources like al-Khaniyah.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment of a debt or right against oneself, which binds the declarant in court.
القضاء (al-qada): A judicial decision or payment ordered by a court; used here as one form of discharge of debt.
الإبراء (al-ibra): A release or discharge from a debt by the creditor, which extinguishes the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a plaintiff brings evidence of a one thousand debt, and the defendant says 'You never had anything against me' and then claims that the debt was already paid (qada), is the plaintiff's evidence accepted?
A) No, because the defendant's claim of payment overrides the evidence.
B) Yes, because the evidence establishes the debt, and the defendant must prove payment.
C) No, because the evidence is only acceptable if the defendant does not raise any defense.
D) Yes, but only if the evidence is written.

Key Takeaway
A clear denial after an acknowledgment nullifies the acknowledgment; plaintiff's evidence of the debt is accepted even if the defendant claims discharge, shifting the burden of proof to the defendant.

Contradictory Statements in Confession and Evidence
Ruling on denial followed by evidence of discharge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules on contradictory statements in judicial proceedings, specifically regarding denial of debt followed by evidence of discharge.

Core Idea
A defendant can bring evidence of payment or discharge after denying a debt, as long as reconciliation of statements is possible.

In Hanafi fiqh, the procedural rules governing contradictory statements in court aim to balance the evidentiary value of confessions and testimony. This passage tackles a common scenario: a defendant first denies any debt, then later claims to have paid or been discharged. The ruling hinges on whether the statements can be reconciled—a key principle in legal reasoning. By understanding this, you will grasp how Hanafi jurists prioritize consistency while allowing for factual reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage to determine the specific judicial act being ruled upon: the defendant's denial of debt followed by evidence of discharge. Note the initial ruling that such evidence is accepted.
The act is the presentation of evidence of discharge after a categorical denial.
2
Locate the condition that changes the ruling
Observe that when the defendant adds 'I do not know you' to the denial, the evidence is rejected. Identify why: impossibility of reconciliation without acquaintance.
Acquaintance is a necessary condition for transactions and discharge.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that Zufar rejects acceptance of discharge evidence after denial, while the majority (and al-Quduri) accept it. Note al-Quduri's exception for cases where the defendant might have acted through an agent.
The dispute revolves around whether denial contradicts the possibility of a prior debt.
4
Apply the principle of tawfiq (reconciliation)
Understand that the mashayikh differ on whether mere possibility of reconciliation suffices or explicit reconciliation is required. The text suggests possibility is sufficient.
Tawfiq is preferred over tarjih in Hanafi usul.
5
Distinguish from claims after acknowledgment
Note the separate ruling: if the defendant acknowledged the debt and then claims payment, the session matters. Both statements in one session are contradictory; separate sessions allow acceptance.
The timing of statements affects the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ليس لي عليك شيءٌ للمقر بألف يُبْطِلُ إقراره، ويل لي عليك ألف بعده بلا حجة لغو. فإن قال المدعى عليه عقيب دعوى مال : ما كان لك علي شيءٍ قط ، فأقام المدعي بينة على ألف ، وهو على القضاء، أو الإبراء، قبلت هذه. وإن زاد على إنكاره ، ولا أعرفك ، رُدَّت : أي قال : ما كان لك عليَّ شيء قط أعرفك، ثُمَّ أقامَ بينة على القضاء أو الإبراء، لا تقبل لتعدرِ التَّوفيق ؛ لأنَّه لا يكون بين اثنين أخذ وإعطاء ، ومعاملة وإبراء بدون المعرفة ، وذَكَرَ القُدُورِيُّ له : أنَّه تُقْبَلُ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ المحتجب ، أو المخدرة قد يأمرُ بعض وكلائه، بإرضائه، ولا يَعْرِفُهُ ، ثُمَّ يَعْرِفُهُ بعد ذلك، فأمكن التوفيق. واعلم أن إمكانَ التَّوفيق هل يكفي في دَفْعِ التَّناقُضِ، أو لا بُدَّ من أَن يُصَرِّحَ بالتوفيق، اختلف فيه المشايخ له وقيد بدعوى الإيفاء بعد الإنكار، إذ لو ادعاه بعد الإقرار بالدين، فإن كان كلا القولين في مجلس وإن لم يقبل التناقض. وإن تفرّقا عن المجلس ثمَّ ادّعاه وأقامَ البيَّنة على الإيفاء بعد الإقرار يقبلُ لعدم التناقض، وإن ادّعى الإيفاء قبل الإقرار لا يقبل. كذا في «البحر» نقلا عن «خزانة المفتيين».
And his statement: 'I have nothing against you' for the one who acknowledged a thousand nullifies his acknowledgment, and 'Woe to me, I have a thousand against you' afterwards without proof is meaningless. If the defendant says in response to a claim of wealth: 'You never had anything against me at all,' then the plaintiff brings evidence for a thousand, and [the defendant] is [proving] payment or discharge, this evidence is accepted. And if he adds to his denial: 'And I do not know you,' it is rejected: meaning he said: 'You never had anything against me at all, I do not know you,' then he brings evidence for payment or discharge, it is not accepted due to impossibility of reconciliation; because there cannot be between two people taking and giving, transaction and discharge without acquaintance. And al-Quduri mentioned for him that it is also accepted, because the veiled one or the hidden woman may order some of his agents to satisfy him and not know him, then know him afterwards, so reconciliation is possible. And know that [the question] whether possibility of reconciliation suffices to repel contradiction, or must one explicitly reconcile, the mashayikh differed about it. And it is restricted to a claim of payment after denial, because if he claims it after acknowledgment of debt, then if both statements are in one session, even if contradiction is not accepted. And if they separate from the session then he claims and brings evidence for payment after acknowledgment, it is accepted due to absence of contradiction. And if he claims payment before acknowledgment, it is not accepted. Thus in al-Bahr quoting from Khizanat al-Muftiyin.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqaya deals with contradictory statements in judicial proceedings. The first ruling: if a person acknowledges a debt of a thousand, then says 'I have nothing against you' (meaning no claim), this nullifies the acknowledgment. But if he says 'Woe to me, I have a thousand against you' without proof, it is meaningless and does not revive the acknowledgment. The second topic: if a defendant initially denies any debt ('you never had anything against me'), then later brings evidence that he already paid or was discharged, the evidence is accepted according to the correct view. The reasoning is that payment or discharge can occur even if no debt actually existed, e.g., the plaintiff may have mistakenly believed there was a debt. A dissenting view by Zufar (mentioned in the hashya) is rejected. The third issue: if the defendant adds 'I do not know you' to his denial, then brings evidence of payment or discharge, the evidence is generally rejected because acquaintance is necessary for transactions. However, al-Quduri accepted it, arguing that a principal may authorize an agent without knowing the claimant, then later come to know him. This possibility makes reconciliation of the statements possible. The mashayikh differed on whether mere possibility of reconciliation suffices or must be explicitly stated. The passage also clarifies that if the defendant claims payment after having acknowledged the debt (rather than denying it), the ruling differs depending on whether the statements were in the same session or separate sessions.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession of a right against oneself, which is binding in Islamic law.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, specifically the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, or other accepted proofs.
تبرئة (tabri'ah): Discharge or absolution from a debt or claim.
توفيق (tawfīq): Reconciliation or harmonization of seemingly contradictory statements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a defendant initially denies any debt and later brings evidence of discharge, what is the ruling according to the majority Hanafi position?
A) The evidence is rejected because the denial and discharge are contradictory.
B) The evidence is accepted because reconciliation of statements is possible.
C) The evidence is accepted only if the defendant explicitly admits prior knowledge.
D) The evidence is rejected unless the defendant can prove he did not know the plaintiff.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defendant's denial of a debt does not automatically bar later evidence of payment or discharge; the court accepts such evidence if the statements can be reconciled.

Evidence After Denying Knowledge: Rejection and Exceptions
Explains ruling when defendant denies knowledge then brings evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting evidence when a defendant first denies knowledge and then brings evidence for qada or ibra', and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute involving Zufar and al-Quduri.

Core Idea
If defendant says 'I don't know you', later evidence for a transaction is rejected – reconciliation is impossible.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qada: a defendant first denies knowing the plaintiff (often combined with denying the debt), then later brings evidence claiming a prior judgment or acquittal. The default Hanafi position rejects such evidence because the statements cannot be reconciled. However, Imam Zufar and al-Quduri raise objections, and al-Quduri allows an exception for persons in seclusion. We will unpack the reasoning and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and its basis
The matn states that evidence is rejected when the defendant says 'I do not know you' and then brings evidence for qada or ibra'. The reason given is ta'addur al-tawfiq – impossibility of reconciling the statements: a transaction requires knowledge between parties.
2
State Zufar's objection and his proof
Imam Zufar argues that evidence should be accepted because qada (judgment) necessarily implies a prior right, and ibra' (acquittal) also implies a prior right.
Zufar: 'Qada requires prior right.'
3
State the Hanafi response to Zufar
The majority respond that judgment may be rendered without a true right (e.g., a judge errs), and acquittal may be declared by a claimant who mistakenly believes he has a right. Thus, the evidence does not prove the underlying transaction.
Judgment can be mistaken; acquittal can be based on error.
4
State al-Quduri's exception and its basis
Al-Quduri argues that for persons in seclusion (muhtajab, mukhadara), reconciliation is possible because they may have sent an agent to settle without knowing the opponent, later learning his identity.
Exception only for those who do not personally handle affairs.
5
Note the practical consequence (mufta bihi)
The relied-upon position is rejection of evidence, but some later texts (al-Kafi) accept evidence for ibra' from such secluded persons. The mufta bihi follows the majority rejection, but the exception is acknowledged.
Mufta bihi: evidence rejected except possibly for ibra' by secluded persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء باب التحكيم والقضاء بالمواريث وإن زاد على إنكاره، ولا أعرفك، رُدَّت
خلافاً لزُفَره؛ لأنَّ القضاء يقتضي سبق حق، وكذا الإبراء، وقد قال: ما كان لك علي شيءٌ فلا يُصَدَّقُ في دعوى القضاء والإبراء.
قلنا: القضاء قد يكون بلا حق، وكذا الإبراء، فإن المدعي قد يبرؤ عن حق ثابت في زعمه، وإن لم يكن ثابتا في الحقيقة.
(وإن زاد على إنكاره، ولا أعرفك، رُدَّت): أي قال: ما كان لك عليَّ شيء قط أعرفك، ثُمَّ أقامَ بينة على القضاء أو الإبراء، لا تقبل لتعدرِ التَّوفيق؛ لأنَّه لا يكون بين اثنين أخذ وإعطاء، ومعاملة وإبراء بدون المعرفة، وذَكَرَ القُدُورِيُّ له: أنَّه تُقْبَلُ أيضاً؛ لأنَّ المحتجب، أو المخدرة قد يأمرُ بعض وكلائه، بإرضائه، ولا يَعْرِفُهُ، ثُمَّ يَعْرِفُهُ بعد ذلك، فأمكن التوفيق.
١] قوله: المحتجب؛ قيل: هو مَن لا يراه أحد لعظمته، وقيل: مَن لا يتولّى الأمور بنفسه.
والمخدرة: هي التي لم تجرِ عادتها بالبروز في مجلس الحكم.
[٢]قوله: فأمكن التوفيق؛ قال في «النهاية»: فعلى هذا لو كان المدعى عليه ممن يتولى الأمور بنفسه لا تقبلُ بينته، وفي «الكافي» تقبلُ بينتُه على الإبراء فيه، هذا الفصل باتفاق الروايات. كذا في «الرمز».
Book of Judgments, Chapter of Arbitration and Judgment on Inheritances. And if he adds to his denial and says 'I do not know you', it is rejected, contrary to Zufar, because judgment requires prior right, and likewise acquittal. And he said: 'You have nothing against me' is not believed in a claim of judgment and acquittal. We say: judgment may be without right, and likewise acquittal, for the claimant may declare acquittal of a right that is established in his opinion even if it is not established in reality. (And if he adds to his denial and says 'I do not know you', it is rejected): meaning he said 'You never had anything against me and I do not know you', then he brought evidence for judgment or acquittal, it is not accepted because reconciliation is impossible; for there cannot be between two persons taking and giving, transaction and acquittal without knowledge. And al-Quduri mentioned that it is also accepted, because the hidden person or the veiled woman may order one of his agents to satisfy him without knowing him, then he knows him after that, so reconciliation is possible. [Marginal note 1] He said: 'al-muhtajab'; it is said he is one whom no one sees due to his greatness, and it is said he is one who does not manage affairs himself. And al-mukhadara: she who is not accustomed to appear in the judgment council. [Marginal note 2] He said: 'so reconciliation is possible'; said in al-Nihaya: upon this, if the defendant is one who manages affairs himself, his evidence is not accepted; and in al-Kafi, his evidence for acquittal is accepted in this matter, by agreement of the narrations. So it is in al-Ramz.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states a case: a defendant first denies the claim and adds 'I do not know you' (la a'rifuk). If he then brings evidence for a prior judgment (qada) or acquittal (ibra'), the evidence is rejected. This is contrary to Imam Zufar, who argues that judgment necessarily implies a prior right, so such evidence should be believed. The majority respond that judgment can occur without a right, e.g., when a judge mistakenly rules in favor of someone. Similarly, acquittal may be declared by a claimant who mistakenly believes he has a right. Therefore, the evidence is not automatically accepted. The commentator then explains that the rejection is due to the impossibility of reconciling the statements: one cannot have a transaction or acquittal without knowing the other party. However, al-Quduri argues that acceptance is possible because a person in seclusion (muhtajab) or a veiled woman (mukhadara) might send an agent to satisfy the opponent without knowing him, and only later come to know him. Thus, reconciliation is possible, so evidence should be accepted. The marginal notes clarify the terms: al-muhtajab is one who is not seen or does not personally handle affairs; al-mukhadara is a woman not accustomed to appearing in court. Another note from al-Nihaya states that if the defendant personally handles affairs, his evidence is not accepted, while al-Kafi accepts evidence for acquittal in this case. This shows nuance within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المحتجب (al-muhtajab): A person who is not seen due to greatness or who does not personally handle his affairs.
المخدرة (al-mukhadara): A woman who is not accustomed to appearing in court.
التوفيق (al-tawfiq): Reconciliation of contradictory statements or actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is evidence rejected when the defendant says 'I don't know you' and then brings evidence for a judgment?
A) Because judgment requires prior right, which is absent.
B) Because reconciliation between the statements is impossible.
C) Because the plaintiff did not present the evidence earlier.
D) Because the defendant is considered trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
When a defendant denies knowledge of the plaintiff, any subsequent evidence for a prior legal transaction is presumptively false due to impossibility of reconciliation, unless the defendant is a person of seclusion who could have dealt through an agent.

Imkan al-Tawfiq: Possibility of Reconciliation in Claim Contradictions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether possibility of reconciliation suffices to avoid contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi aspects regarding imkan al-tawfiq and apply them to determine whether a claim is accepted when temporal order is unclear.

Core Idea
Mere possibility of reconciling contradictory claims does not validate them; certainty is required.

When a person makes a claim and later brings evidence that might contradict it, can the evidence be accepted if there is a possible way to reconcile? Hanafi scholars disagreed on whether the mere possibility of reconciliation suffices or whether explicit reconciliation is required. This lesson examines that dispute through the lens of a concrete case: a plaintiff who first claims a gift but cannot prove it, then claims purchase with evidence but without clarifying the timing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two aspects of imkan al-tawfiq
The first aspect holds that if reconciliation is possible, contradiction is avoided to protect the claim. The second insists that the claim must be certainly valid; mere possibility does not nullify the defendant's right.
Both aspects aim to balance the plaintiff's right to be heard and the defendant's right to certainty.
2
Apply the aspects to the hiba/shira' example
Plaintiff first claims gift (hiba), cannot prove it, then claims purchase (shira') with evidence but does not specify if purchase was before or after the gift. The text says the evidence is rejected because it leaves doubt.
If purchase was before the gift, the gift claim would be inconsistent; if after, it would be consistent. But the doubt prevents acceptance.
3
Analyze Ya'qub Pasha's critique
Ya'qub Pasha argues that the two aspects should apply universally, not just to specific cases. He prefers the first aspect (possibility suffices) as the correct universal view.
This critique highlights a methodological difference: should the principle be applied generally or restricted based on context?
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If possibility suffices, claims with ambiguous evidence may be accepted more readily. If certainty is required, the claim is rejected whenever doubt exists.
The practical consequence affects the burden of proof and how courts handle potentially contradictory claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن إمكانَ التَّوفيق هل يكفي في دَفْعِ التَّناقُضِ، أو لا بُدَّ من أَن يُصَرِّحَ بالتوفيق، اختلف فيه المشايخ له وقيد بدعوى الإيفاء بعد الإنكار، إذ لو ادعاه بعد الإقرار بالدين، فإن كان كلا القولين في مجلس وإن لم يقبل التناقض. وإن تفرّقا عن المجلس ثمَّ ادّعاه وأقامَ البيَّنة على الإيفاء بعد الإقرار يقبلُ لعدم التناقض، وإن ادّعى الإيفاء قبل الإقرار لا يقبل. كذا في «البحر» نقلا عن «خزانة المفتيين».
[٣] قوله: فيه؛ أي في أنَّ إمكان التوفيق هل يكفي في دفع التناقض، أو لا بد فيه من التصريح بالتوفيق، وأيضاً اختلفوا في أنه هل يكفي إمكان التوفيق مطلقاً أو من وجه.

وَجْهُ الأَوَّل: أنَّ مع إمكان التوفيق لا يتحقق التناقض، فيحملُ عليه صيانة لدعواه عن البطلان.
وَجْهُ الثَّانِي: أَنَّه لا بُدَّ للدَّعوى من الصَّحَّةِ يقيناً، فإمكانُ الصَّحَّةِ لا يُبْطِلُ حق المدعى عليه.
إذا عَرَفْتَ هذا، فنقول: في كل صورة يَقَعُ الشَّكُ في صحةِ الدَّعوى: لا نقولُ إنّ إمكانَ الصَّحَّةِ كافٍ، كما إذا ادعى الهبة فَسُئِلَ بينة، فلم يقدر، فادَّعى الشراء، فأقام بينة على الشراء من غير أن يُبَيِّنَ أن الشراء قبل وقتِ الهبة، أو بعده لا تقبل؛ لأنَّه يُحتمل أن يكونَ الشَّراءُ قبل وقت الهبة، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يصح دعوى الشراء على ما مرّ، ويُحْتَمَلُ أن يكون الشراء بعد وقت الهبة، وعلى هذا التقدير يصح دعوى الشراء كما مر.
فإذا وَقَعَ الشَّك في صحةِ الدَّعوى لا نصححه بالشَّكَ؛ لأنَّ غايةً ما في الباب أن شراءه إن كان متحققاً قبل وقتِ الهبة، فيكون معنى دعوى الهبة: إني كنتُ اشتريتها منه، لكن ارتفع ذلك العقد، ثُمَّ صارَ ملكاً له، ثُمَّ وَهَبَ منِّي فلا بُدَّ من إقامة البينة على الهبة، فإذا لم يكن له بينةٌ على الهبة، لا يصح دعواه، ولا يَبْطُلُ حق المدعى عليه بالشك.
[١] قوله: فنقول... الخ؛ قال يعقوب باشا في «حاشية» هذا الكتاب: هاهنا كلام، وهو أن كلام صاحب «الكافي» وغيره من العلماء صريح في اطراد الوجهين، وكلام الشارح له على اختصاص الوجهين ببعض الصور، وعدم الاطراد. والحق الاطراد، فإنَّ دعوى القضاء أو الابراء يحتمل أن يصرف إلى دعوى القضاء بغير حق فيصح، أو بحق كما لا يخفى. انتهى. فتأمل فيه؛ لينكشف عليك الحال.
[٢] قوله: لا يصح؛ لظهور التناقض بين الدعوى والبينة، والتناقض تبطل الدعوى فلا تصح.
[٣] قوله: يصح دعوى الشراء؛ لوضوح التوفيق، فإنه يمكنه أن يقول: وهب منذ
Know that the possibility of reconciliation: whether it is sufficient to repel contradiction, or must one explicitly state the reconciliation? The mashayikh differed on this. And it is restricted to a claim of payment after denial; if he claims payment after acknowledgment of debt, then if both statements were in one sitting, contradiction is not accepted. If they separated from the sitting, then he claims and brings evidence for payment after acknowledgment, it is accepted due to absence of contradiction. If he claims payment before acknowledgment, it is not accepted. So it is in al-Bahr quoting Khizanat al-Muftiyin.
[Note 3] His words: 'fihi' (concerning it); meaning concerning whether the possibility of reconciliation is sufficient to repel contradiction, or must one explicitly state reconciliation. Also they differed on whether the possibility of reconciliation is sufficient absolutely or in some respects.
References: al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 7:41; Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:96.
Cream of al-Nihaya for 'Umdat al-Ri'aya:
The first aspect: With the possibility of reconciliation, contradiction does not materialize, so it is interpreted accordingly to protect the claim from invalidity.
The second aspect: The claim must be certainly valid; possibility of validity does not nullify the defendant's right.
When you know this, we say: In every case where doubt occurs about the validity of the claim, we do not say that the possibility of validity is sufficient. For example, if someone claims a gift (hiba) and is asked for evidence but cannot provide it, then claims purchase (shira') and brings evidence for purchase without clarifying whether the purchase was before or after the time of the gift, it is not accepted; because it is possible that the purchase was before the time of the gift, and on this assessment the claim of purchase is invalid as previously mentioned; and it is possible that the purchase was after the time of the gift, and on this assessment the claim of purchase is valid as previously mentioned.
So when doubt occurs about the validity of the claim, we do not validate it by doubt; because the upshot of the matter is that his purchase if it is certain before the time of the gift, then the meaning of the gift claim is: I bought it from him, but that contract was annulled, then it became his property, then he gifted to me. So evidence for the gift is necessary. So if he has no evidence for the gift, his claim is invalid, and the defendant's right is not voided by doubt.
[Note 1] His words: 'fa-naqulu...' etc. Ya'qub Pasha said in his gloss on this book: There is discussion here: the words of the author of al-Kafi and other scholars are explicit in the universal application of the two aspects, while the words of the commentator restrict them to some cases and deny universality. The truth is universality, for the claim of judgment or acquittal can be interpreted as a claim of judgment without right, thus valid, or with right, as is not hidden. End. So ponder it until the situation becomes clear to you.
[Note 2] His words: 'la yasihh' (it is not valid); because the contradiction between the claim and the evidence is apparent, and contradiction invalidates the claim.
[Note 3] His words: 'yasihh da'wa al-shira'' (the claim of purchase is valid); due to clarity of reconciliation, for he can say: He gifted since...
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) regarding the principle of 'imkan al-tawfiq' — whether the mere possibility of reconciling contradictory statements is sufficient to avoid declaring the statements contradictory, or whether explicit reconciliation is required. Two positions are given: the first holds that if reconciliation is possible, it is adopted to protect the claim; the second insists on certainty of validity. The text then applies this to a specific case: a person first claims a gift (hiba) but cannot prove it, then claims purchase (shira') and brings evidence for purchase without specifying the temporal sequence. The evidence is rejected because it leaves doubt: if the purchase preceded the gift, the claim of gift would be inconsistent; if after, it would be consistent. Since there is doubt, the claim cannot be validated by mere possibility. The marginal note from Ya'qub Pasha criticizes restricting the two aspects to some cases, asserting they apply universally. The final marginal note comments on validity of purchase claim when reconciliation is clear, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
إمكان التوفيق (imkan al-tawfiq): The possibility of reconciling two apparently contradictory statements without explicit proof.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction between two statements or between statements and evidence that invalidates a claim.
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift; in this context, a claim of having received property as a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second wajh (view) in the passage, why is evidence for purchase after a failed gift claim not accepted?
A) Because purchase never occurred.
B) Because the temporal order is unclear, causing doubt.
C) Because the defendant was not present.
D) Because the plaintiff had no witness.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qada, the possibility of reconciling contradictory statements is not sufficient; the claim must be certainly valid, or else it is rejected due to doubt (tanaqud).

Contradiction in Evidence: The Seller's Immunity Claim After Denial
Intra-Hanafi dispute on admissibility of contradictory evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of contradiction (tanāquḍ) to evaluate whether evidence is admissible in a case where a party's prior statement contradicts subsequent evidence, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A party's contradictory evidence is rejected unless reconciliation is clear and direct.

In judicial proceedings, a party may sometimes bring evidence that appears to contradict their own earlier claim. The Hanafi school has detailed rules for evaluating such contradictions. This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute where a seller denies a sale but then brings evidence of immunity from defects. The difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf hinges on whether the logical inconsistency can be resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario and the apparent contradiction
The buyer proves the sale; the seller first denies the sale, then asserts immunity from defects. Contradiction: denial of sale vs. claim that implies the sale existed.
Immunity from defects logically requires a valid sale.
2
State the relied-upon Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad rule that the seller's evidence is rejected (lā tusma‘) because the contradiction is irreconcilable.
This is the muftā bihī position.
3
Follow Abu Yusuf's opposing argument
Abu Yusuf analogizes to the debt case: if someone denies a debt and later brings evidence of payment, it is accepted. Similarly, here evidence of immunity should be accepted despite denial.
He uses qiyas on the maxim 'mā kāna laka ‘alayya shay’un qaṭṭ'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing the cases
In the debt case, payment can be made even for an invalid debt (e.g., to avoid litigation). But immunity from defect only makes sense if the sale is valid; denying the sale contradicts the very basis of immunity, so reconciliation is impossible.
The Hanafi response relies on istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Yusuf, the seller could be exonerated; under the majority, the buyer retains the right to return the defective item. The muftā bihī is the majority view.
This affects the buyer's right to rescind the contract.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the admissibility of evidence when there is an apparent contradiction between a party's earlier statement and later evidence. The text first establishes a general principle: if reconciliation between a claim and evidence is possible, then no contradiction exists; if not, the claim fails. The author then applies this to a specific case: a person first claims a gift, then later claims purchase of the same item. If the timing is unclear, the later claim is not accepted because the possibility of reconciliation (purchase before gift) would invalidate the purchase claim. Only when reconciliation is clear (purchase after gift) is the claim accepted. The key dispute arises in the scenario where a buyer brings evidence of purchase, then wants to return the item due to a defect, and the seller—after denying the sale—brings evidence that the buyer accepted the item free of defects. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad rule that the seller's evidence is inadmissible due to contradiction: the seller denied the sale, then asserted immunity, which implies the sale existed. Abu Yusuf, however, accepts the evidence by analogy to the case where someone says 'you never had any right against me' and then brings evidence of payment—where payment is possible even if the original debt was invalid. The Hanafi response distinguishes: in the debt case, payment can be for an invalid debt, but here immunity from defect only makes sense if the sale is acknowledged, which contradicts the denial. The passage also includes a gloss by Yaqub Pasha affirming that the two aspects (possibility of reconciliation vs. certainty) apply universally, and a note explaining why evidence of payment or purchase after gift is accepted. The lesson is a detailed treatment of how to handle apparent contradictions in legal proceedings.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction between a party's earlier statement and later evidence, making the later evidence invalid if reconciliation (tawfīq) is impossible.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, used to prove claims in court.
رد بعيب (radd bi ‘ayb): Right of the buyer to return a purchased item due to a pre-existing defect that was unknown at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a buyer proves a sale and the seller then brings evidence of immunity from defects after denying the sale, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The seller's evidence is accepted because contradiction can be reconciled.
B) The seller's evidence is not accepted due to apparent contradiction.
C) The seller's evidence is accepted only if the buyer agrees.
D) The buyer's evidence is rejected instead.

Key Takeaway
Evidence that contradicts a prior statement is rejected unless reconciliation is clear and direct; denial of a transaction prevents later claims that presuppose its existence.

Contradictory Claims and Acceptance of Evidence
Ruling on contradictory statements in claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tanaqud to determine when a party's evidence is rejected due to contradiction, and distinguish when tawfiq (reconciliation) is possible.

Core Idea
A party who denies a sale cannot later claim immunity from defects—the contradiction invalidates the evidence unless reconciliation is possible.

In judicial proceedings, a party's own words can become their greatest obstacle. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya examines a realistic scenario: a buyer returns a defective slave, the seller denies the sale, then later presents evidence of immunity from defects. The Hanafi school's leading imams debate whether this evidence should be heard. The key lies in the concept of tanaqud (contradiction) and its saving grace, tawfiq (reconciliation). We will unpack the reasoning, the dispute, and the operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
A buyer claims he bought a slave, paid, found a defect, and wants to return it. The seller denies the sale entirely. The buyer proves the sale. Then the seller claims immunity from defects. Note the sequence: denial of sale → claim of immunity.
Immunity (barā’a) presupposes the existence of the sale, which was denied.
2
State the Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad)
They rule that the seller's evidence of immunity is not accepted (lā tusma‘). Reason: the seller's initial denial of the sale contradicts the later claim of immunity, which requires the sale. No reasonable reconciliation (tawfiq) is possible because immunity without a contract is inconceivable.
This is the ẓāhir al-riwāyah (preferred transmission).
3
Understand Abū Yūsuf’s objection
Abū Yūsuf accepts the evidence by analogy to a debt case: a person who denies a debt may later prove payment; payment can be for a false claim to avoid litigation. Similarly, the seller might not have sold personally—his agent could have sold and granted immunity. Thus, there is possible reconciliation.
Abū Yūsuf prioritizes the possibility of tawfiq over apparent contradiction.
4
Apply the principle of tawfiq (reconciliation)
The passage clarifies that contradiction only bars evidence if reconciliation is impossible. In the immunity case, the ẓāhir al-riwāyah view holds no tawfiq. However, in other examples (e.g., claiming payment after denying debt), tawfiq is possible, so evidence is accepted.
The muftā bihī (operative ruling) is that of Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad.

And whoever presents evidence of purchase and wants to return due to a defect, the evidence of the seller regarding his immunity from every defect is rejected after his denial of the sale. And in every situation doubt does not occur in the validity of his claim, such that it would necessitate invalidating the right of the defendant by doubt. So we say: The possibility of reconciliation is sufficient, as when he presents evidence of payment or discharge after his denial of the claim and the plaintiff's presentation of evidence against him, or the plaintiff presents evidence of purchase after the time of gift, it is accepted. So memorize this principle, for it is very beneficial. [1] Then know that this contradiction only prevents the validity of the claim if the first speech established a right for a specific person; if not, it does not prevent the validity of the claim, as when he says 'I have no right upon anyone from Samarqand' then claims something upon one person from Samarqand, his claim is valid. (And whoever presents evidence of purchase and wants to return due to a defect, the evidence of the seller regarding his immunity from every defect is rejected after his denial of the sale): A man claimed against another: 'I bought this slave from you for one thousand and paid you the thousand, then a defect appeared so I want to return it due to the defect, so you must return the price to me.' The opponent denied the sale, so the plaintiff presented evidence of the sale. The opponent then claimed immunity of the defendant from every defect and presented evidence thereof. It is not heard due to contradiction. According to Abu Yusuf: It is heard by analogy to the mentioned case, which is 'You never owed me anything.' The distinction for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with them) is that in the debt case, the debt may be paid even if it is invalid, while here the claim of immunity from defect necessitates the existence of the sale, and he has denied it. ... Then it becomes clear that contradiction only prevents the validity of the claim if reconciliation is not possible; if possible, it does not prevent. [1] His statement: 'accepted' – As for the first scenario, when he presents evidence of payment or discharge after his denial of the claim and the plaintiff's presentation of evidence against him, it is due to the possibility of reconciliation, because a false right may be paid and discharged to avoid litigation, and he may settle by denying something, then that thing is established and then paid. As for the second scenario, when he presents evidence of purchase after the time of gift, it is because the purchase after the gift confirms his ownership at that time. [2] His statement: 'not heard' – The summary is that the claim in this scenario is not heard, and the evidence is not accepted according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah be pleased with them) due to the existence of contradiction. Indeed, stipulating immunity changes the contract from requiring the attribute of soundness to something else, thus requiring the existence of the contract, since the attribute without the described cannot be conceived, and he has denied it, so it is contradictory. Unlike the mentioned debt case, because the debt may be paid to avoid litigation even if it is invalid, so there is no contradiction due to the possibility of reconciliation. This is the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) that it is accepted because reconciliation is possible: he may not have sold it himself, but rather his agent sold it to him and discharged him from the defect. And Allah knows best, and He is the Guardian of the unseen.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage opens a discussion on contradictory statements in legal claims. The author first states a general ruling: if a buyer presents evidence of purchase and wants to return due to a defect, the seller's evidence claiming immunity from all defects is rejected after the seller has denied the sale. This is because the seller's initial denial of the sale contradicts his later claim of immunity, which presupposes the sale exists. The author emphasizes that the possibility of reconciliation (إمكان التوفيق) between statements is sufficient to avoid contradiction, giving examples like presenting evidence of payment after denying the claim, or evidence of purchase after a gift. He then specifies that contradiction only invalidates a claim if the first statement established a right for a specific person. The core example involves a buyer suing for return of a defective slave, the seller denies the sale, then the buyer proves the sale, and the seller then claims immunity from defects. Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad rule that the seller's evidence is not accepted due to contradiction. Abu Yusuf disagrees, drawing analogy to the case where someone says 'you never owed me anything' then claims a debt—a debt can be paid even if falsely denied to avoid litigation. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish: in the debt case, payment can be for a false claim, so no contradiction; but here, immunity presupposes a valid sale, which was denied. The commentary adds that the seller's stipulation of immunity changes the contract's nature, requiring the existence of the contract itself. The preferred view (ظاهر الراوية) is the rejection of the evidence, though Abu Yusuf's acceptance is based on the possibility that the seller's agent sold it. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute with two positions.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction between two statements in court that prevents acceptance of evidence unless reconciliation is possible.
التوفيق (al-tawfiq): Reconciliation or harmonization between two seemingly contradictory statements, making them non-contradictory.
براءة (barā’a): Immunity from defects; a seller's claim that the item was free of defects at the time of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, why is the seller's evidence of immunity rejected when he previously denied the sale?
A) Because the buyer already paid the price.
B) Because the seller's denial of the sale and his claim of immunity are irreconcilably contradictory—immunity requires the sale to exist.
C) Because the buyer did not prove the defect adequately.
D) Because the seller's evidence was submitted after the buyer's evidence.

Key Takeaway
When a party makes contradictory statements in court, evidence is rejected unless a reasonable reconciliation (tawfiq) can be found; the Hanafi preferred view rejects the seller's immunity evidence after denying the sale.

Effect of 'In Sha Allah' at the End of a Legal Document
Ruling on writing 'إن شاء الله' at the end of a document and its effect on validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the effect of writing 'In Sha Allah' at the end of a legal document and explain why the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is preponderant.

Core Idea
A customary phrase of blessing does not void an entire contract—only the immediate clause is affected according to the preponderant Hanafi view.

When a legal document ends with the phrase 'إن شاء الله' (if Allah wills), does it void the entire document or just the last part? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa, by strict analogy, holds the entire document void, while his two disciples Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule by juristic preference (istihsan) that only the immediate clause is affected. Later authorities uphold the disciples' view due to custom, and this lesson walks you through the reasoning and the practical consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١]قوله : في آخر صك ; أطلقه فشمل ما إذا اشتمل على شيء واحدٍ أو أشياء ، وفي الثاني : الاختلاف، قال الإمام : إذا كُتِبَ بيع وإقرار وإجارة أو غير ذلك، ثم كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى بطل الكل قياساً ; لأن الكل كشيء واحدٍ بحكم العطف. ويبطل الآخر عندهما فقط استحساناً ; لانصراف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه ; لأنَّ الصك للاستيثاق، وكذا الأصلُ في الكلام الاستيثاق، وأشار إلى أنَّ الكتابة كالنطق فلا بُدَّ فيهما من اتصال المشيئة افلوا ترك ،فُرجة، فالاستثناء ينصرف إلى ما يليه اتفاقاً ، كالسكوت.
وحاصله كما في البحر»: إنّهم اتفقوا على أن المشيئة إذا ذكرت بعد جمل متعاطفة بالواو كقوله : عبده حرّ وامرأته طالق، وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله الحرام إن شاء الله تعالى، ينصرف إلى الكل، فيبطل الكل.

And he mentioned: 'If Allah wills' at the end of a document invalidates the whole document according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) only the last part is invalid, and it is istihsan. Meaning: if one writes a document of acknowledgment, then writes at its end: 'Whoever produces this document and claims what is in it of rights, pay him if Allah wills'... [1] His statement: 'at the end of a document' – it is general and includes whether it contains one or multiple matters. In the second case, there is difference: Imam said: if a sale, acknowledgment, lease or other things are written, then 'if Allah wills' is written at the end, the whole is void by qiyas because all is like one thing due to conjunction. And only the last part is void according to the two by istihsan because the exception refers only to what immediately precedes it; because the document is for securing rights, and the principle in speech is also for securing. He indicated that writing is like speech, so connection of the will is necessary in both. If one leaves a gap, then the exception refers only to what immediately follows. The summary as in al-Bahr: They agreed that if the will is mentioned after coordinated sentences with 'waw', like 'his slave is free, his wife is divorced, and upon him is a pilgrimage to the Sacred House if Allah wills', it refers to all, so all is void. So Abu Hanifa followed the general ruling. The two excluded the case of writing a document from the general rule due to a (customary) factor that necessitated specifying the document from the general ruling of a condition following coordinated sentences, due to custom, and incidents are interpreted accordingly, not that it may be written for invalidation; for a purpose that may coincide. Hence their view is preponderant over his. This is from Fath al-Qadir. [2] His statement: 'Whoever produces...' etc.; for it is said: why is this written, and the agency is not valid?
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific issue: if a legal document (صك) contains a phrase 'إن شاء الله تعالى' (if Allah wills) at its end, what is the effect? The ruling is presented with the positions of Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to Abu Hanifa, writing 'In Sha Allah' at the end of the document invalidates the entire document (يبطل كله). According to the two, only the last part (the clause immediately preceding the phrase) is invalidated (يبطل آخره), and this is based on istihsan. The reasoning: by strict qiyas, every clause in the document is linked, so a condition at the end affects the whole. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that custom (عادة) dictates that such phrasing is often used for seeking blessing, not to invalidate the entire document. Moreover, in documents, the principle is to secure rights, so the exception is limited to the adjacent clause. The commentary explains that in ordinary speech with coordinated sentences (e.g., 'his slave is free, his wife is divorced, and upon him is a pilgrimage if Allah wills'), all are void because the condition refers to all. But for documents, the customary practice overrides the general qiyas, and the two's view is preferred. The text ends by stating that their view is rājiḥ (preponderant) over Abu Hanifa's. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute where the later preference (from Fath al-Qadir) favors the two's position due to custom.

Key Terms
صك (sakk): A legal document, deed, or instrument that records rights or obligations.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on custom, necessity, or equity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preponderant view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what happens when 'In Sha Allah' is written at the end of a legal document?
A) The entire document becomes void.
B) Only the last clause of the document becomes void.
C) The document is valid as long as the writer intended a blessing.
D) The document is void only if it contains multiple matters.

Key Takeaway
Writing 'In Sha Allah' at the end of a legal document does not invalidate the entire document; by istihsan, it only affects the immediate clause.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Effect of 'Insha Allah' on a Multi-Clause Document
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'Insha Allah' in documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on adding 'Insha Allah' to a document and apply the mufta bihi ruling when analyzing document validity.

Core Idea
Custom and purpose override strict analogy: 'Insha Allah' only voids the last clause in a document.

When a legal document ends with 'إن شاء الله تعالى', does it nullify the entire deed or only the final provision? The Hanafi school answers this with an important intra-madhhab dispute. Understanding how Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on this point reveals the interplay between qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
Recognize that the dispute concerns a document (sakk) containing multiple clauses (e.g., sale, acknowledgment, lease) with 'Insha Allah' written at the end, referring to the whole document.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Proof
Abu Hanifa holds the entire document invalid (batal al-kull) by qiyas: just as a condition after conjoined sentences in speech applies to all, so does 'Insha Allah' here.
Qiyas on verbal statements like 'My slave is free and my wife is divorced, if Allah wills.'
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say only the last part is invalid (batal al-akhar) by istihsan: documents are for confirmation, and custom restricts the exception to the immediately preceding clause.
Istihsan based on urf (custom) and the purpose of the document (al-sakk lil-istithaq).
4
Analyze the Tarjih (Preponderance)
The text states the Sahibayn's view is preponderant (rajih) because it aligns with customary practice and the document's confirmatory function, making it the mufta bihi.
Referenced from Fath al-Qadir: 'their saying is preponderant over his.'
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
In practice, if a deed contains multiple transactions and ends with 'Insha Allah', only the last transaction is void, while the earlier ones remain valid. This protects the majority of the document from nullification.

كتاب القضاء باب التحكيم والقضاء بالمواريث
فقوله :
٥٠٣
: إن شاء الله تعالى يَنْصَرِفُ إلى الكلِّ عند أبي حنيفة له حتَّى يَبْطُل جميعُ الصك، وهو القياس، كما في قوله : عبدهُ حُرِّ وامرأته طالق إن شاء الله تعالى، وعندهما يَنْصَرِفُ إلى الآخر، وهو الاستحسان ؛ لأنَّ الصَّك للاستيثاق ، فالاستثناءُ يَنْصَرِفُ إلى ما يليه.
على هذا الوجه ؛ لأنه توكيل المجهول، والمجهول لا يصلح وكيلاً.
قلنا : الغرض من كتابة هذا استماع خصومة الوكيل، فإنَّ التوكيل بغير رضاء الخصم لا يلزم عند أبي حنيفة ، وعلى تقدير الرضاء هاهنا يلزم التوكيل بلا رضاه. كذا في «الكفاية»). ١ ] قوله : كما في قوله ؛ والحاصل أن الشرط إذا تعقب جملاً متعاطفة متصلاً بهما، فإنّه للكلِّ، وأما الاستثناء بإلا فإلى الأخير، فلو أقر لاثنين بمالين واستثنى شيئاً كان من الأخير، ولو أقر بمالين كمئة درهم وخمسين ديناراً إلا درهما انصرف إلى الأوّل
استحساناً. أمَّا الاستثناء بإن شاء الله تعالى بعد جملتين إيقاعتين فإليهما، وبعد طلاقين معلقين، أو طلاق معلق وعتق معلّق فإليهما عند محمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له إلى الأخير، واتَّفقوا على انصرافِهِ إلى الأخير في غير العطف، وفي المعطوف بعد السكوت. كذا في «البحر» نقلا «إيضاح الكرماني». (٢) قوله : لأن الصك..الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ الصك يكتب للاستيثاق، والتأكيد
(۲)
للإبطال ، فكان ذلك دلالة على قصر الاستثناء على الذي يليه.
(۱) (الكفاية» (٦) : ٤٢٤ ) . (۲) «البحر الرائق»(٧: ٤٣).
And he mentioned: If "Insha Allah" is written at the end of a document, it invalidates all of it according to him (Abu Hanifa), while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it invalidates only its last part, and this is istihsan. That is, if someone wrote a deed of acknowledgment, then wrote at its end: "Everyone who produces this deed and demands the right therein, pay him, if Allah wills." (Explanation: This necessitates attributing the description of soundness to another, thus necessitating the existence of the contract, for the description without the described is inconceivable, and he has denied it, so it is contradictory. Unlike the issue of debt mentioned previously, for debt may be paid to avoid litigation, and if it is invalid, there is no contradiction due to the possibility of reconciliation. This is the apparent narration, and from Abu Yusuf: it is accepted because reconciliation is possible if he did not sell it himself, but his agent sold it to him and he acquitted him of the defect. And Allah knows best, and He is the Guardian of the unseen.) [1] His statement: "at the end of a document" — it is unrestricted so it includes whether it contains one thing or multiple things. In the second case there is difference. The Imam said: If a sale, acknowledgment, lease, or other things are written, then "Insha Allah" is written at its end, it invalidates all by qiyas, because all is like one thing by virtue of conjunction. But it only invalidates the last part according to them by istihsan, because the exception applies to what follows it, since the document is for confirmation, and similarly the principle in speech is confirmation. And he indicated that writing is like speech, so in both there must be connection of the will. If they left a gap, then the exception applies to what follows it by agreement, like silence. The gist, as in "al-Bahr": They agreed that if the will (mashi'a) is mentioned after sentences conjoined by waw, like his saying: "His slave is free and his wife is divorced, and he owes pilgrimage to the Sacred House of Allah, if Allah wills," it applies to all, so it invalidates all. So Abu Hanifa followed its ruling, while the two (disciples) excluded the case of writing a document from its generality due to a cause that necessitated specifying the document from the general ruling of the condition following conjoined sentences, due to custom, and events are interpreted accordingly, not that it may be written for invalidation for a purpose that might occur; hence their saying is preponderant over his. Thus in "Fath al-Qadir". [2] His statement: "Everyone who produces..." — if it is asked: Why is this written, and agency is not valid in this manner? Because it is agency for an unknown person, and an unknown person is not valid as an agent. We say: The purpose of writing this is to hear the litigation of the agent, for agency without the consent of the opponent is not binding according to Abu Hanifa, and assuming consent here, agency is binding without his consent. Thus in "al-Kifaya". [1] His statement: "as in his saying" — the gist is that if a condition follows conjoined sentences connectedly, it applies to all. As for exception with "illa", it applies to the last. If one acknowledged for two persons two properties and excepted something, it is from the last. If one acknowledged two properties, like one hundred dirhams and fifty dinars except one dirham, it applies to the first by istihsan. As for exception with "Insha Allah" after two declarative sentences, it applies to both. After two conditional divorces, or a conditional divorce and a conditional emancipation, it applies to both according to Muhammad, while according to Abu Yusuf it applies to the last. And they agreed that it applies to the last when there is no conjunction, and when there is conjunction after silence. Thus in "al-Bahr" quoting "Idah al-Kirmani". [2] His statement: "because the document..." — meaning because the document is written for confirmation, and emphasis is for invalidation, so that indicates restricting the exception to what follows it.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of writing "إن شاء الله تعالى" (if Allah wills) at the end of a legal document (صك). It falls under the chapter of judiciary (كتاب القضاء), specifically discussing the validity of documents. The issue: If a document contains multiple clauses (e.g., sale, acknowledgment, lease) and at the end it says "إن شاء الله تعالى" referring to the entire document, does it invalidate the whole document or only the last part? Abu Hanifa holds that it invalidates the entire document (بطل الكل), based on qiyas (analogy) to speech: if one says "My slave is free and my wife is divorced, if Allah wills," the condition applies to both, invalidating both. Similarly, here the condition applies to all conjoined clauses. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that it only invalidates the last part (يبطل الآخر), based on istihsan (juristic preference). Their reasoning: A document is written for confirmation (للاستيثاق), not for invalidation. The custom is that such a phrase is only meant to apply to the last clause. They treat the document as a single unit but consider the purpose and custom. The text notes that the Sahibayn's view is preponderant (راجح) and it is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The passage also clarifies that if there is a gap (فُرجة) or silence between clauses, the exception applies only to the last one by agreement. Additionally, distinctions are made between exception with "إلا" (which goes to the last) and "إن شاء الله" (which may go to both or last depending on context). The discussion includes nuanced differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding conditional divorces and emancipations, but the core ruling for documents is that the Sahibayn's istihsan prevails.

Key Terms
صك (sakk): A legal document or deed, often used for recording transactions, acknowledgments, or judgments.
إن شاء الله تعالى (in shā’ Allāh ta‘ālā): A phrase meaning 'if Allah wills,' used as a condition or exception in legal documents and speech.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning that departs from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable or customary rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, if a document contains multiple transactions and ends with 'إن شاء الله تعالى', what is the effect?
A) The entire document is invalid.
B) Only the last transaction is invalid.
C) The document remains fully valid.
D) The document is invalid only if the phrase is written by the creditor.

Key Takeaway
When 'Insha Allah' appears at the end of a multi-clause document, the mufta bihi Hanafi view invalidates only the last clause, preserving the rest.

Exception with 'InshaAllah' in Legal Documents: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of exception 'InshaAllah' in documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the scope of 'InshaAllah' in legal documents and explain the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: 'InshaAllah' makes all prior clauses conditional; the Sahibayn: it only attaches to the nearest — a dispute driven by purpose vs. analogy.

The phrase 'InshaAllah' is a common expression of humility, but in legal documents its placement can drastically affect validity. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya' presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: does such an exception apply to all preceding statements or only the immediately preceding one? The answer determines whether an entire document is invalidated or only a single clause, revealing a deeper tension between qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference).

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: the scope of an exception phrase when it follows multiple coordinate clauses.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and evidence
Abu Hanifa holds that the condition applies to all clauses, using qiyas to a case where manumission and divorce are both conditioned. The entire document (sakk) is invalidated if the condition fails.
This is described as 'al-qiyas' in the text.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the condition applies only to the last clause, based on istihsan: since the document's purpose is to secure rights, an exception that could nullify multiple rights is contrary to that purpose.
The text says 'This is istihsan because the document is for securing (rights).'
4
Examine sub-differences within the Sahibayn
Muhammad differs from Abu Yusuf when the clauses are of the 'iqa'ah' type (executory sentences like divorce or manumission): Muhammad applies the condition to both, while Abu Yusuf still restricts it to the last.
The text explicitly says 'according to Muhammad... applies to both; according to Abu Yusuf, only to the last.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: If a person acknowledges two debts and says 'InshaAllah', under Abu Hanifa both are conditional and the entire acknowledgment may be void; under the Sahibayn only the second is conditional, so the first debt stands.
This difference directly affects document drafting and court rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم والقضاء بالمواريث
فقوله : إن شاء الله تعالى يَنْصَرِفُ إلى الكلِّ عند أبي حنيفة له حتَّى يَبْطُل جميعُ الصك، وهو القياس، كما في قوله : عبدهُ حُرِّ وامرأته طالق إن شاء الله تعالى، وعندهما يَنْصَرِفُ إلى الآخر، وهو الاستحسان ؛ لأنَّ الصَّك للاستيثاق ، فالاستثناءُ يَنْصَرِفُ إلى ما يليه.
على هذا الوجه ؛ لأنه توكيل المجهول، والمجهول لا يصلح وكيلاً.
قلنا : الغرض من كتابة هذا استماع خصومة الوكيل، فإنَّ التوكيل بغير رضاء الخصم لا يلزم عند أبي حنيفة ، وعلى تقدير الرضاء هاهنا يلزم التوكيل بلا رضاه. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله : كما في قوله ؛ والحاصل أن الشرط إذا تعقب جملاً متعاطفة متصلاً بهما، فإنّه للكلِّ، وأما الاستثناء بإلا فإلى الأخير، فلو أقر لاثنين بمالين واستثنى شيئاً كان من الأخير، ولو أقر بمالين كمئة درهم وخمسين ديناراً إلا درهما انصرف إلى الأوّل استحساناً. أمَّا الاستثناء بإن شاء الله تعالى بعد جملتين إيقاعتين فإليهما، وبعد طلاقين معلقين، أو طلاق معلق وعتق معلّق فإليهما عند محمد ، وعند أبي يوسف له إلى الأخير، واتَّفقوا على انصرافِهِ إلى الأخير في غير العطف، وفي المعطوف بعد السكوت. كذا في «البحر» نقلا «إيضاح الكرماني». قوله : لأن الصك..الخ ؛ يعني لأنَّ الصك يكتب للاستيثاق، والتأكيد للإبطال ، فكان ذلك دلالة على قصر الاستثناء على الذي يليه.
Chapter of arbitration and judgment regarding inheritances. His statement: 'If Allah wills' applies to all according to Abu Hanifa, to the extent that it invalidates the entire document, and this is analogy, as in his statement: 'His slave is free and his wife is divorced, if Allah wills.' According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it applies to the last (clause), and this is juristic preference, because the document is for securing (rights), so the exception applies to what is next to it. ... Thus in 'al-Bahr' quoting 'Idah al-Kirmani'. His statement: 'because the document...' means that the document is written for securing rights, and the emphasis is for invalidation, so that indicates restricting the exception to what is next to it.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over the legal effect of the phrase 'InshaAllah' (if Allah wills) when appended to multiple clauses in a document or statement. The base text (matn) likely stated a ruling; the commentator (sharh) explains the positions. Abu Hanifa holds that the exception applies to all preceding clauses jointly (the qiyas position), so if the document contains multiple acknowledgments, the condition invalidates the entire document. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) adopt istihsan, holding that it applies only to the immediately preceding clause, because the purpose of a document (sakk) is to secure rights; an exception that could destroy numerous rights would defeat that purpose. The commentary extends the discussion to the exception 'illa' (except), where for two debts the exception attaches to the last mentioned, and to cases of suspended divorces and manumissions. The key distinction is between conditions (shart) and exceptions (istithna). For conditions, the text states that if a condition follows multiple coordinate clauses together, it applies to all. For exceptions with 'illa', it applies to only the last. For 'InshaAllah' after two executory sentences (iqa'atayn), it applies to both according to Muhammad but only to the last according to Abu Yusuf. In the absence of conjunction (ghayr al-'ataf) or after a pause (sukut), all agree it applies to the last.

Key Terms
صك (sakk): A legal document, bond, or deed used to record rights and obligations.
استثناء (istithnā'): Making an exception to a preceding statement; here specifically the phrase 'InshaAllah' or 'illa'.
استيثاق (istīthāq): The act of securing or confirming a right; the purpose of a sakk.
إيقاع (īqā'): An executory unilateral disposition, such as a divorce or manumission, which takes effect immediately unless conditioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the divorce is conditional
B) Both the manumission and divorce are conditional
C) The statement is invalid
D) Only the manumission is conditional

Key Takeaway
The scope of 'InshaAllah' in legal documents divides the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa extends it to all coordinate clauses by qiyas, while the Sahibayn restrict it to the nearest by istihsan, prioritizing the document's purpose of securing rights.

Istishab al-Hal as a Defensive Proof in Inheritance Disputes
Ruling on istishab in inheritance disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istishab al-hal as a defensive proof to determine whose claim is believed in inheritance disputes over the timing of conversion.

Core Idea
Istishab al-hal preserves the status quo but cannot create a new right.

In inheritance disputes, the timing of a critical event—such as conversion to Islam—can determine who inherits. The Hanafi school relies on the principle of istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) to decide cases where no clear evidence exists. This lesson unpacks two classic scenarios from the commentary on al-Hidayah and explains why the heirs are believed over the wife's claim, using istishab as a defensive (not positive) proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Disputed Cases
Read the two scenarios: (1) Christian husband, wife Muslim, she claims conversion after death; (2) Muslim husband, wife Christian, she claims conversion before death. In both, the ruling is the same: the heirs are believed.
The gender and religion of the wife and husband vary, but the ruling is consistent.
2
Understand the Principle: Istishab al-Hal
The commentator defines istishab as establishing a matter at one time based on its existence at another. It is a defensive proof (hujjah dafi'ah), not a positive proof (hujjah muthbitah). Two types: from past to present (e.g., life of a missing person) and from present to past (e.g., flow of a mill's water).
Hanafis restrict istishab to defense; other schools may use it more broadly.
3
Apply Istishab as a Defensive Proof
In both cases, the heirs rely on istishab: the wife's non-Muslim status at death (or the absence of conversion before death) is presumed to continue. The wife's current apparent state (zahir) is insufficient to prove conversion at the earlier time because that would use zahir for entitlement (istihqaq). The heirs are defending the status quo.
The wife needs to prove entitlement; the heirs only need to defend against the claim.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf with Zufar
Imam Zufar differs in the first case: he gives the word to the wife because the conversion is a new event and should be attributed to the nearest time (after death). However, the majority position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that the heirs are believed, and this is the mufta bihi.
The difference arises from how to treat a new event in the context of istishab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نصراني مات ، فقالت عرسه : أسلمتُ بعد موته ، وقال ورثته : لا بل قبله ، صدقوا ، كما في مسلم مات ، فقالت عرسه : أسلمتُ قبل موته ، وقالوا : بل بعد موته
قوله : نصراني ؛ ذكر مسألتين مما يتعلّق إثباته باستصحاب الحال، وهو الحكمُ بثبوت أمر في وقتِ بناءً على ثبوته في وقت آخر، وهو على نوعين : أحدهما : أن يقال : هو كان ثابتاً في الماضي، فيكون ثابتاً في الحال، كحياة المفقود. والثاني : أن يقال : هو ثابت في الحال، فيحكم بثبوتِهِ في الماضي ؛ كجريان ماء الطاحونة ، وهو حجّةٌ دافعة لا مثبتة عندنا كما عرف في أصول الفقه. والتقرير في المسألة الأولى : أنَّ نصرانياً إذا مات فجاءت امرأته، وهي مسلمة، وقالت : أسلمتُ بعد موته، وقال الورثة : لا بل أسلمت قبل موته، فيكون القولُ للورثة، وصدقوا. والتحرير في المسألة الثانية : أنَّ مسلماً إذا مات فجاءت امرأته، وهي نصرانية، وقالت : أسلمت قبل موته، وقالت الورثة : لا بل أسلمت بعد موته ، فالقول للورثة ، وصدقوا. ولا يقال : إنّها مسلمة في الحال، فتكون مسلمةً قبل موته ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر لا يصلح حجة للاستحقاق، والمرأة محتاجة إلى الاستحقاق، ويشهد لهم ظاهر الحدوث أيضا. فالحاصل أنَّ المرأة تتمسك في هذه المسألة بما يتمسك به الورثة في المسألة الأولى، والورثة في هذه المسألة يتمسكون بما سكتت به المرأة في المسألة الأولى، وغير أنها في المسألتين تتمسك بالظاهر ؛ لإثبات الاستحقاق، والظاهر يكفي للدفع لا الاستحقاق، والورثة هم الدافعون جميعاً في المسألتين. كذا في شروح «الهداية»(۱). (۱) «الكفاية» و«العناية) و(فتح القدير» (٦ : ٤٢٥ - ٤٢٧).
A Christian man died, his wife said: 'I embraced Islam after his death,' and his heirs said: 'No, rather before it.' They are believed, just as a Muslim man died, his wife said: 'I embraced Islam before his death,' and they said: 'Rather after his death.' His statement: 'A Christian' – he mentions two issues related to proof by istishab al-hal, which is the judgement of establishing a matter at a time based on its establishment at another time. It is of two types: one: it is said it was established in the past, so it is established in the present, like the life of a missing person. The second: it is said it is established in the present, so one judges its establishment in the past, like the flow of a mill's water. It is a defensive proof, not a positive one according to us, as known in usul al-fiqh. The explanation of the first issue: a Christian man dies, his wife who is Muslim comes and says: 'I embraced Islam after his death,' and the heirs say: 'No, rather you embraced Islam before his death.' So the word is given to the heirs, and they are believed. The exposition of the second issue: a Muslim man dies, his wife who is Christian comes and says: 'I embraced Islam before his death,' and the heirs say: 'No, rather after his death.' So the word is given to the heirs, and they are believed. And it is not said: she is Muslim in the present, so she must have been Muslim before his death, because what is apparent (zahir) is not valid as proof for entitlement (istihqaq), and the woman needs entitlement. The apparent of origination also testifies for them (the heirs). So the result: the woman in this issue adheres to what the heirs adhere to in the first issue, and the heirs in this issue adhere to what the woman remained silent about in the first issue. And she in both issues adheres to the apparent to prove entitlement, but the apparent suffices for defense not entitlement, and the heirs are the defenders in both issues. This is found in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. (1) al-Kifayah, al-Inayah, and Fath al-Qadir (6:425-427).
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discusses two inheritance disputes where the timing of a wife's conversion to Islam is contested. The ruling is that the word of the heirs is taken, not the wife's. The underlying legal principle is istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity). Istishab is used here as a defensive proof: it can prevent a claim but not establish an entitlement. In both cases, the heirs are defending against the wife's claim to inheritance based on her alleged conversion at a particular time. The apparent (zahir) that the wife is currently Muslim is not enough to prove she was Muslim at the time of death, because the need is for entitlement (istihqaq), which requires stronger proof. The commentary explains that istishab works by presuming that a state continues until the contrary is proven. Here, the wife's conversion is a new event (hadith), so the principle of adding events to the nearest possible time (as used by Zufar, mentioned later) would differ. But the Hanafi position (as given in al-Hidayah) favors the heirs because the cause of deprivation (i.e., the wife being non-Muslim at death) is established in the present, and this is used to prove it in the past via istishab. However, this is for defense only. The woman's claim requires positive proof, which she lacks.

Key Terms
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): A legal presumption that a state of affairs continues to exist until the contrary is proven; used here as a defensive proof (hujjah dafi'ah).
الظاهر (al-zahir): Apparent evidence; in this context, it refers to the observable fact that the wife is currently Muslim, but such evidence is insufficient to prove her conversion before death for inheritance purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, why are the heirs believed over the wife's claim about the timing of her conversion?
A) Because the wife's testimony is rejected due to her gender.
B) Because the principle of istishab al-hal provides a defensive proof for the heirs.
C) Because the wife admitted she converted after death.
D) Because the heirs have more witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, istishab al-hal is a defensive proof: it can preserve an existing right but cannot create a new one, so in disputes over conversion timing, the heirs are believed over the wife's claim.
